Topic 1: Introduction to Machine Learning

Covers: AI & ML Overview, Supervised/Unsupervised/Reinforcement Learning, Bias-Variance Tradeoff, Overfitting & Underfitting, Parametric vs Non-Parametric, Flexible vs Inflexible Models, K-Nearest Neighbors (KNN), Decision Trees, Feature Scaling, Cost-Sensitive Learning, Precision/Recall/F1

1. Basic Understanding

1.1 What is Machine Learning?

In plain English: Machine Learning is teaching computers to learn from data instead of being explicitly programmed. Rather than writing rules like "if temperature > 30°C, recommend AC", you show the computer thousands of examples and it figures out the patterns itself.

Formally (Tom Mitchell): A program learns from experience $E$ with respect to task $T$ and performance measure $P$, if its performance on $T$ as measured by $P$ improves with experience $E$.

Real-world examples:

  • Netflix recommending movies (learns from your watch history)
  • Email spam filters (learns from labeled spam/not-spam emails)
  • Self-driving cars (learns from millions of driving examples)

1.2 Types of Learning

Think of it this way:

  • Supervised: You're a student with an answer key — you learn by comparing your answers to correct ones
  • Unsupervised: You're sorting a pile of photos into groups without anyone telling you the categories
  • Reinforcement: You're learning to play a game — no one tells you the "right" move, but you get scores/penalties
TypeInputGoalExamples
Supervised$(x_i, y_i)$ pairsLearn $f: X \to Y$Regression, Classification
Unsupervised$x_i$ onlyFind structure/patternsClustering, PCA, Anomaly Detection
ReinforcementStates, Actions, RewardsLearn optimal policy $\pi^*$Game AI, Robotics, Control
Semi-supervisedFew labeled + many unlabeledLeverage unlabeled dataText classification with limited labels
Exam Tip: "Determining the age of a pet dog using breed lifespan database" is NOT reinforcement learning — it's regression/statistical estimation. RL requires sequential decision-making with states, actions, and reward signals.

1.3 Regression vs Classification

Regression: Predicting a NUMBER. "How much will this house cost?" → $y \in \mathbb{R}$ (continuous). Loss: MSE, MAE, Huber.

Classification: Predicting a CATEGORY. "Is this email spam or not?" → $y \in \{0,1,\ldots,K-1\}$ (discrete). Loss: 0-1 loss, cross-entropy, hinge.

1.4 Bias-Variance Tradeoff

The Central Dilemma of ML: Simple models (like a straight line) might miss important patterns (high bias = underfitting). Complex models (like a wiggly polynomial) might memorize noise in training data (high variance = overfitting). The sweet spot is in between.

Analogy: Imagine describing a friend's face. Too simple: "They have a face" (high bias). Too complex: "They had a specific pimple at coordinate (3.2, 4.7) on Tuesday" (high variance — won't generalize). Just right: "They have brown eyes, curly hair, and a scar on their chin."

Expected Prediction Error Decomposition:

$$E[(y - \hat{f}(x))^2] = \underbrace{\text{Bias}^2[\hat{f}(x)]}_{\text{systematic error}} + \underbrace{\text{Var}[\hat{f}(x)]}_{\text{model instability}} + \underbrace{\sigma^2}_{\text{irreducible noise}}$$

where:

$$\text{Bias}[\hat{f}(x)] = E[\hat{f}(x)] - f(x)$$ $$\text{Var}[\hat{f}(x)] = E\left[(\hat{f}(x) - E[\hat{f}(x)])^2\right]$$ $$\sigma^2 = \text{Var}(\epsilon) \quad \text{(irreducible, inherent noise in data)}$$

In words:

  • Bias²: How far off is our model ON AVERAGE from the truth? (systematic under/over-prediction)
  • Variance: How much does our model CHANGE if we train on different data? (instability)
  • Noise (σ²): Randomness in the data itself — NO model can reduce this

1.5 Overfitting and Underfitting

The Two Ways Models Can Fail:

  • Underfitting (too simple): Your model is too dumb to capture the pattern. Like using a straight line to fit a curve — it misses the shape entirely.
  • Overfitting (too complex): Your model memorizes the training data (including noise) and fails on new data. Like a student who memorizes practice answers word-for-word but can't solve new problems.

How to tell: If training error ≈ test error ≈ high → underfitting. If training error is low but test error is high → overfitting.

Underfitting (High Bias)Overfitting (High Variance)
Training ErrorHighVery Low (≈ 0)
Test ErrorHighHigh
Gap (Test - Train)Small (both bad)Large
ModelToo simpleToo complex
FixMore features, higher degree, less regularizationRegularization, fewer features, more data, pruning

1.6 Parametric vs Non-Parametric Methods

Two approaches to learning:

  • Parametric: Assume a specific shape upfront (e.g., "I bet it's a straight line") and just learn the parameters (slope, intercept). Fast, but wrong if the assumption is bad.
  • Non-Parametric: Make no assumptions about shape — let the data speak. More flexible but needs more data and is slower.

Analogy: Parametric = fitting a pre-made template. Non-parametric = freehand drawing.

AspectParametricNon-Parametric
AssumptionAssumes functional form (e.g., linear)No fixed functional form
ParametersFixed number (independent of $n$)Grows with data size $n$
ExamplesLinear/Logistic Regression, LDA, Naive BayesKNN, Decision Trees, Kernel SVM
BiasHigher (if assumed form is wrong)Lower (more flexible)
VarianceLower (fewer parameters)Higher (more parameters)
Data neededLessMore
InterpretabilityHigher (coefficients have meaning)Lower (often black-box)
ComputationFast predictionCan be slow (stores all data)
Key insight: Parametric methods reduce the problem to estimating a fixed set of parameters. If the true function matches the assumed form, they're optimal. If not, they have inherent bias that no amount of data can fix. Non-parametric methods can approximate any function given enough data, but need much more data to do so.

1.7 Flexible vs Inflexible Models

Flexibility spectrum:

Inflexible ← Linear Regression < Ridge < LASSO < KNN (large K) < KNN (small K) < Decision Tree (deep) → Flexible

When flexible methods are better:

  • Large sample size $n$, small number of predictors $p$
  • Highly nonlinear true relationship
  • Low noise variance

When inflexible methods are better:

  • Small $n$, large $p$ (high-dimensional)
  • High noise variance $\sigma^2$ (flexible fits noise)
  • Approximately linear true relationship
  • Interpretability is needed

1.8 K-Nearest Neighbors (KNN)

The simplest ML algorithm: To classify a new point, just look at the $K$ closest training points and take a majority vote. No training phase at all — it just memorizes the data!

Analogy: You move to a new neighborhood and wonder "Is this a good area?" You ask your $K$ nearest neighbors their opinion and go with the majority. If $K=1$, you ask one neighbor (risky — might be an outlier). If $K=100$, you ask everyone in town (too general — ignores local variation).

KNN Classification: For query $\mathbf{x}_0$, find $K$ closest training points $\mathcal{N}_K(\mathbf{x}_0)$:

$$\hat{y} = \text{mode}\{y_i : \mathbf{x}_i \in \mathcal{N}_K(\mathbf{x}_0)\}$$

KNN Regression:

$$\hat{f}(x_0) = \frac{1}{K}\sum_{\mathbf{x}_i \in \mathcal{N}_K(x_0)} y_i$$

Weighted KNN: Weight inversely by distance $w_i = 1/d(\mathbf{x}_0, \mathbf{x}_i)$:

$$\hat{y} = \frac{\sum_{i \in \mathcal{N}_K} w_i \cdot y_i}{\sum_{i \in \mathcal{N}_K} w_i}$$

Distance metrics:

  • Euclidean: $d(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{x}') = \sqrt{\sum_{j=1}^p (x_j - x_j')^2}$
  • Manhattan: $d(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{x}') = \sum_{j=1}^p |x_j - x_j'|$
  • Minkowski ($p$-norm): $d(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{x}') = \left(\sum_{j=1}^p |x_j - x_j'|^q\right)^{1/q}$

KNN and Bias-Variance

$K$ valueBiasVarianceBehavior
$K = 1$Zero training errorVery HighOverfitting — jagged boundary
$K$ moderateModerateModerateGood generalization
$K = n$Very HighZeroAlways predicts majority class
Curse of Dimensionality: In high dimensions, all points become approximately equidistant. KNN fails because "nearest" neighbors aren't meaningfully close. Need exponentially more data as $d$ grows.

1.9 Decision Trees

What is a Decision Tree? A flowchart-like model that makes decisions by asking a series of yes/no questions about features. "Is income > $50K?" → "Is age > 30?" → "Approved for loan."

Analogy: It's like the game "20 Questions" — at each step, you ask the question that best narrows down the answer. The "best question" is the one that most clearly separates the classes (measured by Gini impurity or entropy).

Splitting Criteria (Classification):

Gini Impurity — the probability that a randomly chosen sample from node $t$ would be incorrectly classified if labeled according to the class distribution in that node:

$$G(t) = 1 - \sum_{k=1}^K p_k^2$$

Entropy — a measure of uncertainty (disorder) in the class distribution at a node; equals 0 when all samples belong to one class (pure), maximum when classes are equally likely:

$$H(t) = -\sum_{k=1}^K p_k \log_2 p_k$$

Information Gain — the reduction in entropy achieved by splitting a node on a particular feature; the split that maximizes IG is chosen:

$$IG = H(\text{parent}) - \sum_{\text{children}} \frac{|c|}{|t|} H(c)$$

Splitting Criteria (Regression):

$$\min_{j,s}\left[\sum_{x_i \in R_1(j,s)}(y_i - \bar{y}_{R_1})^2 + \sum_{x_i \in R_2(j,s)}(y_i - \bar{y}_{R_2})^2\right]$$

Prediction: Each leaf predicts the majority class (classification) or mean of $y$ values (regression) in that region.

Decision Trees: Properties

  • Advantages: Interpretable, handles mixed types, no scaling needed, captures interactions
  • Disadvantages: High variance (unstable), prone to overfitting, axis-aligned splits only
  • Deep tree: Low bias, high variance → overfitting
  • Shallow tree: High bias, low variance → underfitting
  • Outlier sensitivity: Only affects local partition (unlike linear regression which affects globally)

Regression Trees (vs Classification Trees)

Key difference: Classification trees predict a class label at each leaf; regression trees predict a continuous value (the mean of $y$ values in that leaf).

Splitting criterion — minimize SSE:

For every possible split point (feature $j$, threshold $s$), compute:

$$\text{SSE}(j,s) = \underbrace{\sum_{x_i \in \text{Left}}(y_i - \bar{y}_{\text{Left}})^2}_{\text{Left child SSE}} + \underbrace{\sum_{x_i \in \text{Right}}(y_i - \bar{y}_{\text{Right}})^2}_{\text{Right child SSE}}$$

Choose the $(j, s)$ that gives the smallest total SSE.

How it works:

  1. Start with all data in one node. Its prediction = $\bar{y}$ (mean of all $y$).
  2. For each feature, try every possible split threshold. Compute left/right means and SSE.
  3. Pick the best split (lowest SSE). Create two child nodes.
  4. Recurse on each child until stopping criterion is met (max depth, min samples, etc.).

Prediction: For a new $x$, traverse the tree and output $\hat{y} = \bar{y}_{\text{leaf}}$ (mean of the training $y$ values in the leaf where $x$ lands).

AspectClassification TreeRegression Tree
TargetCategorical $y \in \{0,1,\ldots\}$Continuous $y \in \mathbb{R}$
Split criterionGini impurity / EntropySSE (sum of squared errors)
Leaf predictionMajority class voteMean of $y$ values in leaf
Impurity measure$1 - \sum p_k^2$ or $-\sum p_k \log p_k$$\sum(y_i - \bar{y})^2$

Decision Tree Pruning (Overfitting Control)

Problem: A fully-grown decision tree achieves 100% training accuracy by memorizing the data (one leaf per sample). It overfits → poor test performance.

Pre-pruning (Early Stopping) — stop growing before the tree gets too complex:

  • Max depth: Stop splitting beyond a fixed depth (e.g., depth ≤ 5)
  • Min samples per leaf: Require ≥ $n$ samples to create a leaf (e.g., min_leaf ≥ 10)
  • Min samples to split: Only split a node if it has ≥ $m$ samples
  • Min impurity decrease: Only split if $\Delta\text{Gini}$ or $\Delta\text{SSE}$ exceeds a threshold

Post-pruning — grow the full tree, then remove unhelpful branches:

  • Cost-Complexity Pruning (CCP): Minimize a penalized objective: $$R_\alpha(T) = R(T) + \alpha \cdot |T|$$ where $R(T)$ = misclassification rate (or SSE), $|T|$ = number of leaves, $\alpha$ = complexity penalty. Larger $\alpha$ → simpler tree. CCP removes the branch that increases error the least.
  • Reduced-Error Pruning: For each internal node, check if replacing its subtree with a single leaf increases validation error. If not (or error stays same), prune it. Repeat bottom-up.

How to choose $\alpha$? Use cross-validation: try a range of $\alpha$ values, pick the one that minimizes CV error.

Pre-pruning vs Post-pruning:
  • Pre-pruning (proactive): Faster training, but may stop too early — a mediocre split might enable an excellent split below it.
  • Post-pruning (reactive): More thorough, generally produces better trees, but requires growing the full tree first (expensive for large data).

1.10 Feature Scaling

Why scale features? Imagine comparing "income" (range: 20,000–200,000) with "age" (range: 18–80). Without scaling, income dominates any distance calculation simply because its numbers are bigger — not because it's more important! Feature scaling puts all features on equal footing.

Standardization (Z-score): $x' = \frac{x - \mu}{\sigma}$ → mean 0, variance 1

Min-Max Normalization: $x' = \frac{x - x_{\min}}{x_{\max} - x_{\min}}$ → range [0,1]

When scaling is essential:
  • KNN: Yes — distance-based, features with large range dominate
  • SVM: Yes — margin depends on distances
  • PCA: Yes — variance-based, unscaled features with large variance dominate
  • Gradient Descent: Yes — helps convergence (isotropic loss landscape)
  • Ridge/LASSO: Yes — penalty treats all coefficients equally
When scaling is NOT needed:
  • Decision Trees: Splits are based on thresholds, invariant to monotone transforms
  • Naive Bayes: Computes probabilities independently per feature
  • Linear Regression (OLS): Predictions unchanged (coefficients rescale to compensate)

Important: Scaling changes OLS coefficients but NOT predictions or the optimal loss value.

1.11 Cost-Sensitive Learning

Why not just minimize errors equally? Because some mistakes are worse than others! Missing a cancer diagnosis (false negative) is far worse than a false alarm (false positive). Cost-sensitive learning assigns different penalties to different types of errors so the model prioritizes avoiding the expensive mistakes.

Weighted classification error:

$$E_{in} = \frac{1}{N}\sum_{i=1}^N c(y_i) \cdot \mathbb{1}[y_i \neq h(\mathbf{x}_i)]$$

where $c(y_i)$ assigns higher cost to classes where misclassification is more dangerous.

Cost matrix approach:

Predict +Predict −
Actual +0 (correct)$c_{FN}$ (miss)
Actual −$c_{FP}$ (false alarm)0 (correct)

Set $c_{FN} \gg c_{FP}$ when false negatives are dangerous (medical, safety).

1.12 Evaluation Metrics for Classification

Beyond Accuracy: If 99% of emails are not-spam, a model that always says "not spam" has 99% accuracy but is completely useless! You need metrics that care about the rare class:

  • Precision: "When I say it's spam, how often am I right?" (avoid false alarms)
  • Recall: "Of all actual spam, how much did I catch?" (avoid missing things)
  • F1: Harmonic mean of Precision & Recall — balances both concerns
Predicted PositivePredicted Negative
Actual PositiveTP (True Positive)FN (False Negative / Type II)
Actual NegativeFP (False Positive / Type I)TN (True Negative)
$$\text{Accuracy} = \frac{TP + TN}{TP + TN + FP + FN}$$ $$\text{Precision} = \frac{TP}{TP + FP} \quad \text{(of predicted +, how many correct?)}$$ $$\text{Recall (Sensitivity/TPR)} = \frac{TP}{TP + FN} \quad \text{(of actual +, how many found?)}$$ $$\text{Specificity (TNR)} = \frac{TN}{TN + FP}$$ $$F_1 = \frac{2 \cdot \text{Precision} \cdot \text{Recall}}{\text{Precision} + \text{Recall}} = \frac{2TP}{2TP + FP + FN}$$ $$F_\beta = \frac{(1+\beta^2) \cdot \text{Precision} \cdot \text{Recall}}{\beta^2 \cdot \text{Precision} + \text{Recall}}$$

1.13 Loss Functions Summary

Regression Losses:

$$\text{MSE} = \frac{1}{n}\sum(y_i - \hat{y}_i)^2 \quad \text{(sensitive to outliers, minimizer = mean)}$$ $$\text{MAE} = \frac{1}{n}\sum|y_i - \hat{y}_i| \quad \text{(robust to outliers, minimizer = median)}$$ $$\text{Huber}_\delta(r) = \begin{cases} \frac{1}{2}r^2 & |r| \leq \delta \\ \delta|r| - \frac{\delta^2}{2} & |r| > \delta \end{cases}$$

Classification Losses:

$$\text{0-1 Loss: } L = \mathbb{1}[y \neq \hat{y}]$$ $$\text{Cross-Entropy: } L = -[y\log\hat{p} + (1-y)\log(1-\hat{p})]$$ $$\text{Hinge (SVM): } L = \max(0, 1 - y \cdot f(x))$$ $$\text{Exponential (AdaBoost): } L = e^{-y \cdot f(x)}$$

1.14 Effect of Outliers on Models

Ranking by sensitivity to high-leverage outlier (most → least):

  1. Linear Regression — Global effect; MSE amplifies outlier; shifts entire line
  2. KNN Regression — Local effect only in neighborhood of outlier
  3. Decision Tree Regression — Only affects local leaf partition
  4. Support Vector Regression — ε-insensitive; outlier may have zero effect if outside tube

2. When to Use

KNN

  • Non-linear decision boundaries needed
  • No distributional assumptions
  • Small-to-medium datasets (slow for large $n$: $O(nd)$ per query)
  • Low-dimensional data ($d$ small — curse of dimensionality otherwise)
  • Baseline model for comparison

Decision Trees

  • Need interpretability (if-then rules)
  • Mixed feature types (categorical + numerical) without encoding
  • Feature interactions important
  • No need for feature scaling
  • Usually better as part of ensemble (Random Forest, XGBoost)

Linear/Parametric Models

  • High-dimensional data ($p \gg n$) with regularization
  • Need interpretable coefficients
  • Approximately linear relationship or limited data
  • Fast training and prediction needed

3. All Formulae

Bias-Variance

$$\text{EPE}(x_0) = \sigma^2 + \text{Bias}^2[\hat{f}(x_0)] + \text{Var}[\hat{f}(x_0)]$$ $$\text{Bias}[\hat{f}(x_0)] = E[\hat{f}(x_0)] - f(x_0)$$ $$\text{Var}[\hat{f}(x_0)] = E\left[(\hat{f}(x_0) - E[\hat{f}(x_0)])^2\right]$$

KNN Formulas

$$\hat{f}_{\text{reg}}(x_0) = \frac{1}{K}\sum_{x_i \in \mathcal{N}_K(x_0)} y_i$$ $$P(Y=j|X=x_0) = \frac{1}{K}\sum_{x_i \in \mathcal{N}_K(x_0)} \mathbb{1}(y_i = j)$$ $$\hat{f}_{\text{weighted}}(x_0) = \frac{\sum_{i=1}^K w_i y_i}{\sum_{i=1}^K w_i}, \quad w_i = \frac{1}{d(x_0, x_i)^q}$$ $$d_{\text{Euclidean}} = \sqrt{\sum_{j=1}^p(x_j - x_j')^2}, \quad d_{\text{Manhattan}} = \sum_{j=1}^p|x_j - x_j'|$$

Decision Tree Metrics

$$\text{Gini}(t) = 1 - \sum_{k=1}^K p_k^2$$ $$\text{Entropy}(t) = -\sum_{k=1}^K p_k \log_2 p_k$$ $$\text{IG}(t, \text{split}) = H(t) - \sum_{c} \frac{|c|}{|t|} H(c)$$ $$\text{MSE split}: \min_{j,s}\left[\sum_{i \in L}(y_i - \bar{y}_L)^2 + \sum_{i \in R}(y_i - \bar{y}_R)^2\right]$$

Evaluation Metrics

$$\text{Precision} = \frac{TP}{TP+FP}, \quad \text{Recall} = \frac{TP}{TP+FN}$$ $$F_1 = \frac{2 \cdot P \cdot R}{P + R}, \quad \text{Accuracy} = \frac{TP+TN}{N}$$ $$\text{Specificity} = \frac{TN}{TN+FP}, \quad \text{FPR} = 1 - \text{Specificity}$$

Feature Scaling

$$x_{\text{std}} = \frac{x - \mu}{\sigma}, \quad x_{\text{norm}} = \frac{x - x_{\min}}{x_{\max} - x_{\min}}$$

4. Best Practices

KNN

  • Always standardize features before applying KNN
  • Use odd $K$ for binary classification to avoid ties
  • Select $K$ via cross-validation
  • Use weighted KNN for unequal density regions
  • Avoid high-dimensional data (use PCA first if needed)
  • KNN with $K=1$: training error = 0 always (memorizes data)

Decision Trees

  • Limit depth or prune to control overfitting
  • Use min_samples_leaf to avoid tiny leaves
  • Prefer ensemble (RF/XGBoost) over single tree for prediction
  • No feature scaling needed
  • Single trees are unstable — small data changes → different tree

General Model Selection

  • Always evaluate final model on a held-out test set
  • Use CV to select hyperparameters (K, depth, λ), NOT to report final performance
  • Prefer simpler model when performance is similar (Occam's razor)
  • Report appropriate metric: accuracy (balanced classes), F1 (imbalanced), AUC-ROC
Common Exam Mistakes:
  • Thinking low $K$ in KNN always generalizes better (NO — it overfits)
  • Assuming flexible always beats simple (curse of dimensionality!)
  • Using accuracy on imbalanced data (use precision/recall/F1 instead)
  • Forgetting to standardize before distance-based methods
  • Confusing training error with generalization error
  • Claiming Decision Trees need feature scaling (they don't)

5. Example Problems (Solved Exam Questions)

Problem 1: Weighted KNN Classification [Practice Assignment]

Question: In Weighted K-Nearest Neighbors (KNN), instead of giving each neighbor an equal vote, we weight votes by the inverse of their distance: closer neighbors get MORE influence. Given labeled training points: $(0,0)$ Red, $(1,0)$ Red, $(2,2)$ Blue, $(8,1)$ Blue. Using weighted KNN with $K=3$ and weights $w_i = 1/d_i$ (inverse distance weighting), classify the query point $(1,1)$.

Recall: In standard KNN, we find the $K$ nearest neighbors and take a majority vote. In weighted KNN, each neighbor's vote is weighted by $w_i = 1/d_i$, so closer neighbors contribute more to the decision. We sum weights per class and pick the class with the highest total weight.

Step 1: Compute Euclidean distances from query point $(1,1)$ to all training points

PointLabelDistance $d = \sqrt{(x_1-1)^2+(x_2-1)^2}$
$(0,0)$Red$\sqrt{(1-0)^2+(1-0)^2} = \sqrt{2} \approx 1.414$
$(1,0)$Red$\sqrt{(1-1)^2+(1-0)^2} = \sqrt{0+1} = 1.0$
$(2,2)$Blue$\sqrt{(1-2)^2+(1-2)^2} = \sqrt{1+1} = \sqrt{2} \approx 1.414$
$(8,1)$Blue$\sqrt{(1-8)^2+(1-1)^2} = \sqrt{49+0} = 7.0$

Step 2: Select the $K=3$ nearest neighbors

Sorting by distance: $(1,0)$ [d=1.0], $(0,0)$ [d=1.414], $(2,2)$ [d=1.414]. The 3 nearest are these three.

Step 3: Compute weighted votes per class

$$\text{Weight for Red} = w_{(1,0)} + w_{(0,0)} = \frac{1}{1.0} + \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} = 1.0 + 0.707 = 1.707$$ $$\text{Weight for Blue} = w_{(2,2)} = \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} = 0.707$$

Step 4: Classify by highest total weight

Red (1.707) > Blue (0.707), so classify as $\boxed{\text{Red}}$

Key insight: The closest point $(1,0)$ is Red and has the strongest weight (1.0). Without weighting (standard KNN), it would be 2 Red vs 1 Blue = still Red. Here the weighting reinforces that decision. Weighting helps most when some neighbors are much closer than others.

Problem 2: KNN Model Selection [Practice]

Question: Given:

$K$Train AccVal AccDiagnosis
1Very HighLowOverfitting
5HighHighBest balance
25ModerateModerateStarting to underfit
100LowLowSevere underfitting

Answer: $K = 5$ preferred. It has the best validation accuracy, indicating good generalization.

Why evaluate on separate test set after CV? Because CV was used to SELECT $K$ — the CV score is optimistic for the selected model (selection bias). A truly held-out test set gives an unbiased generalization estimate.

Problem 3: Flexible vs Inflexible [Practice — Pinak Mahapatra]

(a) Large $n$, small $p$: Flexible better. Enough data to support complex models without overfitting.

(b) Large $p$, small $n$: Inflexible better. Flexible models overfit with limited data in high dimensions. Use regularized models (Ridge/LASSO).

(c) Highly nonlinear: Flexible better. Linear models have high, irreducible bias for nonlinear truth.

(d) High $\sigma^2$: Inflexible better. Flexible models fit noise → high variance. Simpler models provide better generalization.

Problem 4: Precision & Recall [Practice — Shubhranil]

Confusion matrix:

Pred +Pred −
Actual +80 (TP)20 (FN)
Actual −40 (FP)860 (TN)
$$\text{Precision} = \frac{80}{80+40} = \frac{80}{120} = 0.667 \;(66.7\%)$$ $$\text{Recall} = \frac{80}{80+20} = \frac{80}{100} = 0.800 \;(80\%)$$ $$\text{Accuracy} = \frac{80+860}{1000} = 0.94 \;(94\%)$$ $$F_1 = \frac{2(0.667)(0.8)}{0.667+0.8} = \frac{1.067}{1.467} = 0.727$$

Why recall matters more in medical diagnosis:

  • False negative (FN = 20): Patient has disease but goes undiagnosed → fatal consequences
  • False positive (FP = 40): Healthy patient gets extra tests → inconvenient but not dangerous
  • Cost of FN ≫ Cost of FP in safety-critical applications

Problem 5: Effect of Outliers on Different ML Models [End-Term Q4]

Question: Consider a high-leverage outlier (a point with extreme $x$ value that is far from the true regression line). Arrange the following methods in order of DECREASING influence of this outlier: Linear Regression, KNN Regression, Decision Tree Regression, Support Vector Regression (SVR). Justify each ranking.

Recall: A "high-leverage" point has extreme predictor values. "Influence" means how much adding this one point changes the model's predictions across the entire input space. Different loss functions and model structures handle outliers differently.

  1. Linear Regression (MOST affected):

    OLS minimizes the global MSE $\sum(y_i - \hat{y}_i)^2$. Squared loss amplifies large residuals. High leverage means the point has outsized influence on the slope (formally: influence $\propto h_{ii} = x_i(X^TX)^{-1}x_i^T$). A single outlier can pull the entire regression line toward it.

  2. KNN Regression:

    KNN is a local method — predictions at a query point depend ONLY on its $K$ nearest neighbors. The outlier only affects predictions for query points that have it as a neighbor. Distant predictions are completely unaffected.

  3. Decision Tree Regression:

    Trees partition the input space. An outlier only affects the leaf node (partition) it falls into. All other regions of the tree are completely unchanged. Similar to KNN in being local, but even more contained within its partition.

  4. SVR (LEAST affected):

    SVR uses the ε-insensitive loss: points within a tube of width ε around the prediction have ZERO loss. The model only depends on support vectors (points at or outside the tube boundary). A single outlier that's far from the tube may not even become a support vector, having literally zero effect on the model.

Summary ranking: Linear Regression > KNN > Decision Tree > SVR (most to least affected)

Problem 6: Weighted Classification Error for Imbalanced Data [End-Term Q3]

Question: An electric car company collected data of 10,000 simulated tests and observed the car crashed with a pedestrian about 100 times. A classification model is to be built. Propose a weighted classification error metric for the problem and briefly justify.

Recall: In cost-sensitive learning, we assign different misclassification costs to different types of errors. The standard (unweighted) accuracy would be meaningless here because a trivial "always predict no-crash" gets $9900/10000 = 99\%$ accuracy while missing every crash!

Step 1: Identify the asymmetry

  • False Negative (predict no-crash when crash occurs): Catastrophic — pedestrian gets hit
  • False Positive (predict crash when none occurs): Inconvenient — unnecessary braking

Missing a crash (FN) is FAR worse than a false alarm (FP), so we must penalize FN much more heavily.

Step 2: Propose the weighted error metric

$$E_{\text{weighted}} = \frac{1}{N}\left[w_{\text{crash}} \sum_{\text{crash}} \mathbb{1}[\hat{y}_i \neq y_i] + w_{\text{no-crash}} \sum_{\text{no-crash}} \mathbb{1}[\hat{y}_i \neq y_i]\right]$$

Assign: $w_{\text{crash}} = 100$ (high cost for missing a crash), $w_{\text{no-crash}} = 1$ (low cost for false alarm).

Step 3: Justify the ratio

  • Safety justification: Missing a crash is ~100× worse than a false alarm (life-threatening vs. inconvenient).
  • Class balance justification: The crash class has 100 samples vs 9900 no-crash. Weighting by $w_{\text{crash}} = 100$ makes the effective contribution of each class equal: $100 \times 100 = 10000 \approx 1 \times 9900$. This prevents the model from ignoring the minority class.

Effect: The model is incentivized to err on the side of caution — predicting "crash" even when uncertain. This is appropriate for safety-critical applications where false negatives have severe consequences.

Problem 7: Finding the Representative Value of a Dataset [End-Term Q2]

Question: Given a dataset of $M$ values, we want to find a single "representative" value $c$ that best summarizes the data. (a) What is the optimal representative when the data has NO outliers? (b) What should we use when outliers ARE present? Derive each from an optimization perspective.

Recall: The choice of representative depends on the loss function we minimize. Different loss functions have different sensitivities to extreme values.

(a) No outliers → Use the Mean (minimizes MSE/squared loss):

$$c^* = \arg\min_c \sum_{i=1}^M (y_i - c)^2$$

Taking derivative and setting to zero: $-2\sum(y_i - c) = 0 \implies c = \frac{1}{M}\sum_{i=1}^M y_i = \bar{y}$

The mean is the MSE-optimal representative. It works well when data is roughly symmetric without extreme values, because squared loss gives quadratically increasing weight to far-away points.

(b) With outliers → Use the Median (minimizes MAE/absolute loss):

$$c^* = \arg\min_c \sum_{i=1}^M |y_i - c| = \text{median}(y_1, \ldots, y_M)$$

The median is robust because the gradient of $|y_i - c|$ is always $\pm 1$ regardless of how far away $y_i$ is. An outlier at $y = 10000$ contributes the same gradient as a normal point at $y = 5$. This makes the median insensitive to extreme values.

Intuition: Mean = "balance point" (affected by all values proportionally). Median = "midpoint by count" (only cares about ordering, not magnitudes).

Problem 8: True/False Justifications [End-Term Q1]

Question: For each statement, determine if it is TRUE or FALSE and provide a clear justification explaining your reasoning.

(c) "Determining the age of a dog using a breed lifespan database is Reinforcement Learning (RL)" → FALSE

Why: Reinforcement Learning requires 5 specific elements: an agent, an environment, states, actions, and rewards, with sequential decision-making over time. Looking up a dog's age from a database is simply a regression/lookup problem — given input (breed, characteristics), predict output (age). This is supervised learning (or even just database retrieval), not RL. There's no agent taking actions or receiving rewards.

(d) "KNN with lower K always generalizes better than logistic regression" → FALSE

Why: The word "always" makes this false. Lower $K$ in KNN means:

  • Higher model complexity (more flexible decision boundary)
  • Lower bias but HIGHER variance → prone to overfitting
In high dimensions (curse of dimensionality) or with noisy data, logistic regression (a parametric, low-variance model) often generalizes BETTER than low-K KNN. No universal "always" statement holds across all possible datasets.

(e) "A cross-validation set of 200 gives a better $E_{out}$ estimate than 100" → FALSE (in the given context)

Why: With 8000 total training points:

  • CV set of 200 → train on 7800, validate on 200
  • CV set of 100 → train on 7900, validate on 100
The model trained on 7900 points is closer to the full 8000-point model, so its validation error is a less biased estimate of the true $E_{out}$ of the final model. The tradeoff: fewer validation points = higher variance of the estimate, but the question asks about estimating $E_{out}$ of the model that will ultimately be trained on all 8000 points. The 100-point CV set gives less bias in that estimate.

Problem 9: Bias-Variance with Tree Depth [Practice — Pinak]

(a) Increasing tree depth:

  • Bias: ↓ (model can fit more complex patterns)
  • Variance: ↑ (model becomes sensitive to specific training data)
  • Training error: ↓ monotonically (more capacity to fit training data)

(b) Why deep trees overfit: At maximum depth, each leaf may contain 1 point → perfect training fit but captures noise. Essentially memorizing the data with no generalization.

(c) Test vs train error behavior:

  • Training error: Monotonically ↓ with depth
  • Test error: Initially ↓ (reducing bias), then ↑ (variance dominates) — U-shaped

(d) Optimal depth: Where validation/test error is minimized. This is the bias-variance sweet spot.

(e) When non-parametric beats linear: True function is complex/nonlinear and sufficient data available. Linear beats non-parametric when: limited data, high noise, approximately linear relationship.

Problem 10: Feature Scaling and OLS [Practice — Pinak]

Question: A predictor is scaled by factor of 2. What happens?

If $x_j' = 2x_j$, then in OLS:

  • The optimal loss does NOT change (same predictions possible)
  • The coefficient $\hat{\beta}_j$ halves: $\hat{\beta}_j' = \hat{\beta}_j / 2$ (compensates for doubled input)
  • Predictions $\hat{y}$ are identical: $\hat{\beta}_j' \cdot x_j' = (\hat{\beta}_j/2)(2x_j) = \hat{\beta}_j x_j$

Why it matters for regularization: Ridge/LASSO penalize $\|\beta\|$. If features aren't scaled, features with large range have small $\beta$ and get penalized less → unfair regularization. Always standardize before regularized regression.

Problem 11: Decision Tree — Entropy and Information Gain [Practice Q12]

Question: Classify using features Outlook $\in$ {Sunny, Overcast} and Humidity $\in$ {High, Normal}, label Play $\in$ {Yes, No}:

OutlookHumidityPlay
SunnyHighNo
SunnyNormalYes
OvercastHighYes
OvercastNormalYes
SunnyHighNo

(a) Compute entropy $H(\text{Play})$. (b) Compute information gain for Outlook and Humidity. (c) Which feature is the root?

Recall: Entropy measures impurity: $H(S) = -\sum_c p_c \log_2 p_c$. Information gain = entropy reduction from splitting: $IG(S, A) = H(S) - \sum_{v} \frac{|S_v|}{|S|} H(S_v)$

(a) Entropy of full dataset:

Play: Yes = 3, No = 2 (out of 5 total)

$$H(\text{Play}) = -\frac{3}{5}\log_2\frac{3}{5} - \frac{2}{5}\log_2\frac{2}{5}$$ $$= -0.6 \times (-0.737) - 0.4 \times (-1.322) = 0.442 + 0.529 = \boxed{0.971 \text{ bits}}$$

(b) Information Gain — Split on Outlook:

Sunny (3 samples): Yes=1, No=2 → $H = -\frac{1}{3}\log_2\frac{1}{3} - \frac{2}{3}\log_2\frac{2}{3} = 0.528 + 0.390 = 0.918$

Overcast (2 samples): Yes=2, No=0 → $H = 0$ (pure node!)

$$IG(\text{Outlook}) = 0.971 - \frac{3}{5}(0.918) - \frac{2}{5}(0) = 0.971 - 0.551 = \boxed{0.420}$$

Information Gain — Split on Humidity:

High (3 samples): rows (Sunny,High,No), (Overcast,High,Yes), (Sunny,High,No) → Yes=1, No=2 → $H = 0.918$

Normal (2 samples): rows (Sunny,Normal,Yes), (Overcast,Normal,Yes) → Yes=2, No=0 → $H = 0$

$$IG(\text{Humidity}) = 0.971 - \frac{3}{5}(0.918) - \frac{2}{5}(0) = 0.971 - 0.551 = \boxed{0.420}$$

(c) Which feature for root?

Both have equal information gain (0.420). Either can be chosen as the root node. In practice, ties are broken arbitrarily (by feature order, or random selection).

Note: In both splits, the "Overcast" (or "Normal") subset is already pure (all Yes), so no further splitting is needed there. The impure subset (Sunny/High) would need a second-level split.

Topic 2: Mathematical Foundations

Covers: Linear Algebra (Vectors, Matrices, Eigenvalues, Span, Independence), Convexity, Probability (Bayes Theorem, Expected Value, Variance), Statistics (Hypothesis Testing, t-statistics, Confidence Intervals, MLE, MAP), Random Walks

Why this matters: Every ML algorithm is built on math. Linear algebra tells us how data is structured (vectors, matrices). Probability tells us how to handle uncertainty. Statistics tells us how to learn from data. You don't need to be a mathematician — but understanding these building blocks makes everything else click.

1. Basic Understanding

1.1 Linear Algebra Essentials

What is this? Linear algebra is the math of arrows (vectors) and grids (matrices). In ML, each data point is a vector (a list of numbers), and our dataset is a matrix (a table of numbers). Understanding how vectors relate to each other (independence, span) tells us about the structure of our data.

Vectors and Spaces

Linear Combination: $\mathbf{v} = c_1\mathbf{v}_1 + c_2\mathbf{v}_2 + \ldots + c_k\mathbf{v}_k$

Span: $\text{Span}\{\mathbf{v}_1, \ldots, \mathbf{v}_k\}$ = set of all linear combinations of those vectors

Linear Independence: Vectors $\{\mathbf{v}_1, \ldots, \mathbf{v}_k\}$ are linearly independent iff:

$$c_1\mathbf{v}_1 + c_2\mathbf{v}_2 + \ldots + c_k\mathbf{v}_k = \mathbf{0} \implies c_1 = c_2 = \ldots = c_k = 0$$

Key Rule: In $\mathbb{R}^n$, at most $n$ vectors can be linearly independent. If you have $> n$ vectors in $\mathbb{R}^n$, they MUST be linearly dependent.

Rank: $\text{rank}(A)$ = number of linearly independent columns = number of linearly independent rows

Important Matrix Properties

Transpose: $(AB)^T = B^TA^T$

Inverse: $(AB)^{-1} = B^{-1}A^{-1}$, exists iff $\det(A) \neq 0$

Symmetric: $A = A^T$. All eigenvalues are real.

Positive Definite (PD): $\mathbf{x}^TA\mathbf{x} > 0$ for all $\mathbf{x} \neq 0$ iff all eigenvalues $> 0$

Positive Semi-Definite (PSD): $\mathbf{x}^TA\mathbf{x} \geq 0$ for all $\mathbf{x}$ iff all eigenvalues $\geq 0$

Orthogonal: $Q^TQ = QQ^T = I$, preserves lengths and angles

Eigenvalues and Eigenvectors

Intuition: An eigenvector is a direction that doesn't change when you apply a matrix transformation — it only gets stretched or shrunk. The eigenvalue tells you by how much. Think of stretching a rubber sheet: most points move in complex ways, but along certain axes it just stretches uniformly — those are the eigenvectors.

$$A\mathbf{v} = \lambda\mathbf{v}$$

where $\lambda$ = eigenvalue, $\mathbf{v}$ = eigenvector (direction unchanged by $A$)

Finding eigenvalues: Solve $\det(A - \lambda I) = 0$ (characteristic equation)

Eigendecomposition: If $A$ is symmetric: $A = V\Lambda V^T$ where $V$ is orthogonal

Trace: $\text{tr}(A) = \sum \lambda_i$

Determinant: $\det(A) = \prod \lambda_i$

Role in ML:

  • PCA: eigenvectors of covariance matrix = principal components
  • Convexity: Hessian PD ⟹ function strictly convex (unique minimum)
  • Covariance matrices are always PSD

Column Space and Projection

Column space of $X$: $\text{Col}(X) = \{X\beta : \beta \in \mathbb{R}^p\}$

Projection of $\mathbf{y}$ onto $\text{Col}(X)$:

$$\hat{\mathbf{y}} = X(X^TX)^{-1}X^T\mathbf{y} = H\mathbf{y}$$

where $H = X(X^TX)^{-1}X^T$ is the hat matrix (projection matrix)

Residual: $\mathbf{r} = \mathbf{y} - \hat{\mathbf{y}}$ is orthogonal to $\text{Col}(X)$: $X^T\mathbf{r} = 0$

This is the geometric interpretation of OLS!

1.2 Convexity

What is convexity? A convex function is bowl-shaped — it curves upward everywhere. If you put a ball anywhere on its surface, it will roll to one single lowest point. This is GREAT for optimization because gradient descent is guaranteed to find the best solution (no tricky local minima to get stuck in). Most ML loss functions are designed to be convex for this reason.

Convex Set: $S$ is convex if $\forall \mathbf{x}, \mathbf{y} \in S$, $\forall \lambda \in [0,1]$: $\lambda\mathbf{x} + (1-\lambda)\mathbf{y} \in S$

Convex Function: $f$ is convex if:

$$f(\lambda\mathbf{x} + (1-\lambda)\mathbf{y}) \leq \lambda f(\mathbf{x}) + (1-\lambda)f(\mathbf{y})$$

Equivalent conditions for twice-differentiable $f$:

  • $f''(x) \geq 0$ (1D)
  • Hessian $H(f) \succeq 0$ (PSD) for all $\mathbf{x}$ (multi-D)

Strictly convex: Hessian $\succ 0$ (PD) → unique global minimum

Why it matters: Convex optimization → any local minimum is the global minimum → gradient descent converges to optimal solution.

Examples of Convex Functions

  • $f(x) = x^2$ (Hessian = 2 > 0) ✓
  • $f(\mathbf{x}) = \|\mathbf{x}\|^2 = \mathbf{x}^T\mathbf{x}$ (Hessian = $2I$ PD) ✓
  • $f(\mathbf{x}) = \mathbf{x}^TA\mathbf{x}$ is convex iff $A$ is PSD
  • MSE loss is convex in parameters for linear regression
  • Cross-entropy loss is convex in parameters for logistic regression
  • $f(x) = |x|$ is convex but not differentiable at 0

1.3 Probability

Why probability in ML? Data is noisy and uncertain. Probability gives us a language to say "I'm 85% sure this email is spam" rather than just "spam" or "not spam." It's the foundation of Bayesian methods, generative models, and understanding how confident we should be in our predictions.

Basic Rules

$$P(A \cup B) = P(A) + P(B) - P(A \cap B)$$ $$P(A|B) = \frac{P(A \cap B)}{P(B)}$$ $$P(A \cap B) = P(A|B)P(B) = P(B|A)P(A)$$

Independence: $P(A \cap B) = P(A)P(B)$ iff $A \perp B$

Law of Total Probability:

$$P(A) = \sum_i P(A|B_i)P(B_i)$$

Bayes Theorem

Intuition: Bayes' theorem answers: "Now that I've seen evidence, how should I update my beliefs?" You start with a prior belief (before seeing data), observe evidence (likelihood), and get an updated posterior belief. It's the backbone of spam filters, medical diagnosis, and all Bayesian ML.

Example: A disease affects 1% of people (prior). A test is 90% accurate. If you test positive, Bayes tells you the ACTUAL probability you're sick (which might be surprisingly low due to false positives!).

$$P(A|B) = \frac{P(B|A) \cdot P(A)}{P(B)} = \frac{P(B|A) \cdot P(A)}{\sum_i P(B|A_i)P(A_i)}$$

In ML context:

$$P(\text{class}|data) = \frac{P(data|\text{class}) \cdot P(\text{class})}{P(data)}$$ $$\text{Posterior} = \frac{\text{Likelihood} \times \text{Prior}}{\text{Evidence}}$$

Expected Value and Variance

Definition (Expected Value): The expected value $E[X]$ of a random variable $X$ is the probability-weighted average of all possible values it can take. It represents the long-run average outcome if the experiment were repeated infinitely many times.

Definition (Variance): The variance $\text{Var}(X)$ measures the expected squared deviation of $X$ from its mean. It quantifies how spread out the distribution is — higher variance means outcomes are more dispersed around the mean.

$$E[X] = \sum_x x \cdot P(X=x) \quad \text{(discrete)}$$ $$E[X] = \int x \cdot f(x)\,dx \quad \text{(continuous)}$$ $$\text{Var}(X) = E[(X - \mu)^2] = E[X^2] - (E[X])^2$$ $$\text{Var}(aX + b) = a^2\text{Var}(X)$$ $$E[X+Y] = E[X] + E[Y] \quad \text{(always — linearity of expectation)}$$ $$\text{Var}(X+Y) = \text{Var}(X) + \text{Var}(Y) \quad \text{(if independent)}$$

Standard Deviation: $\sigma = \sqrt{\text{Var}(X)}$ — same units as $X$, more interpretable than variance.

Important Distributions

Bernoulli: $P(X=1) = p$, $E[X] = p$, $\text{Var}(X) = p(1-p)$

Binomial: $P(X=k) = \binom{n}{k}p^k(1-p)^{n-k}$, $E[X] = np$, $\text{Var} = np(1-p)$

Gaussian: $f(x) = \frac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi\sigma^2}}\exp\left(-\frac{(x-\mu)^2}{2\sigma^2}\right)$

Uniform on $\{1,\ldots,n\}$: $E[X] = \frac{n+1}{2}$, e.g., fair die: $E = 3.5$

Random Walks

What is a Random Walk? Imagine a drunk person walking on a number line, randomly stepping left or right. Will they eventually fall off a cliff (reach 0)? Random walks model stock prices, molecular diffusion, and gambler's ruin problems.

Starting at $x = i$, moving right with prob $p$, left with prob $q = 1-p$. Absorbing barrier at $x = 0$.

Probability of reaching 0 (ruin probability):

$$P(\text{ruin from position } i) = \begin{cases} 1 & \text{if } p \leq 1/2 \\ (q/p)^i & \text{if } p > 1/2 \end{cases}$$

Key insights:

  • Symmetric walk ($p = 1/2$): Probability of eventual ruin = 1 (certain to hit 0)
  • Biased walk ($p > 1/2$): Ruin probability $< 1$, decreasing with starting position $i$
  • Increasing $p$ → higher survival probability (drift away from cliff)
  • Increasing starting distance $i$ → lower ruin probability (further from danger)

1.4 Statistical Inference

What is statistical inference? It's the art of drawing conclusions about the world from limited data. Given a sample, we want to estimate unknown parameters (MLE/MAP), test hypotheses ("is this feature actually useful?"), and quantify our uncertainty (confidence intervals).

Maximum Likelihood Estimation (MLE)

Intuition: MLE asks: "What parameter values would make my observed data MOST LIKELY to have occurred?" Imagine you flip a coin 10 times and get 7 heads. MLE says the most likely $p$ (probability of heads) is 0.7 — because that value maximizes the chance of seeing exactly what you saw.

$$\hat{\theta}_{MLE} = \arg\max_\theta \prod_{i=1}^n P(x_i|\theta) = \arg\max_\theta \sum_{i=1}^n \log P(x_i|\theta)$$

Key property: MLE for Gaussian noise linear regression = OLS estimate

If $y_i = \mathbf{x}_i^T\beta + \epsilon_i$ with $\epsilon_i \sim N(0, \sigma^2)$, then maximizing likelihood equals minimizing $\sum(y_i - \mathbf{x}_i^T\beta)^2$

Maximum A Posteriori (MAP)

Intuition: MAP is MLE with a "reality check." While MLE blindly follows the data, MAP also incorporates your prior beliefs. If you flip a coin 3 times and get 3 heads, MLE says $p=1.0$ (always heads!). MAP says "Wait, I know coins are usually fair" and pulls the estimate back toward 0.5. This is exactly what regularization does!

$$\hat{\theta}_{MAP} = \arg\max_\theta P(\theta|data) = \arg\max_\theta [P(data|\theta) \cdot P(\theta)]$$ $$= \arg\max_\theta [\log P(data|\theta) + \log P(\theta)]$$

Connection to regularization:

  • Gaussian prior $P(\theta) \propto e^{-\lambda\|\theta\|^2}$ → MAP = Ridge regression
  • Laplace prior $P(\theta) \propto e^{-\lambda\|\theta\|_1}$ → MAP = LASSO

Hypothesis Testing

Definition: A hypothesis test is a formal statistical procedure for deciding whether observed data provides sufficient evidence to reject a default assumption (null hypothesis) in favor of an alternative. It controls the probability of incorrectly rejecting a true null hypothesis at a pre-specified significance level $\alpha$ (typically 0.05).

Null hypothesis $H_0$: Default assumption (e.g., $\beta_j = 0$, coefficient is not significant)

Alternative $H_1$: What we want to show (e.g., $\beta_j \neq 0$)

t-statistic:

$$t_j = \frac{\hat{\beta}_j - 0}{SE(\hat{\beta}_j)} = \frac{\hat{\beta}_j}{SE(\hat{\beta}_j)}$$

Standard error of $\hat{\beta}$: $SE(\hat{\beta}_j) = \hat{\sigma}\sqrt{(X^TX)^{-1}_{jj}}$

where $\hat{\sigma}^2 = \frac{RSS}{n-p}$ (unbiased noise variance estimator)

Decision rule: If $|t_j| > t_{\alpha/2, n-p}$ (critical value), reject $H_0$ at level $\alpha$

p-value: Probability of observing test statistic as extreme as computed, given $H_0$ is true. Reject $H_0$ if p-value $< \alpha$.

Confidence Intervals

Definition: A $(1-\alpha)\times 100\%$ confidence interval is a range of values, computed from the sample data, such that if the experiment were repeated many times, $(1-\alpha)\times 100\%$ of such intervals would contain the true population parameter. It does NOT mean there is a $(1-\alpha)$ probability that the true value lies in this specific interval — the true value is fixed; it's the interval that's random across repetitions.

$$\hat{\beta}_j \pm t_{\alpha/2, n-p} \cdot SE(\hat{\beta}_j)$$

Interpretation: We are 95% confident that the true $\beta_j$ lies within this interval. If the interval does not contain 0, we conclude the coefficient is statistically significant at the 5% level (equivalent to rejecting $H_0: \beta_j = 0$).

Width depends on: (1) Confidence level — higher confidence → wider interval. (2) Sample size $n$ — more data → narrower interval. (3) Data variability $\hat{\sigma}$ — more noise → wider interval.

Variance Formulas

Population variance: $\sigma^2 = \frac{1}{N}\sum(x_i - \bar{x})^2$

Sample variance (unbiased): $s^2 = \frac{1}{n-1}\sum(x_i - \bar{x})^2$

Computational formula: $\sigma^2 = \frac{1}{N}\sum x_i^2 - \bar{x}^2$ (mathematically equivalent)

Unbiased noise estimator in regression:

$$\hat{\sigma}^2 = \frac{RSS}{n-p} = \frac{\sum(y_i - \hat{y}_i)^2}{n - p}$$

Divide by $n-p$ (not $n$) because $p$ degrees of freedom used fitting the model.

2. When to Use

Linear Algebra

  • Understanding projection (OLS = projection of $y$ onto column space of $X$)
  • PCA (eigendecomposition of covariance matrix)
  • Checking if features are redundant (linear dependence)
  • Kernel methods (PSD kernel matrices)

Convexity

  • Proving optimization problems have unique solutions
  • Justifying that gradient descent finds global minimum
  • Understanding why MSE and cross-entropy are preferred losses

Probability / Bayes

  • Naive Bayes classifier
  • Gaussian Discriminant Analysis
  • Understanding posterior probability
  • Bayesian interpretation of regularization

Statistical Inference

  • Testing if features are significant ($t$-test on coefficients)
  • Model comparison (F-test)
  • Confidence intervals for predictions
  • Bootstrap for variance estimation

3. All Formulae

Linear Algebra

$$A\mathbf{v} = \lambda\mathbf{v} \quad \text{(eigenvalue equation)}$$ $$\det(A - \lambda I) = 0 \quad \text{(characteristic equation)}$$ $$\text{tr}(A) = \sum_i \lambda_i, \quad \det(A) = \prod_i \lambda_i$$ $$\hat{\mathbf{y}} = H\mathbf{y} = X(X^TX)^{-1}X^T\mathbf{y} \quad \text{(projection/hat matrix)}$$ $$\mathbf{r} = (I - H)\mathbf{y}, \quad X^T\mathbf{r} = 0 \quad \text{(residual orthogonality)}$$

Convexity Tests

$$f \text{ convex} \iff f''(x) \geq 0 \;\forall x \quad \text{(1D)}$$ $$f \text{ convex} \iff H(f) = \nabla^2 f \succeq 0 \;\forall \mathbf{x} \quad \text{(multi-D)}$$ $$f \text{ strictly convex} \iff H(f) \succ 0 \;\forall \mathbf{x}$$

Probability

$$P(A|B) = \frac{P(B|A)P(A)}{P(B)}$$ $$E[X] = \sum x P(x), \quad \text{Var}(X) = E[X^2] - (E[X])^2$$ $$\text{Cov}(X,Y) = E[XY] - E[X]E[Y]$$ $$f_{\text{Gaussian}}(x) = \frac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi\sigma^2}}\exp\left(-\frac{(x-\mu)^2}{2\sigma^2}\right)$$

Statistical Inference

$$t_j = \frac{\hat{\beta}_j}{SE(\hat{\beta}_j)}, \quad SE(\hat{\beta}_j) = \hat{\sigma}\sqrt{(X^TX)^{-1}_{jj}}$$ $$\hat{\sigma}^2 = \frac{RSS}{n-p} = \frac{\|\mathbf{y} - X\hat{\beta}\|^2}{n-p}$$ $$\text{CI: } \hat{\beta}_j \pm t_{\alpha/2,n-p} \cdot SE(\hat{\beta}_j)$$ $$\hat{\theta}_{MLE} = \arg\max_\theta \sum_i \log P(x_i|\theta)$$ $$\hat{\theta}_{MAP} = \arg\max_\theta [\log P(data|\theta) + \log P(\theta)]$$

Bootstrap Variance

$$\text{Var}_{\text{boot}}(\hat{\theta}) = \frac{1}{B-1}\sum_{b=1}^B (\hat{\theta}_b - \bar{\hat{\theta}})^2$$

where $\hat{\theta}_b$ is the estimate from $b$-th bootstrap sample, $\bar{\hat{\theta}} = \frac{1}{B}\sum_b\hat{\theta}_b$

4. Best Practices

  • Convexity check: Always verify your objective is convex before relying on gradient descent to find global optimum
  • Check linear independence: In $\mathbb{R}^n$, $k > n$ vectors are always dependent
  • Numerical stability: Use SVD instead of directly computing $(X^TX)^{-1}$ for OLS
  • Bootstrap: Use $B \geq 1000$ resamples for stable variance estimates
  • t-test: Requires residuals approximately normal (Central Limit Theorem helps for large $n$)
  • MLE vs MAP: MLE overfits with small data; MAP (with proper prior = regularization) helps
Common Mistakes:
  • Confusing sample variance ($n-1$) with population variance ($N$)
  • Forgetting that $>n$ vectors in $\mathbb{R}^n$ are always dependent
  • Assuming PD implies symmetric (PD requires symmetric by definition)
  • Using $n$ instead of $n-p$ for noise variance estimate
  • Confusing MLE with MAP (MAP adds prior/regularization)

5. Example Problems (Solved Exam Questions)

Problem 1: Linear Independence Check [Mid-Term Q2]

Question: Given the set of vectors $\{(1,2), (1,3), (1,4)\} \subset \mathbb{R}^2$. (a) Are these vectors linearly independent? (b) What subspace of $\mathbb{R}^2$ do they span?

Recall: Vectors $\{v_1, \ldots, v_k\}$ are linearly independent if the only solution to $c_1v_1 + c_2v_2 + \ldots + c_kv_k = 0$ is $c_1 = c_2 = \ldots = c_k = 0$. A key theorem: in $\mathbb{R}^n$, at most $n$ vectors can be linearly independent.

(a) Not linearly independent (they are linearly DEPENDENT)

Why: We have 3 vectors in $\mathbb{R}^2$. Since the dimension of the space is 2, any set of more than 2 vectors MUST be linearly dependent. This is a direct consequence of the theorem above.

Explicit dependence relation: We can verify: $(1,4) = 2(1,3) - (1,2)$, which gives us $-1\cdot(1,2) + 2\cdot(1,3) + (-1)\cdot(1,4) = (0,0)$ with not-all-zero coefficients.

(b) Span = all of $\mathbb{R}^2$ (the entire 2D plane)

Why: Check if any two of the vectors are linearly independent. Take $(1,2)$ and $(1,3)$: they are NOT scalar multiples of each other (not parallel), so they are linearly independent and form a basis for $\mathbb{R}^2$. Since a basis spans the whole space, any vector in $\mathbb{R}^2$ can be written as a combination of these.

Key takeaway for ML: In linear regression, if your feature vectors span $\mathbb{R}^p$, the system $X\beta = y$ has a unique least-squares solution. If they don't span the full space, you have multicollinearity issues.

Problem 2: Convexity Check Using the Hessian [Mid-Term Q1a]

Question: Determine whether the function $f(x_1, x_2) = x_1^2 + x_2^2$ is convex. Use the Hessian matrix criterion and explain what convexity implies about optimization.

Recall: A twice-differentiable function is convex if and only if its Hessian matrix $H$ is positive semi-definite (PSD) everywhere, i.e., all eigenvalues $\geq 0$. If all eigenvalues are strictly $> 0$, the function is strictly convex (has a unique global minimum).

Step 1: Compute the Hessian (matrix of second partial derivatives)

$$H = \begin{bmatrix} \frac{\partial^2 f}{\partial x_1^2} & \frac{\partial^2 f}{\partial x_1 \partial x_2} \\ \frac{\partial^2 f}{\partial x_2 \partial x_1} & \frac{\partial^2 f}{\partial x_2^2} \end{bmatrix} = \begin{bmatrix} 2 & 0 \\ 0 & 2 \end{bmatrix} = 2I$$

Step 2: Check positive definiteness (find eigenvalues)

For a diagonal matrix, the eigenvalues are the diagonal entries: $\lambda_1 = 2 > 0$, $\lambda_2 = 2 > 0$.

Both eigenvalues are strictly positive → Hessian is positive definite everywhere.

Step 3: Conclude

$f(x_1, x_2) = x_1^2 + x_2^2$ is strictly convex. $\checkmark$

What this means for optimization:

  • There exists a unique global minimum (at the origin $(0,0)$ where $f = 0$)
  • Any local minimum IS the global minimum (no "traps")
  • Gradient descent is guaranteed to converge to the global optimum

Why this matters in ML: If your loss function is convex (e.g., MSE for linear regression, log-loss for logistic regression), gradient descent will find the optimal parameters. Non-convex losses (e.g., neural networks) may get stuck in local minima.

Problem 3: Bayes' Theorem – Red Ball from Two Boxes [Quiz Q2]

Question: Two boxes are available. Box 1 contains 5 red and 3 blue balls. Box 2 contains 2 red and 6 blue balls. A box is chosen at random (each equally likely), and a ball is drawn. The ball turns out to be red. Using Bayes' theorem, find the posterior probability that the ball came from Box 1: $P(\text{Box 1} | \text{Red})$.

Recall: Bayes' theorem: $P(A|B) = \frac{P(B|A) \cdot P(A)}{P(B)}$. In ML terms: posterior = (likelihood × prior) / evidence. This is the fundamental formula behind Bayesian classification.

Step 1: Identify the prior probabilities

$$P(B_1) = P(B_2) = \frac{1}{2} \quad \text{(box chosen at random)}$$

Step 2: Compute the likelihoods (probability of drawing red from each box)

$$P(\text{Red}|B_1) = \frac{5}{8} \quad \text{(5 red out of 8 total in Box 1)}$$ $$P(\text{Red}|B_2) = \frac{2}{8} = \frac{1}{4} \quad \text{(2 red out of 8 total in Box 2)}$$

Step 3: Compute the total probability of red (evidence/marginal)

$$P(\text{Red}) = P(\text{Red}|B_1)P(B_1) + P(\text{Red}|B_2)P(B_2) = \frac{5}{8}\cdot\frac{1}{2} + \frac{2}{8}\cdot\frac{1}{2} = \frac{5}{16} + \frac{2}{16} = \frac{7}{16}$$

Step 4: Apply Bayes' theorem

$$P(B_1|\text{Red}) = \frac{P(\text{Red}|B_1) \cdot P(B_1)}{P(\text{Red})} = \frac{\frac{5}{16}}{\frac{7}{16}} = \boxed{\frac{5}{7} \approx 0.714}$$

Interpretation: Before seeing the ball color, each box was equally likely (prior = 0.5). After observing a red ball, Box 1 becomes MORE likely (posterior = 5/7 ≈ 0.714) because Box 1 has a higher proportion of red balls. The evidence updated our belief — this is exactly how Bayesian classifiers work!

Problem 4: Coin Toss Game [Quiz Q1]

Question: Player A has $n+1$ fair coins, B has $n$ fair coins. Probability A gets more heads than B?

Answer: $\boxed{P = \frac{1}{2}}$

Elegant proof: Let A flip $n+1$ coins, B flip $n$ coins. Consider A's first $n$ coins vs B's $n$ coins. By symmetry, $P(\text{A's first } n > B) = P(B > \text{A's first } n)$.

A's $(n+1)$th coin is independent. When the first $n$ are tied (probability = some $p_{\text{tie}}$), the extra coin gives A the advantage with prob $1/2$.

By a symmetry argument: $P(A > B) = P(A_{\text{tails}} > B_{\text{heads}})$ where $A_{\text{tails}}$ counts tails in A's coins. Since $P(A_{\text{heads}} > B) + P(A_{\text{tails}} > B) = 1$ (they can't both get more heads than B's fixed outcome), and by symmetry these are equal, each = $1/2$.

Problem 5: Expected Value – Dice Game [Quiz Q20 / Practice]

Question: Two fair dice. Win ₹10 if sum ≥ 9, lose ₹5 otherwise. Find expected value.

Count winning outcomes (sum ≥ 9):

  • Sum = 9: (3,6),(4,5),(5,4),(6,3) → 4 ways
  • Sum = 10: (4,6),(5,5),(6,4) → 3 ways
  • Sum = 11: (5,6),(6,5) → 2 ways
  • Sum = 12: (6,6) → 1 way

Total winning = 10, Total outcomes = 36

$$P(\text{win}) = \frac{10}{36} = \frac{5}{18}, \quad P(\text{lose}) = \frac{26}{36} = \frac{13}{18}$$ $$E[\text{game}] = 10 \cdot \frac{5}{18} + (-5) \cdot \frac{13}{18} = \frac{50 - 65}{18} = -\frac{15}{18} = -\frac{5}{6} \approx -0.83$$

Conclusion: Expected value ≈ −₹0.83 per game → unfavorable game. Don't play.

Problem 6: Expected Value of the Sum of Two Dice [Mid-Term Q5b]

Question: Two fair six-sided dice are rolled. What is the expected value of their sum? Use the linearity of expectation property.

Recall: The linearity of expectation states that $E[X + Y] = E[X] + E[Y]$ ALWAYS, regardless of whether $X$ and $Y$ are independent or not. This is one of the most powerful properties in probability.

Step 1: Find the expected value of a single fair die

$$E[X] = \frac{1}{6}(1 + 2 + 3 + 4 + 5 + 6) = \frac{21}{6} = 3.5$$

Step 2: Apply linearity of expectation

$$E[X + Y] = E[X] + E[Y] = 3.5 + 3.5 = \boxed{7}$$

Key insight: We did NOT need to enumerate all 36 possible outcomes and compute the weighted average! Linearity of expectation lets us break a complex expected value into simpler parts. This property holds even if the dice were correlated (e.g., the second die must match the first) — the expected sum would still be 7.

Problem 7: Probability of Losing 5 Consecutive Coin Tosses [Mid-Term Q5a]

Question: A fair coin is tossed 5 times. What is the probability of getting tails ("losing") on ALL 5 tosses?

Recall: For independent events, the probability of ALL events occurring equals the product of their individual probabilities: $P(A_1 \cap A_2 \cap \ldots \cap A_n) = P(A_1) \cdot P(A_2) \cdots P(A_n)$.

Step 1: Identify the probability of losing a single toss

$$P(\text{tails on one toss}) = \frac{1}{2}$$

Step 2: Since tosses are independent, multiply

$$P(\text{5 tails in a row}) = \left(\frac{1}{2}\right)^5 = \frac{1}{32} = \boxed{0.03125}$$

Interpretation: There's about a 3.1% chance of losing 5 times in a row. This means it WILL happen roughly once every 32 attempts — it's unlikely but not rare.

Problem 8: Computing the t-statistic for Hypothesis Testing [Quiz Q17]

Question: In a linear regression model, a coefficient is estimated as $\hat{\beta}_j = 2.5$ with standard error $SE(\hat{\beta}_j) = 0.5$. Compute the t-statistic and determine whether this feature is statistically significant at the 5% level.

Recall: The t-statistic tests the null hypothesis $H_0: \beta_j = 0$ (feature has no effect). It measures how many standard errors the estimate is away from zero: $t_j = \frac{\hat{\beta}_j - 0}{SE(\hat{\beta}_j)}$. A large $|t|$ means the coefficient is unlikely to be zero by chance.

Step 1: Compute the t-statistic

$$t_j = \frac{\hat{\beta}_j}{SE(\hat{\beta}_j)} = \frac{2.5}{0.5} = \boxed{5}$$

Step 2: Compare to critical value for significance

At the 5% significance level (two-tailed), the critical value is approximately $|t| > 2$ (for large $n$, from the normal approximation). Since $|t| = 5 \gg 2$, we reject $H_0$.

Conclusion: This feature is statistically significant — its coefficient is very unlikely to be zero. The estimate is 5 standard errors away from zero, corresponding to a p-value $< 0.001$. We have strong evidence this feature has a real effect on the response.

Problem 9: Unbiased Estimate of Noise Variance [Quiz Q16]

Question: A linear regression model with $p = 5$ parameters is fit to $n = 50$ observations, yielding $\text{RSS} = 200$. Estimate the noise variance $\hat{\sigma}^2$ using the unbiased estimator. Why is the denominator $n-p$ rather than $n$?

Recall: In the linear model $y = X\beta + \epsilon$ with $\epsilon_i \sim N(0, \sigma^2)$, we estimate $\sigma^2$ from the residuals. The unbiased estimator divides RSS by the degrees of freedom $n - p$ (not $n$), where $p$ = number of estimated parameters.

Step 1: Apply the formula

$$\hat{\sigma}^2 = \frac{\text{RSS}}{n - p} = \frac{200}{50 - 5} = \frac{200}{45} = \boxed{4.44}$$

Step 2: Why $n - p$ (degrees of freedom)?

We "used up" $p = 5$ degrees of freedom fitting the model (estimating 5 parameters from data). The residuals satisfy $p$ constraints ($X^T\mathbf{r} = 0$), so only $n - p = 45$ of them are truly free. Dividing by $n$ would underestimate $\sigma^2$ because the fitted model absorbs some noise as "signal."

Why this matters: $\hat{\sigma}^2$ is used to compute standard errors of coefficients ($SE(\hat{\beta}_j)$), confidence intervals, and t-statistics. An underestimate leads to overconfident statistical conclusions.

Problem 10: Bootstrap Variance Estimation [Quiz Q18]

Question: You perform bootstrap resampling and obtain 3 bootstrap estimates of a statistic: $\{2, 4, 6\}$. Compute the bootstrap estimate of variance. This tells you how uncertain your original estimate is.

Recall: Bootstrap variance estimates the uncertainty of a statistic by resampling. Given $B$ bootstrap estimates $\hat{\theta}_1^*, \ldots, \hat{\theta}_B^*$, the bootstrap variance is: $\text{Var}_{\text{boot}} = \frac{1}{B-1}\sum_{b=1}^B (\hat{\theta}_b^* - \bar{\theta}^*)^2$ (sample variance of the bootstrap estimates).

Step 1: Compute the mean of bootstrap estimates

$$\bar{\theta}^* = \frac{2 + 4 + 6}{3} = 4$$

Step 2: Compute the sample variance

$$\text{Var}_{\text{boot}} = \frac{1}{B-1}\sum_{b=1}^B(\hat{\theta}_b^* - \bar{\theta}^*)^2 = \frac{(2-4)^2 + (4-4)^2 + (6-4)^2}{3-1} = \frac{4 + 0 + 4}{2} = \boxed{4}$$

Interpretation: The bootstrap variance is 4, giving a standard error of $\sqrt{4} = 2$. This means our statistic has an estimated uncertainty of ±2 standard deviations around its central value.

Note: Some formulations use $\frac{1}{B}$ instead of $\frac{1}{B-1}$ (population vs sample variance). With $\frac{1}{B}$: $\frac{8}{3} \approx 2.67$. The $\frac{1}{B-1}$ version (Bessel's correction) is unbiased.

Problem 11: Variance Formula Equivalence [Quiz Q11]

Question: Dataset $\{3,7,5,3,5,5,7,3,7,5,7,3,5,7,3\}$. Compare $A = \frac{1}{N}\sum(x_i - \bar{x})^2$ vs $B = \frac{1}{N}\sum x_i^2 - \bar{x}^2$. Find $A - B$.

Proof they're identical:

$$A = \frac{1}{N}\sum(x_i - \bar{x})^2 = \frac{1}{N}\sum(x_i^2 - 2x_i\bar{x} + \bar{x}^2)$$ $$= \frac{1}{N}\sum x_i^2 - 2\bar{x}\cdot\frac{1}{N}\sum x_i + \bar{x}^2 = \frac{1}{N}\sum x_i^2 - 2\bar{x}^2 + \bar{x}^2 = \frac{1}{N}\sum x_i^2 - \bar{x}^2 = B$$

Answer: $A - B = \boxed{0}$

Problem 12: Random Walk [Practice Q4]

Question: Person at $x=1$, cliff at $x=0$. Moves right with prob $p$, left with $q = 1-p$.

(a) Symmetric ($p = 1/2$): Probability of eventually falling = 1. In a symmetric 1D random walk with an absorbing barrier, the walker is guaranteed to eventually reach the barrier. This is a classical result from gambler's ruin.

(b) Increasing $p$ (bias rightward): Survival probability increases. With drift away from cliff, the expected position moves rightward over time. The probability of ruin from position $i$ is $(q/p)^i < 1$ when $p > 1/2$.

(c) Starting at $x = i$ instead of $x = 1$: Further from cliff → lower ruin probability. Specifically, $P(\text{ruin}) = (q/p)^i$ for $p > 1/2$. Since $q/p < 1$, raising to higher power $i$ makes it smaller.

Problem 13: Biased Coin [Practice Q1]

Question: Biased coin tossed twice. $P(H) = p$, $P(T) = q = 1-p$. Find $P(\text{exactly 1H, 1T})$.

$$P(\text{exactly 1H, 1T}) = P(HT) + P(TH) = pq + qp = 2pq = 2p(1-p)$$

Answer: $\boxed{2p(1-p)}$

Note: This is maximized when $p = 1/2$, giving $2(1/4) = 1/2$.

Problem 14: Dice Game with Re-roll [Practice Bonus Q2]

Question: Roll fair die. If 1-5, receive that many dollars. If 6, receive $5 and roll again. Find expected value.

Let $E$ = expected value of the game.

$$E = \frac{1}{6}(1 + 2 + 3 + 4 + 5) + \frac{1}{6}(5 + E)$$ $$E = \frac{15}{6} + \frac{5}{6} + \frac{E}{6}$$ $$E - \frac{E}{6} = \frac{20}{6}$$ $$\frac{5E}{6} = \frac{20}{6}$$ $$E = \boxed{4}$$

Entry fee $5 > E = 4$, so don't play (negative expected profit).

Created by Kushal Ghosh (gkushalg01@gmail.com)

Topic 3: Linear Regression

Covers: Simple & Multiple Linear Regression, OLS Derivation, Normal Equations, Geometric Interpretation (Projection), R² & Adjusted R², Residual Properties, Statistical Inference (t-test, F-test), Polynomial Regression, Effect of Outliers, Feature Scaling on Coefficients

What is Linear Regression? It's the simplest (and most important!) way to model the relationship between variables. Given data like (house size, price), linear regression finds the best-fit line (or hyperplane in higher dimensions) through your data. "Best" means the line that minimizes the total squared distance between the line and your data points.

Analogy: Imagine placing a ruler on a scatterplot and rotating it until it "fits" the points as closely as possible. Linear regression is the mathematical way to find that perfect ruler position.

1. Basic Understanding

1.1 Model

In plain English: We assume the output is a weighted sum of inputs, plus some random noise:

price ≈ (weight₁ × size) + (weight₂ × bedrooms) + (weight₃ × location) + baseline + noise

Our job: find those weights (called $\beta$) that best explain the data.

Simple Linear Regression: $y = \beta_0 + \beta_1 x + \epsilon$

Multiple Linear Regression: $\mathbf{y} = X\boldsymbol{\beta} + \boldsymbol{\epsilon}$

where $X \in \mathbb{R}^{n \times p}$ (design matrix with intercept column), $\boldsymbol{\beta} \in \mathbb{R}^p$, $\boldsymbol{\epsilon} \sim N(\mathbf{0}, \sigma^2 I)$

Assumptions:

  1. Linearity: $E[y|X] = X\beta$
  2. Independence: Observations are independent
  3. Homoscedasticity: $\text{Var}(\epsilon_i) = \sigma^2$ (constant)
  4. Normality (for inference): $\epsilon_i \sim N(0, \sigma^2)$
  5. No perfect multicollinearity: $\text{rank}(X) = p$

1.2 OLS Derivation

What is OLS (Ordinary Least Squares)? The method for finding the best weights. "Least Squares" means we minimize the sum of squared errors — the gap between what we predicted and what actually happened. Why squared? Because (1) it penalizes big mistakes more than small ones, and (2) it gives us a nice smooth bowl to optimize (convex!).

Objective: Minimize RSS (Residual Sum of Squares):

$$\hat{\boldsymbol{\beta}} = \arg\min_{\boldsymbol{\beta}} \|\mathbf{y} - X\boldsymbol{\beta}\|^2 = \arg\min_{\boldsymbol{\beta}} (\mathbf{y} - X\boldsymbol{\beta})^T(\mathbf{y} - X\boldsymbol{\beta})$$

Expand:

$$RSS(\boldsymbol{\beta}) = \mathbf{y}^T\mathbf{y} - 2\boldsymbol{\beta}^TX^T\mathbf{y} + \boldsymbol{\beta}^TX^TX\boldsymbol{\beta}$$

Take gradient and set to zero:

$$\nabla_\beta RSS = -2X^T\mathbf{y} + 2X^TX\boldsymbol{\beta} = 0$$

Normal Equations:

$$\boxed{X^TX\hat{\boldsymbol{\beta}} = X^T\mathbf{y}}$$

Closed-form solution (if $X^TX$ invertible):

$$\boxed{\hat{\boldsymbol{\beta}} = (X^TX)^{-1}X^T\mathbf{y}}$$

1.3 Simple Linear Regression Formulas

For $y = \beta_0 + \beta_1 x$:

$$\hat{\beta}_1 = \frac{\sum_{i=1}^n (x_i - \bar{x})(y_i - \bar{y})}{\sum_{i=1}^n (x_i - \bar{x})^2} = \frac{S_{xy}}{S_{xx}}$$ $$\hat{\beta}_0 = \bar{y} - \hat{\beta}_1 \bar{x}$$

The regression line always passes through $(\bar{x}, \bar{y})$.

1.4 Geometric Interpretation

Intuition: Your data vector $\mathbf{y}$ lives in $n$-dimensional space. The column space of $X$ is a flat surface (subspace) that represents all possible predictions. OLS finds the point on that surface closest to $\mathbf{y}$ — which is the orthogonal projection! The residual $\mathbf{r}$ is the perpendicular gap from $\mathbf{y}$ to the surface.

Analogy: Imagine shining a flashlight straight down on a flying bird ($\mathbf{y}$) onto a flat floor (column space). The shadow ($\hat{\mathbf{y}}$) is the OLS prediction. The height of the bird above its shadow is the residual.

OLS = Orthogonal projection of $\mathbf{y}$ onto the column space of $X$

$$\hat{\mathbf{y}} = X\hat{\boldsymbol{\beta}} = X(X^TX)^{-1}X^T\mathbf{y} = H\mathbf{y}$$

where $H = X(X^TX)^{-1}X^T$ is the hat matrix (idempotent: $H^2 = H$, symmetric)

Residual vector:

$$\mathbf{r} = \mathbf{y} - \hat{\mathbf{y}} = (I - H)\mathbf{y}$$

Key property: $\mathbf{r} \perp \text{Col}(X)$, i.e., $X^T\mathbf{r} = 0$

This means the residual is orthogonal to every column of $X$ (including the intercept column → $\sum r_i = 0$).

1.5 R² (Coefficient of Determination)

What is R²? It's a "report card" for your model. R² tells you what fraction of the variation in your data is explained by the model. R² = 0.85 means "my model explains 85% of why $y$ varies." The remaining 15% is noise or patterns we missed.

Analogy: If weather explains 70% of ice cream sales (R²=0.7), the remaining 30% comes from other factors (promotions, events, randomness).

$$R^2 = 1 - \frac{RSS}{TSS} = 1 - \frac{\sum(y_i - \hat{y}_i)^2}{\sum(y_i - \bar{y})^2}$$

where:

  • $TSS = \sum(y_i - \bar{y})^2$ (Total Sum of Squares — total variability)
  • $RSS = \sum(y_i - \hat{y}_i)^2$ (Residual Sum of Squares — unexplained)
  • $ESS = TSS - RSS$ (Explained Sum of Squares)

Interpretation: $R^2 = 0.85$ means 85% of variance in $y$ is explained by the model.

Range: $0 \leq R^2 \leq 1$ (for OLS with intercept). Higher is better.

Problem: $R^2$ never decreases when adding features (even useless ones).

Adjusted R²:

$$R^2_{\text{adj}} = 1 - \frac{RSS/(n-p)}{TSS/(n-1)} = 1 - \frac{n-1}{n-p}(1 - R^2)$$

Penalizes adding features that don't improve fit proportional to their cost.

1.6 Statistical Inference for Regression

Distribution of $\hat{\beta}$:

$$\hat{\boldsymbol{\beta}} \sim N(\boldsymbol{\beta}, \sigma^2(X^TX)^{-1})$$

Standard Error: $SE(\hat{\beta}_j) = \hat{\sigma}\sqrt{(X^TX)^{-1}_{jj}}$

t-test for individual coefficient:

$$t_j = \frac{\hat{\beta}_j}{SE(\hat{\beta}_j)} \sim t_{n-p} \quad \text{under } H_0: \beta_j = 0$$

F-test for overall model significance:

$$F = \frac{(TSS - RSS)/p}{RSS/(n-p-1)} \sim F_{p, n-p-1} \quad \text{under } H_0: \text{all } \beta_j = 0$$

Unbiased noise variance:

$$\hat{\sigma}^2 = \frac{RSS}{n-p}$$

1.7 Polynomial Regression

$$y = \beta_0 + \beta_1 x + \beta_2 x^2 + \ldots + \beta_d x^d + \epsilon$$

This is still LINEAR regression (linear in parameters $\beta$), just with transformed features $x, x^2, \ldots, x^d$.

As degree $d$ increases:

  • Training error: Monotonically decreases (more flexibility)
  • Validation/Test error: U-shaped (initially decreases, then increases due to overfitting)
  • Bias: Decreases
  • Variance: Increases

Model Selection: Choose degree where validation error is minimized.

1.8 Effect of High-Leverage Outliers

Leverage: $h_{ii} = H_{ii}$ (diagonal of hat matrix). High leverage = far from $\bar{x}$.

Influence: High leverage + large residual = influential point.

Cook's Distance: Measures change in $\hat{\beta}$ when point $i$ removed:

$$D_i = \frac{(\hat{\mathbf{y}} - \hat{\mathbf{y}}_{(i)})^T(\hat{\mathbf{y}} - \hat{\mathbf{y}}_{(i)})}{p \cdot \hat{\sigma}^2} = \frac{r_i^2}{p} \cdot \frac{h_{ii}}{(1-h_{ii})^2}$$

Effect of adding a high-leverage outlier:

  • Slope changes significantly (pulled toward outlier)
  • Intercept shifts
  • $R^2$ likely decreases (more unexplained variation)

1.9 Feature Scaling in OLS

If feature $x_j$ is scaled by factor $c$ (i.e., $x_j' = cx_j$):

  • New coefficient: $\hat{\beta}_j' = \hat{\beta}_j / c$
  • Predictions: Unchanged ($\hat{\beta}_j' \cdot x_j' = \hat{\beta}_j \cdot x_j$)
  • RSS: Unchanged
  • $R^2$: Unchanged

Key insight: OLS predictions are invariant to feature scaling. BUT coefficients change, so their magnitudes are NOT directly comparable across differently-scaled features.

For regularization (Ridge/LASSO): Standardize FIRST because penalty $\|\beta\|$ treats all coefficients equally.

2. When to Use

Use Linear Regression When:

  • Relationship between features and response is approximately linear
  • Need interpretable coefficients ($\beta_j$ = effect of $x_j$ on $y$)
  • Prediction of continuous outcome
  • Small dataset (few parameters to estimate)
  • Baseline model for comparison

Don't Use When:

  • Relationship is highly nonlinear (consider polynomial, trees, KNN)
  • Response is categorical (use logistic regression, SVM)
  • Many outliers (consider robust regression, MAE loss)
  • Multicollinearity (use Ridge, PCA regression)
  • $p > n$ (use regularized methods: Ridge, LASSO)

3. All Formulae

OLS Solution

$$\hat{\boldsymbol{\beta}} = (X^TX)^{-1}X^T\mathbf{y}$$ $$\hat{\mathbf{y}} = X\hat{\boldsymbol{\beta}} = H\mathbf{y}, \quad H = X(X^TX)^{-1}X^T$$ $$\mathbf{r} = \mathbf{y} - \hat{\mathbf{y}} = (I-H)\mathbf{y}, \quad X^T\mathbf{r} = 0$$

Simple Linear Regression

$$\hat{\beta}_1 = \frac{\sum(x_i - \bar{x})(y_i - \bar{y})}{\sum(x_i - \bar{x})^2} = \frac{S_{xy}}{S_{xx}} = r_{xy}\frac{s_y}{s_x}$$ $$\hat{\beta}_0 = \bar{y} - \hat{\beta}_1\bar{x}$$

Gradient of OLS Loss

$$L(\boldsymbol{\beta}) = \frac{1}{2}\|X\boldsymbol{\beta} - \mathbf{y}\|^2$$ $$\nabla_\beta L = X^T(X\boldsymbol{\beta} - \mathbf{y})$$

Setting to zero gives normal equations.

Goodness of Fit

$$TSS = \sum(y_i - \bar{y})^2, \quad RSS = \sum(y_i - \hat{y}_i)^2, \quad ESS = TSS - RSS$$ $$R^2 = 1 - \frac{RSS}{TSS} = \frac{ESS}{TSS}$$ $$R^2_{\text{adj}} = 1 - \frac{RSS/(n-p)}{TSS/(n-1)}$$

Inference

$$\hat{\sigma}^2 = \frac{RSS}{n-p}, \quad SE(\hat{\beta}_j) = \hat{\sigma}\sqrt{(X^TX)^{-1}_{jj}}$$ $$t_j = \frac{\hat{\beta}_j}{SE(\hat{\beta}_j)}, \quad \text{CI: } \hat{\beta}_j \pm t_{\alpha/2,n-p} \cdot SE(\hat{\beta}_j)$$ $$F = \frac{(TSS-RSS)/p}{RSS/(n-p-1)}$$

Leverage and Influence

$$h_{ii} = [H]_{ii}, \quad \sum h_{ii} = p \quad \text{(trace of H)}$$ $$\text{Average leverage} = p/n$$ $$D_i = \frac{r_i^2}{p\hat{\sigma}^2} \cdot \frac{h_{ii}}{(1-h_{ii})^2} \quad \text{(Cook's distance)}$$

MLE Connection

If $\epsilon_i \sim N(0, \sigma^2)$:

$$\hat{\beta}_{MLE} = \hat{\beta}_{OLS} = (X^TX)^{-1}X^Ty$$ $$\hat{\sigma}^2_{MLE} = \frac{RSS}{n} \quad \text{(biased, vs unbiased } \frac{RSS}{n-p}\text{)}$$

4. Best Practices

  • Check assumptions: Plot residuals vs fitted values (look for patterns, heteroscedasticity)
  • Residual normality: QQ plot of residuals for valid inference
  • Multicollinearity: Check VIF (Variance Inflation Factor). VIF > 10 is problematic.
  • Influential points: Check Cook's distance. $D_i > 1$ is highly influential.
  • Don't extrapolate: Predictions outside training range are unreliable
  • Use adjusted R²: For comparing models with different numbers of features
  • Standardize for comparison: Standardize features to compare coefficient magnitudes
Common Mistakes:
  • Using $R^2$ alone to compare models with different numbers of features
  • Interpreting correlation as causation
  • Ignoring multicollinearity (inflated standard errors)
  • Predicting outside training range (extrapolation)
  • Using $n$ instead of $n-p$ for $\hat{\sigma}^2$
  • Forgetting that OLS is the MLE under Gaussian noise assumption

5. Example Problems (Solved Exam Questions)

Problem 1: Least Squares Regression [Quiz Q5 / Mid-Term Q6]

Question: Given three data points $(1,2), (2,5), (3,4)$, find the best-fit line $y = wx + b$ using the Ordinary Least Squares (OLS) method. The OLS method finds the slope $w$ and intercept $b$ that minimize the sum of squared residuals $\text{RSS} = \sum_{i=1}^n (y_i - wx_i - b)^2$.

Recall: The OLS formulas for simple linear regression are: $w = \frac{\sum(x_i - \bar{x})(y_i - \bar{y})}{\sum(x_i - \bar{x})^2}$ and $b = \bar{y} - w\bar{x}$. This gives the UNIQUE line that minimizes the total squared vertical distance from points to the line.

Step 1: Compute the sample means $\bar{x}$ and $\bar{y}$:

$$\bar{x} = \frac{1+2+3}{3} = 2, \quad \bar{y} = \frac{2+5+4}{3} = \frac{11}{3} \approx 3.67$$

Step 2: Compute slope:

$$w = \frac{\sum(x_i - \bar{x})(y_i - \bar{y})}{\sum(x_i - \bar{x})^2}$$ $$\text{Numerator: } (1-2)(2-\frac{11}{3}) + (2-2)(5-\frac{11}{3}) + (3-2)(4-\frac{11}{3})$$ $$= (-1)(-\frac{5}{3}) + 0 + (1)(\frac{1}{3}) = \frac{5}{3} + \frac{1}{3} = 2$$ $$\text{Denominator: } (1-2)^2 + (2-2)^2 + (3-2)^2 = 1 + 0 + 1 = 2$$ $$w = \frac{2}{2} = 1$$

Step 3: Compute intercept:

$$b = \bar{y} - w\bar{x} = \frac{11}{3} - 1 \cdot 2 = \frac{11}{3} - 2 = \frac{5}{3}$$

Answer: $\boxed{y = x + \frac{5}{3}}$

Problem 2: Linear Regression from Data [Mid-Term Q6]

Question: Hours studied vs exam score: $(1,40), (3,65), (5,75)$. (a) Derive the linear regression model. (b) Predict the exam score of a student who studied for 0 hours.

(a) Derive the model:

$$\bar{x} = \frac{1+3+5}{3} = 3, \quad \bar{y} = \frac{40+65+75}{3} = 60$$ $$w = \frac{(1-3)(40-60) + (3-3)(65-60) + (5-3)(75-60)}{(1-3)^2 + (3-3)^2 + (5-3)^2}$$ $$= \frac{(-2)(-20) + 0 + (2)(15)}{4 + 0 + 4} = \frac{40 + 30}{8} = \frac{70}{8} = 8.75$$ $$b = 60 - 8.75(3) = 60 - 26.25 = 33.75$$

Model: $\boxed{y = 8.75x + 33.75}$

Interpretation: Each additional hour of study increases expected score by 8.75 points.

(b) Prediction for 0 hours ($x = 0$):

$$y = 8.75(0) + 33.75 = \boxed{33.75}$$

The intercept represents the predicted score with zero study hours. Note: extrapolating beyond the data range ($x \in [1,5]$) may be unreliable.

Problem 3: R² (Coefficient of Determination) Computation [Quiz Q14]

Question: A linear regression model is fit to data. The Residual Sum of Squares is $\text{RSS} = 120$ and the Total Sum of Squares is $\text{TSS} = 300$. Compute the coefficient of determination $R^2$ and interpret what it means about model fit quality.

Recall: $R^2 = 1 - \frac{\text{RSS}}{\text{TSS}}$ where RSS = $\sum(y_i - \hat{y}_i)^2$ (unexplained variance) and TSS = $\sum(y_i - \bar{y})^2$ (total variance). $R^2$ ranges from 0 (model explains nothing) to 1 (model explains everything).

Step 1: Apply the formula

$$R^2 = 1 - \frac{\text{RSS}}{\text{TSS}} = 1 - \frac{120}{300} = 1 - 0.4 = \boxed{0.6}$$

Step 2: Interpret the result

$R^2 = 0.6$ means 60% of the total variance in the response variable $y$ is explained by the regression model. The remaining 40% is unexplained (captured in the residuals). This is a moderately good fit — better than nothing but there's substantial unexplained variation.

Problem 4: Geometric Interpretation of OLS [Quiz Q12]

Question: In linear regression, the fitted values $\hat{\mathbf{y}} = X\hat{\beta}$ form a vector in $\mathbb{R}^n$. What is the geometric interpretation of $\hat{\mathbf{y}}$ in relation to the observation vector $\mathbf{y}$ and the column space of the design matrix $X$?

Recall: The column space of $X$, written $\text{Col}(X)$, is the set of all possible vectors $X\beta$ as $\beta$ varies. OLS finds the $\beta$ such that $X\beta$ is as close to $\mathbf{y}$ as possible (minimizes $\|\mathbf{y} - X\beta\|^2$).

Answer: $\hat{\mathbf{y}} = X\hat{\beta}$ is the orthogonal projection of the response vector $\mathbf{y}$ onto the column space of $X$.

What this means geometrically:

  • $\hat{\mathbf{y}}$ is the point in $\text{Col}(X)$ that is closest to $\mathbf{y}$ in Euclidean distance
  • The residual vector $\mathbf{r} = \mathbf{y} - \hat{\mathbf{y}}$ is perpendicular (orthogonal) to the entire column space of $X$
