  • Think of it like dropping a perpendicular from $\mathbf{y}$ onto a flat surface (the column space) — the foot of the perpendicular is $\hat{\mathbf{y}}$

Why this matters: This explains why OLS is sometimes called "projection regression" — it projects the data onto the space of possible predictions.

Problem 5: Residual Orthogonality Property [Quiz Q15]

Question: In OLS linear regression, what is the key geometric property of the residual vector $\mathbf{r} = \mathbf{y} - \hat{\mathbf{y}}$? Prove it algebraically.

Answer: The residual vector $\mathbf{r} = \mathbf{y} - \hat{\mathbf{y}}$ is orthogonal to every column of $X$ (i.e., orthogonal to the entire column space of $X$).

Algebraic proof (step-by-step):

We want to show $X^T\mathbf{r} = \mathbf{0}$:

$$X^T\mathbf{r} = X^T(\mathbf{y} - X\hat{\beta})$$

Substitute $\hat{\beta} = (X^TX)^{-1}X^T\mathbf{y}$:

$$= X^T\mathbf{y} - X^TX \cdot (X^TX)^{-1}X^T\mathbf{y}$$ $$= X^T\mathbf{y} - I \cdot X^T\mathbf{y} = X^T\mathbf{y} - X^T\mathbf{y} = \mathbf{0} \quad \checkmark$$

Practical consequence: Since the vector $\mathbf{1}$ (all ones) is a column of $X$ (the intercept column), we get $\mathbf{1}^T\mathbf{r} = 0$, meaning residuals always sum to zero in OLS with an intercept.

Intuition: The residuals are "pure noise" — they contain no information that could be linearly extracted by any feature in $X$. If they weren't orthogonal to some column of $X$, we could use that correlation to improve the fit further.

Problem 6: MLE Equals OLS Under Gaussian Noise [Quiz Q13]

Question: In linear regression $y_i = x_i^T\beta + \epsilon_i$, if we assume the noise is Gaussian $\epsilon_i \sim N(0, \sigma^2)$, show that the Maximum Likelihood Estimate (MLE) of $\beta$ is identical to the OLS estimate. Why does this matter?

Recall: MLE finds the parameters that make the observed data most probable. OLS minimizes the sum of squared residuals. We'll show these two different motivations lead to the SAME answer.

Step 1: Write the likelihood function

Since $y_i|x_i \sim N(x_i^T\beta, \sigma^2)$ and observations are independent:

$$L(\beta) = \prod_{i=1}^n \frac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi\sigma^2}}\exp\left(-\frac{(y_i - x_i^T\beta)^2}{2\sigma^2}\right)$$

Step 2: Take the log (easier to maximize)

$$\log L(\beta) = -\frac{n}{2}\log(2\pi\sigma^2) - \frac{1}{2\sigma^2}\sum_{i=1}^n(y_i - x_i^T\beta)^2$$

Step 3: Identify what depends on $\beta$

Only the second term involves $\beta$. Since $-\frac{1}{2\sigma^2}$ is a negative constant:

$$\max_\beta \log L(\beta) \iff \min_\beta \sum_{i=1}^n(y_i - x_i^T\beta)^2 = \text{RSS}$$

Conclusion: $\hat{\beta}_{MLE} = \hat{\beta}_{OLS} = (X^TX)^{-1}X^Ty$

Why this matters: It gives OLS a probabilistic justification — if noise is truly Gaussian, OLS isn't just a convenient formula, it's the statistically optimal estimator (most efficient, unbiased).

Problem 7: Gradient of the Linear Regression Loss Function [Quiz Q9]

Question: The linear regression loss (mean squared error) in matrix form is $L(\theta) = \frac{1}{2}\|X\theta - y\|^2$. Compute the gradient $\nabla_\theta L$ and show how setting it to zero gives the Normal Equations.

Recall: The gradient tells us how to adjust $\theta$ to reduce the loss. We need it for gradient descent, and setting it to zero gives us the closed-form OLS solution.

Step 1: Expand the squared norm using $(a^Ta = \|a\|^2)$

$$L(\theta) = \frac{1}{2}(X\theta - y)^T(X\theta - y)$$ $$= \frac{1}{2}(\theta^TX^TX\theta - 2\theta^TX^Ty + y^Ty)$$

Step 2: Differentiate term by term

  • $\nabla_\theta (\theta^TX^TX\theta) = 2X^TX\theta$ (quadratic form rule: $\nabla_\theta(\theta^TA\theta) = 2A\theta$ for symmetric $A$)
  • $\nabla_\theta (2\theta^TX^Ty) = 2X^Ty$ (linear term rule)
  • $\nabla_\theta (y^Ty) = 0$ (constant)

Step 3: Combine

$$\nabla_\theta L = \frac{1}{2}(2X^TX\theta - 2X^Ty) = \boxed{X^T(X\theta - y)}$$

Step 4: Setting gradient to zero → Normal Equations

$$X^T(X\theta - y) = 0 \implies X^TX\theta = X^Ty \implies \theta^* = (X^TX)^{-1}X^Ty$$

Key insight: The gradient $X^T(X\theta - y) = X^T\mathbf{r}$ is just $X^T$ times the residual vector. Setting it to zero enforces that residuals are orthogonal to all columns of $X$ — connecting back to the geometric interpretation.

Problem 8: Effect of Outlier on Regression [Quiz Q3 / Practice]

Question: A high-leverage outlier far from the regression line is added. Effect?

  • Slope: Changes significantly — pulled toward outlier (high leverage amplifies influence)
  • Intercept: Changes to accommodate new slope
  • $R^2$: Likely decreases — the outlier adds unexplained variation (large residual relative to its contribution to TSS)

Why: OLS minimizes sum of SQUARED residuals. A point far from the line has a huge squared residual, which dominates the optimization and drags the line toward it.

Problem 9: Polynomial Regression Overfitting [Practice / Quiz Q4]

Question: Polynomial degrees 1-20 trained. Describe training/validation error behavior.

Training error: Generally decreases as degree increases (more capacity to fit data). At degree $\geq n-1$, can perfectly interpolate all points → training error = 0.

Validation error: First decreases (bias reduction dominates), then increases (variance/overfitting dominates). U-shaped curve.

Why training error decreases: Higher degree = more parameters = richer function class that includes all lower-degree solutions.

Why test error increases beyond optimal: Model fits noise in training data. High-degree polynomials oscillate wildly between data points, producing poor predictions on new data.

Identifying overfitting: Large gap between training and validation error.

Optimal degree: Minimum of validation error curve. E.g., if degree 3 has balanced train/test performance vs degree 6 with low train but high test → prefer degree 3.

Problem 10: Interpreting R² = 0.85 [Quiz Q19]

Question: A linear regression model has $R^2 = 0.85$. Provide the correct interpretation of this value, and identify common wrong interpretations that beginners often make.

Recall: $R^2 = 1 - \frac{\text{RSS}}{\text{TSS}}$ measures the proportion of variance in $y$ explained by the model, ranging from 0 (no explanatory power) to 1 (perfect fit).

Correct interpretation: "85% of the total variance in the response variable $y$ is explained by the linear relationship with the predictors. Only 15% of the variance remains unexplained."

Common WRONG interpretations (avoid these!):

  • ❌ "85% of predictions are correct" — that's classification accuracy, not regression $R^2$
  • ❌ "The correlation is 0.85" — the correlation $r = \pm\sqrt{R^2} = \pm\sqrt{0.85} \approx \pm 0.922$ (for simple regression only)
  • ❌ "The model is 85% accurate" — $R^2$ is about variance explained, not prediction accuracy
  • ❌ "The model is good" — whether $R^2 = 0.85$ is "good" depends on the domain (excellent for social science, mediocre for physics)

Problem 11: Unbiased Estimate of Noise Variance $\hat{\sigma}^2$ [Quiz Q16]

Question: A linear regression model with $p = 5$ parameters is fit to $n = 50$ data points, yielding $\text{RSS} = 200$. Compute the unbiased estimate of the noise variance $\hat{\sigma}^2$. Why do we divide by $n-p$ instead of $n$?

Recall: The noise variance $\sigma^2 = \text{Var}(\epsilon_i)$ tells us how much inherent randomness exists in the data. We estimate it from the residuals, but must correct for the degrees of freedom lost when fitting $p$ parameters.

Step 1: Apply the unbiased estimator formula

$$\hat{\sigma}^2 = \frac{\text{RSS}}{n - p} = \frac{200}{50 - 5} = \frac{200}{45} = \boxed{4.44}$$

Step 2: Why $n-p$ (degrees of freedom correction)?

We "used up" $p = 5$ degrees of freedom when fitting the model — the residuals are constrained by the $p$ normal equations ($X^T\mathbf{r} = 0$). Only $n - p = 45$ residual values are truly "free." Dividing by $n$ would systematically underestimate $\sigma^2$ (biased downward), because the fitted model always explains some noise as signal.

Analogy: It's like computing sample variance with $n-1$ instead of $n$ (Bessel's correction) — same idea, extended to regression where we lose $p$ degrees of freedom instead of 1.

Problem 12: Feature Scaling and Coefficients [Practice — Pinak]

Question: Predictor scaled by factor 2. What changes?

If $x_j' = 2x_j$, design matrix column doubles. In $y = \beta_0 + \beta_j x_j + \ldots$:

  • New: $y = \beta_0 + \beta_j'(2x_j) + \ldots$ → $\beta_j' = \beta_j/2$
  • Predictions: $\beta_j' \cdot x_j' = (\beta_j/2)(2x_j) = \beta_j x_j$ → unchanged
  • RSS, $R^2$, $\hat{\sigma}^2$: All unchanged
  • The coefficient $\hat{\beta}_j$ changes (halves)

Implication: Raw coefficient magnitudes are meaningless for comparing feature importance unless features are on the same scale. Standardize first for fair comparison.

Created by Kushal Ghosh (gkushalg01@gmail.com)

Topic 4: Model Assessment & Selection

Covers: Goodness of Fit, Training vs Test Error, Bias-Variance Decomposition, Subset Selection (Best, Forward, Backward), Cross-Validation (K-Fold, LOOCV, Stratified), Bootstrap & Bootstrap Variance, In-Sample Methods (AIC, BIC, Cp, Adjusted R²)

The Big Question: You've built a model — but how do you know if it's actually GOOD? You can't just check how well it fits the training data (that's like grading students on the exact questions they practiced). Model assessment tells you how well your model will perform on NEW, unseen data.

Analogy: Training error is like testing yourself with flashcards you already memorized. Test error is like taking the actual exam. Cross-validation is like doing practice exams on material you didn't study.

1. Basic Understanding

1.1 Training Error vs Test Error

Why can't we just use training error? Because the model was designed to minimize it! A polynomial of degree $n-1$ can perfectly fit $n$ points (zero training error) but will predict terribly on new data. Training error is like letting a student write the test answers during the test — it tells you nothing about what they actually learned.

Training error ($E_{in}$): Error on data used to fit the model.

$$E_{in} = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n L(y_i, \hat{f}(x_i))$$

Test error ($E_{out}$): Expected error on new, unseen data.

$$E_{out} = E_{(x,y) \sim P}[L(y, \hat{f}(x))]$$

Key insight: $E_{in}$ is optimistically biased — the model was optimized to minimize it. We need $E_{out}$ to assess true performance.

Optimism: $E_{out} \geq E_{in}$ (on average). The gap increases with model complexity.

1.2 Bias-Variance Decomposition (for Model Selection)

$$E_{out} = \text{Bias}^2 + \text{Variance} + \text{Irreducible Noise}$$

Model complexity tradeoff:

  • Too simple → high bias, low variance (underfitting)
  • Too complex → low bias, high variance (overfitting)
  • Optimal → best tradeoff where $E_{out}$ is minimized

Model selection goal: Find complexity level (e.g., number of features, polynomial degree, regularization strength) that minimizes estimated $E_{out}$.

1.3 Subset Selection

Best Subset Selection

Fit all $2^p$ possible models (every combination of features). Select best by CV or information criterion.

Computationally infeasible for $p > 20-30$.

Forward Stepwise Selection

  1. Start with null model (intercept only)
  2. At each step, add the feature that most improves the fit
  3. Continue until stopping criterion (e.g., no significant improvement)

Complexity: $O(p^2)$ models to fit. Greedy — not guaranteed to find global best.

Backward Stepwise Selection

  1. Start with full model (all $p$ features)
  2. At each step, remove the least useful feature
  3. Continue until stopping criterion

Requires $n > p$ (need full model to be fittable).

1.4 Cross-Validation

What is Cross-Validation? A clever way to estimate test error WITHOUT needing a separate test set. The idea: take turns holding out parts of your data, train on the rest, and test on the held-out part. It's like a group study session where each person takes a turn being the "quiz master" while others study.

K-Fold Cross-Validation

  1. Split data into $K$ equal folds
  2. For each fold $k = 1, \ldots, K$:
    • Train on all data except fold $k$
    • Test on fold $k$
  3. Average the $K$ test errors
$$CV_K = \frac{1}{K}\sum_{k=1}^K E_k = \frac{1}{K}\sum_{k=1}^K \frac{1}{|S_k|}\sum_{i \in S_k} L(y_i, \hat{f}^{(-k)}(x_i))$$

Typical: $K = 5$ or $K = 10$. Each point is used for testing exactly once.

Leave-One-Out Cross-Validation (LOOCV)

Special case: $K = n$ (each fold has 1 point).

$$CV_n = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n L(y_i, \hat{f}^{(-i)}(x_i))$$

For linear regression (shortcut):

$$CV_n = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n \left(\frac{y_i - \hat{y}_i}{1 - h_{ii}}\right)^2$$

where $h_{ii}$ = leverage (diagonal of hat matrix). No need to refit $n$ times!

K-Fold vs LOOCV Comparison

AspectK-Fold (K=5 or 10)LOOCV (K=n)
BiasSlightly higher (trains on $(K-1)/K$ fraction)Nearly unbiased (trains on $n-1$ points)
VarianceLower (folds are more different)Higher (training sets overlap heavily)
Computation$K$ model fits$n$ model fits (expensive unless shortcut exists)
Best forLarge datasets, expensive modelsSmall datasets (maximize training data)

1.5 Bootstrap

What is Bootstrap? You only have ONE dataset, but you want to know "how much would my result change if I collected data again?" Bootstrap solves this by creating fake new datasets: you randomly draw $n$ samples WITH replacement from your data (some points appear multiple times, some are left out). Repeat this many times and see how your statistic varies.

Analogy: Imagine you have a bag of 100 marbles. You pull out 100 marbles one at a time (putting each back before drawing again). You'll get a slightly different mix each time — this simulates "collecting new data."

Idea: Estimate sampling distribution by resampling WITH replacement from the data.

  1. Draw $B$ bootstrap samples of size $n$ (with replacement)
  2. Compute statistic $\hat{\theta}_b$ on each sample
  3. Use distribution of $\{\hat{\theta}_1, \ldots, \hat{\theta}_B\}$ to estimate variability

Bootstrap Variance:

$$\widehat{\text{Var}}(\hat{\theta}) = \frac{1}{B-1}\sum_{b=1}^B (\hat{\theta}_b - \bar{\hat{\theta}})^2$$

Bootstrap Confidence Interval (percentile method):

$$[\hat{\theta}_{(\alpha/2)}, \hat{\theta}_{(1-\alpha/2)}]$$

(the $\alpha/2$ and $1-\alpha/2$ percentiles of bootstrap distribution)

Key property: Each bootstrap sample includes about 63.2% of original points (each point has prob $(1-1/n)^n \approx e^{-1} \approx 0.368$ of NOT being selected).

1.6 In-Sample Methods (Information Criteria)

What are AIC/BIC? They're "scorecards" that balance model fit vs. complexity WITHOUT needing a validation set. The idea: a model with more parameters always fits better, so we PENALIZE extra parameters. AIC/BIC = (how badly the model fits) + (penalty for complexity). Lower score = better model.

AIC vs BIC: BIC penalizes complexity MORE harshly (prefers simpler models). For large $n$, BIC chooses simpler models than AIC.

Estimate $E_{out}$ without a validation set by penalizing model complexity:

AIC (Akaike Information Criterion):

$$AIC = -2\log L(\hat{\theta}) + 2p$$

For Gaussian: $AIC = n\log(RSS/n) + 2p$ (lower is better)

BIC (Bayesian Information Criterion):

$$BIC = -2\log L(\hat{\theta}) + p\log n$$

Penalizes complexity more heavily than AIC for $n > 7$.

Mallows' Cp:

$$C_p = \frac{RSS_p}{\hat{\sigma}^2} - n + 2p$$

where $\hat{\sigma}^2$ from the full model. Good model: $C_p \approx p$.

Adjusted R²:

$$R^2_{\text{adj}} = 1 - \frac{RSS/(n-p)}{TSS/(n-1)}$$
CriterionPenaltyTends to select
AIC$2p$Slightly larger models (better prediction)
BIC$p\log n$Smaller models (consistent model selection)
Adjusted R²$(n-1)/(n-p)$ factorBalanced

2. When to Use

Cross-Validation

  • K-Fold ($K$=5-10): Default choice. Good bias-variance tradeoff for estimating $E_{out}$.
  • LOOCV: Very small datasets where losing even one fold of data for validation hurts. Also when an analytical shortcut exists (linear models).
  • Stratified K-Fold: Imbalanced classification — maintains class proportions in each fold.
  • Repeated K-Fold: Reduces variance of CV estimate by averaging over multiple random splits.

Bootstrap

  • Estimating standard errors/confidence intervals for any statistic
  • When analytical formulas don't exist or are hard to derive
  • Assessing model stability

Information Criteria

  • Comparing many models quickly (no refitting needed)
  • When computation is expensive and CV is impractical
  • AIC for prediction accuracy; BIC for model identification

3. All Formulae

Cross-Validation

$$CV_K = \frac{1}{K}\sum_{k=1}^K \frac{1}{|S_k|}\sum_{i \in S_k}(y_i - \hat{f}^{(-k)}(x_i))^2$$ $$CV_{\text{LOOCV}} = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n\left(\frac{y_i - \hat{y}_i}{1 - h_{ii}}\right)^2 \quad \text{(linear models shortcut)}$$

Bootstrap

$$\widehat{\text{Var}}(\hat{\theta}) = \frac{1}{B-1}\sum_{b=1}^B(\hat{\theta}_b - \bar{\hat{\theta}})^2$$ $$\widehat{SE}(\hat{\theta}) = \sqrt{\widehat{\text{Var}}(\hat{\theta})}$$ $$P(\text{point not in bootstrap sample}) = (1-1/n)^n \to 1/e \approx 0.368$$

Information Criteria

$$AIC = -2\log L + 2p$$ $$BIC = -2\log L + p\log n$$ $$C_p = \frac{RSS_p}{\hat{\sigma}^2} - n + 2p$$ $$R^2_{\text{adj}} = 1 - \frac{RSS/(n-p)}{TSS/(n-1)} = 1 - \frac{n-1}{n-p}(1-R^2)$$

Optimism and Effective Parameters

$$E_{out} \approx E_{in} + \frac{2d\sigma^2}{n} \quad \text{(linear models, Cp formula)}$$

where $d$ = effective number of parameters. More complex model → more optimism.

4. Best Practices

  • Use CV for model selection, test set for final evaluation. Never use the test set during model selection/tuning.
  • K=10 is standard. K=5 for large datasets or expensive models.
  • LOOCV for small datasets (n < 50-100) where K-fold loses too much training data.
  • Stratified CV for classification with imbalanced classes.
  • Bootstrap with B ≥ 1000 for stable variance estimates.
  • Report mean ± std of CV scores to indicate estimate reliability.
  • One-standard-error rule: Choose simplest model within 1 SE of the best CV score.
Common Mistakes:
  • Using training error to select models (always optimistic)
  • Data leakage: preprocessing (scaling, feature selection) before splitting → overly optimistic CV
  • Using test set for tuning then reporting test performance (selection bias)
  • Not accounting for computational cost of LOOCV for non-linear models
  • Comparing AIC/BIC across models with different response transformations

5. Example Problems (Solved Exam Questions)

Problem 1: CV Strategies [Practice — Shubhranil]

Question: (A) Small dataset, 50 samples. (B) Large dataset, 50,000 samples, expensive training. Which CV method for each?

(A) Small dataset (50 samples): LOOCV preferred.

  • With K=10 fold, each fold trains on only 45 points — significant data loss
  • LOOCV trains on 49 points each time → nearly unbiased estimate
  • With only 50 samples, computation is manageable (50 model fits)
  • Higher variance of LOOCV is acceptable given the benefit of using almost all data for training

(B) Large dataset (50,000 samples): K-Fold (K=5 or 10) preferred.

  • LOOCV requires 50,000 model fits → computationally prohibitive if training is expensive
  • K=5 → only 5 fits, each on 40,000 points (plenty of data)
  • With large $n$, even $K$-fold gives nearly unbiased estimates
  • Lower variance than LOOCV (folds are more diverse)

(C) Tradeoffs:

LOOCVK-Fold
BiasNearly zeroSlightly higher
VarianceHigh (overlapping sets)Lower
Computation$O(n)$ fits$O(K)$ fits
Training data used$n-1$ per fold$n(K-1)/K$ per fold

Problem 2: Bootstrap Variance Estimation [Quiz Q18]

Question: In bootstrap model assessment, suppose we generate $B = 3$ bootstrap samples and compute an estimate $\hat{\theta}^{*(b)}$ for each sample. The bootstrap estimate of variance is: $$\text{Var}_{\text{boot}} = \frac{1}{B}\sum_{b=1}^B (\hat{\theta}^{*(b)} - \bar{\hat{\theta}}^*)^2$$ If the bootstrap estimates are $\{2, 4, 6\}$, what is the bootstrap variance?

Recall: The bootstrap estimates uncertainty by resampling from the original data. The formula given in this course uses $\frac{1}{B}$ (population variance formula), NOT $\frac{1}{B-1}$.

Step 1: Compute the mean of bootstrap estimates

$$\bar{\hat{\theta}}^* = \frac{2+4+6}{3} = 4$$

Step 2: Compute squared deviations from the mean

  • $(\hat{\theta}_1^* - \bar{\hat{\theta}}^*)^2 = (2-4)^2 = 4$
  • $(\hat{\theta}_2^* - \bar{\hat{\theta}}^*)^2 = (4-4)^2 = 0$
  • $(\hat{\theta}_3^* - \bar{\hat{\theta}}^*)^2 = (6-4)^2 = 4$

Step 3: Compute bootstrap variance (using $\frac{1}{B}$ as per the given formula)

$$\text{Var}_{\text{boot}} = \frac{1}{B}\sum_{b=1}^B (\hat{\theta}^{*(b)} - \bar{\hat{\theta}}^*)^2 = \frac{4 + 0 + 4}{3} = \boxed{\frac{8}{3} \approx 2.67}$$

Answer: (a) 2.67

Bootstrap standard error:

$$\widehat{SE}_{\text{boot}} = \sqrt{\frac{8}{3}} \approx 1.63$$
Important: The quiz formula uses $\frac{1}{B}$ (giving 2.67), NOT $\frac{1}{B-1}$ (which would give 4). Always use the formula as stated in the problem. In practice with large $B$ (hundreds/thousands), the difference is negligible.

Problem 3: CV Set Size and $E_{out}$ Estimation [End-Term Q1e]

Question: You have 10,000 data points total, with 8,000 allocated for training. You're debating whether to use a cross-validation set of size 200 or 100 to estimate the out-of-sample error $E_{out}$. Is a larger CV set (200) always better than a smaller one (100) for estimating $E_{out}$? Explain the bias-variance tradeoff in validation set sizing.

Recall: The purpose of a validation set is to estimate how well the FINAL model (trained on all available training data) will perform on unseen data. There's a fundamental tradeoff: larger validation set = more accurate estimate but the model being evaluated was trained on less data (and thus differs from the final model).

Answer: FALSE — a larger CV set is NOT necessarily better. Here's why:

Scenario comparison:

CV set = 200CV set = 100
Training size7,8007,900
Model trained on7,800 (farther from final 8,000-point model)7,900 (closer to final model)
Bias of $E_{out}$ estimateHigher (model differs more from final)Lower (model is more similar to final)
Variance of estimateLower (more test points)Higher (fewer test points)

Key insight: We want to estimate $E_{out}$ of the model that will ultimately use ALL 8,000 training points. The model trained on 7,900 points is closer to this final model than one trained on 7,800 points. So the 100-point CV set gives a less biased (though noisier) estimate of the final model's true error.

Bottom line: It depends on whether you prioritize low bias (smaller CV set, more training data) or low variance (larger CV set, more evaluation points). Neither is universally "better."

Problem 4: Why Evaluate on a Separate Test Set After Cross-Validation [Practice — Shubhranil]

Question: After using cross-validation to select the best model/hyperparameters, why is it important to evaluate on a completely separate test set that was never used during training or model selection? Why can't we just report the best CV score?

Recall: The goal is to get an unbiased estimate of how well our chosen model will perform on truly new data. Cross-validation was used as a TOOL for making a choice, but the winning score from that process has a specific bias.

Answer: Because the best CV score is optimistically biased due to "selection bias" (also called the winner's curse).

Step-by-step reasoning:

  1. We searched over many models (e.g., different hyperparameters, feature sets) and picked the one with the BEST CV score
  2. Selection bias: By choosing the minimum error among many candidates, we're biased toward models that got "lucky" on the particular CV folds. The best score overestimates performance.
  3. More models compared = more optimistic bias: If you compare 100 models by CV, the best CV score is more optimistically biased than if you compared only 5 models.
  4. Analogy: It's like looking at 100 random students' grades and reporting the highest one as the "class performance" — that's biased upward.

Solution: A truly held-out test set — never seen during training OR selection — gives an unbiased estimate of generalization error.

Correct workflow:

Training data → CV (select best model) → Retrain on full training set → Test set (report this score)

Problem 5: Polynomial Model Selection [Practice — Tarus]

Question: Degrees 1-6, data from unknown polynomial ≤ 6 with noise.

(a) Why training error decreases with degree:

Higher degree = more parameters = richer function class. Each lower-degree model is a special case of higher-degree (by setting higher coefficients to 0). The optimization can always do at least as well → training error is non-increasing.

(b) Why test error increases beyond optimal:

Beyond the true degree, extra parameters fit noise rather than signal. The model becomes specialized to training data's random fluctuations → poor generalization (overfitting). Variance increases faster than bias decreases.

(c) Role of bias, variance, overfitting:

  • Bias: Error from wrong model assumptions. Decreases as degree increases (more flexible).
  • Variance: Error from sensitivity to training data. Increases with degree.
  • Overfitting: When variance dominates — model fits noise, train error ≪ test error.

(d) Degree 2 (high bias), Degree 6 (high variance), Degree 3 (balanced):

Prefer Degree 3 — best generalization (lowest test error). It captures the true complexity without fitting noise.

Problem 6: Comparing Information Criteria (AIC, BIC, Adjusted R²)

Question: Three common model selection criteria are AIC (Akaike Information Criterion), BIC (Bayesian Information Criterion), and Adjusted $R^2$. When should you use each? How do their penalties for model complexity differ?

Recall: All three balance goodness-of-fit against model complexity, but with different tradeoffs:

  • $\text{AIC} = -2\log L + 2p$ (penalty = $2p$)
  • $\text{BIC} = -2\log L + p\log n$ (penalty = $p\log n$)
  • $\text{Adj. } R^2 = 1 - \frac{\text{RSS}/(n-p)}{\text{TSS}/(n-1)}$ (penalizes via degrees of freedom)

When to use each:

SituationBest ChoiceReason
Goal: best predictionsAIC or CVAIC asymptotically minimizes prediction error (equivalent to leave-one-out CV)
Goal: find the true modelBICBIC is consistent: selects the true model as $n \to \infty$
Quick, interpretable comparisonAdjusted $R^2$Simple, same scale as $R^2$, easy to explain
Non-linear/non-parametric modelsCVAIC/BIC require a likelihood function; CV works for ANY model

BIC penalty is STRONGER than AIC: Since $p\log n > 2p$ whenever $n > e^2 \approx 7.4$, BIC always selects simpler models than AIC for any reasonably-sized dataset. This means BIC is more conservative — it needs stronger evidence to justify adding parameters.

Topic 5: Optimization

Covers: Gradient Descent (Batch, Mini-Batch, SGD), Learning Rate, Convergence, Convex Optimization, Lagrangian & Lagrange Multipliers, Primal & Dual Problems, KKT Conditions, Constrained Optimization

What is Optimization? In ML, we always have a loss function that measures "how wrong our model is." Optimization is the process of finding the model parameters that make this loss as small as possible. It's like finding the lowest point in a valley.

Two flavors:

  • Unconstrained: "Find the lowest point anywhere" → Gradient Descent
  • Constrained: "Find the lowest point, but stay within certain boundaries" → Lagrangian/KKT methods

1. Basic Understanding

1.1 Gradient Descent

The Core Idea: Imagine you're blindfolded on a hilly landscape and want to reach the lowest valley. Strategy: feel the slope beneath your feet, then take a step downhill. Repeat. That's gradient descent! The gradient tells you which direction is "uphill," so you go the opposite way.

Three variants:

  • Batch GD: Survey the ENTIRE landscape before each step (accurate but slow)
  • SGD: Take a step based on just ONE random measurement (fast but noisy)
  • Mini-Batch: Survey a small area before stepping (good balance)

Update Rule:

$$\theta_{t+1} = \theta_t - \eta \nabla L(\theta_t)$$

where $\eta$ = learning rate, $\nabla L$ = gradient of loss function.

Direction of steepest descent: $-\nabla L(\theta)$ (negative gradient points toward decreasing loss)

Types of Gradient Descent

MethodBatch SizeUpdate per EpochProperties
Batch GDAll $n$ samples1 updateSmooth convergence, expensive per step, exact gradient
Mini-Batch GD$m$ samples ($1 < m < n$)$n/m$ updatesGood balance, parallelizable, noisy gradient
SGD1 sample$n$ updatesFastest per step, very noisy, can escape local minima

Batch GD gradient (for MSE):

$$\nabla L = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n \nabla L_i(\theta) = \frac{1}{n}X^T(X\theta - y)$$

SGD gradient (random sample $i$):

$$\nabla L \approx \nabla L_i(\theta) = x_i(x_i^T\theta - y_i)$$

Mini-batch (batch $B \subset \{1,...,n\}$, $|B|=m$):

$$\nabla L \approx \frac{1}{m}\sum_{i \in B} \nabla L_i(\theta)$$

Sample Usage Calculation

Dataset: $n$ samples, mini-batch size $m$, $T$ iterations:

  • Samples used per iteration: $m$
  • Total samples processed in $T$ iterations: $T \times m$
  • Epochs completed: $T \times m / n$

Example: $n = 1000$, $m = 50$, 10 iterations → $10 \times 50 = 500$ samples used (half an epoch).

1.2 Learning Rate

What is the learning rate? It's the SIZE of each step you take downhill. Too big → you overshoot the valley and bounce around (or fly off the mountain!). Too small → you'll get there eventually, but it takes forever. Finding the right step size is crucial.

Too large $\eta$: Diverges (overshoots minimum, oscillates or explodes)

Too small $\eta$: Very slow convergence (many iterations needed)

Optimal for quadratic: $\eta < 2/\lambda_{\max}$ where $\lambda_{\max}$ is largest eigenvalue of Hessian

Learning rate schedules:

  • Constant: $\eta_t = \eta_0$
  • Step decay: $\eta_t = \eta_0 \cdot \gamma^{\lfloor t/s \rfloor}$
  • Inverse: $\eta_t = \eta_0 / (1 + \alpha t)$

1.3 Advanced Optimizers

Momentum:

$$v_{t+1} = \gamma v_t + \eta \nabla L(\theta_t)$$ $$\theta_{t+1} = \theta_t - v_{t+1}$$

Accumulates past gradients → faster in consistent direction, dampens oscillation.

Adam (Adaptive Moment Estimation):

$$m_t = \beta_1 m_{t-1} + (1-\beta_1)\nabla L_t \quad \text{(1st moment)}$$ $$v_t = \beta_2 v_{t-1} + (1-\beta_2)(\nabla L_t)^2 \quad \text{(2nd moment)}$$ $$\hat{m}_t = m_t/(1-\beta_1^t), \quad \hat{v}_t = v_t/(1-\beta_2^t) \quad \text{(bias correction)}$$ $$\theta_{t+1} = \theta_t - \frac{\eta \hat{m}_t}{\sqrt{\hat{v}_t} + \epsilon}$$

Default: $\beta_1 = 0.9$, $\beta_2 = 0.999$, $\epsilon = 10^{-8}$

1.4 Convex Optimization

Definition (Convex Function): A function $f$ is convex if the line segment between any two points on the graph lies on or above the graph:

$$f(\lambda x + (1-\lambda)y) \leq \lambda f(x) + (1-\lambda)f(y), \quad \forall\, x, y, \;\lambda \in [0,1]$$

Equivalently, for twice-differentiable functions: $f$ is convex iff the Hessian $\nabla^2 f \succeq 0$ (positive semi-definite) everywhere.

A convex optimization problem:

$$\min_\mathbf{x} f(\mathbf{x}) \quad \text{subject to } g_i(\mathbf{x}) \leq 0, \; h_j(\mathbf{x}) = 0$$

where $f$ and all $g_i$ are convex, and $h_j$ are affine (linear).

Key property: Any local minimum is a global minimum. No local minima traps.

1.5 Stationary Points, Saddle Points, and Initialization

The problem with non-convex landscapes: In real-world ML (neural networks), the loss surface isn't a simple bowl — it's more like a mountain range with valleys (local minima), peaks (local maxima), and mountain passes (saddle points). Gradient descent can get stuck at any of these because the gradient is zero there! The starting point (initialization) determines which valley you end up in.

Stationary point: Any point where $\nabla f = 0$. Types:

  • Local minimum: $f'' > 0$ (1D) or Hessian PD (multi-D)
  • Local maximum: $f'' < 0$ (1D) or Hessian ND (multi-D)
  • Saddle point: A stationary point that is neither a local minimum nor a local maximum — the function curves upward in some directions and downward in others. Characterized by an indefinite Hessian (both positive and negative eigenvalues).

GD gets stuck at ANY stationary point because the update $\theta - \eta \cdot 0 = \theta$ (no movement).

For non-convex functions:

  • Multiple stationary points exist
  • Initialization determines which one GD converges to
  • In high dimensions: saddle points vastly outnumber local minima
  • Solutions: random restarts, momentum, SGD noise, learning rate schedules

For convex functions: Only one stationary point exists (the global minimum) → no such issues.

1.6 Lagrangian and Lagrange Multipliers

What is this? Sometimes we need to optimize with CONSTRAINTS (e.g., "minimize cost, but the budget can't exceed $1000"). The Lagrangian is a mathematical trick: instead of solving a constrained problem directly, we merge the constraint INTO the objective function using a "penalty weight" (the Lagrange multiplier $\lambda$). A larger $\lambda$ means the constraint is more important.

ML connection: Regularization is equivalent to constrained optimization! Ridge regression minimizes loss subject to "weights must be small" — the $\lambda$ in regularization IS a Lagrange multiplier.

Problem: $\min f(\mathbf{x})$ subject to $g_i(\mathbf{x}) \leq 0$, $h_j(\mathbf{x}) = 0$

Lagrangian:

$$\mathcal{L}(\mathbf{x}, \boldsymbol{\lambda}, \boldsymbol{\nu}) = f(\mathbf{x}) + \sum_{i=1}^m \lambda_i g_i(\mathbf{x}) + \sum_{j=1}^p \nu_j h_j(\mathbf{x})$$

where $\lambda_i \geq 0$ (inequality multipliers), $\nu_j$ (equality multipliers, unrestricted)

1.7 KKT Conditions

What are KKT conditions? They're the "checklist" for optimality in constrained problems. If a point satisfies ALL four KKT conditions, it's optimal (for convex problems). Think of it as the constrained version of "set the derivative to zero."

The most important one — Complementary Slackness: Either a constraint is "tight" (active, fully used up) or its multiplier is zero (it doesn't matter). You can't have a constraint that's slack AND has a nonzero multiplier.

Necessary conditions for optimality (and sufficient if problem is convex):

  1. Stationarity: $\nabla_\mathbf{x} \mathcal{L} = \nabla f + \sum_i \lambda_i \nabla g_i + \sum_j \nu_j \nabla h_j = 0$
  2. Primal feasibility: $g_i(\mathbf{x}^*) \leq 0$, $h_j(\mathbf{x}^*) = 0$
  3. Dual feasibility: $\lambda_i \geq 0$
  4. Complementary slackness: $\lambda_i g_i(\mathbf{x}^*) = 0$ for all $i$

Complementary slackness means: Either the constraint is active ($g_i = 0$) or the multiplier is zero ($\lambda_i = 0$). If constraint is inactive ($g_i < 0$), it doesn't affect the solution.

1.8 Primal and Dual Problems

Intuition: Every constrained optimization problem can be viewed from two angles:

  • Primal: "Minimize the objective directly while respecting constraints" (the original problem)
  • Dual: "Maximize a lower bound on the optimal value by adjusting the penalty weights"

For convex problems, both give the SAME answer (strong duality). The dual is often easier to solve and reveals important structure (e.g., SVMs use the dual to find support vectors and enable kernel trick).

Primal: $p^* = \min_\mathbf{x} \max_{\lambda \geq 0, \nu} \mathcal{L}(\mathbf{x}, \lambda, \nu)$

Dual: $d^* = \max_{\lambda \geq 0, \nu} \min_\mathbf{x} \mathcal{L}(\mathbf{x}, \lambda, \nu)$

Weak duality: $d^* \leq p^*$ (always)

Strong duality: $d^* = p^*$ (holds for convex problems satisfying Slater's condition)

Why dual matters in ML:

  • SVM: Dual formulation reveals support vectors and enables kernelization
  • Dual problem may be easier to solve (fewer variables when $n < p$)

2. When to Use

MethodWhen to Use
Batch GDSmall datasets, need precise convergence
Mini-batch GDDefault for large datasets, GPU parallelism
SGDVery large datasets, online learning
AdamDeep learning default, sparse gradients, non-stationary
Closed-form (Normal eq.)$n$ and $p$ small enough ($p < 10^4$), linear models
Lagrangian/KKTConstrained optimization (SVM, regularization interpretation)

3. All Formulae

Gradient Descent

$$\theta_{t+1} = \theta_t - \eta \nabla L(\theta_t)$$ $$\text{For MSE: } \nabla L = X^T(X\theta - y)/n$$ $$\text{For single function: } \theta_{t+1} = \theta_t - \eta f'(\theta_t)$$

Convergence Conditions

$$\text{Convex } + \eta < \frac{2}{\lambda_{\max}(H)} \implies \text{converges}$$ $$\text{Rate: } L(\theta_t) - L(\theta^*) \leq O(1/t) \text{ for GD on smooth convex}$$ $$\text{Rate: } O(\rho^t) \text{ for strongly convex, } \rho = 1 - \eta\mu < 1$$

Lagrangian & KKT

$$\mathcal{L}(\mathbf{x},\lambda,\nu) = f(\mathbf{x}) + \sum_i\lambda_i g_i(\mathbf{x}) + \sum_j \nu_j h_j(\mathbf{x})$$ $$\text{KKT: } \nabla_x\mathcal{L}=0, \; g_i \leq 0, \; \lambda_i \geq 0, \; \lambda_ig_i = 0$$

Momentum & Adam

$$v_{t+1} = \gamma v_t + \eta\nabla L_t, \quad \theta_{t+1} = \theta_t - v_{t+1}$$ $$\text{Adam: } \theta_{t+1} = \theta_t - \frac{\eta\hat{m}_t}{\sqrt{\hat{v}_t}+\epsilon}$$

4. Best Practices

  • Start with Adam (default learning rate 0.001) for new problems
  • Monitor loss curve: Should decrease smoothly. Oscillations → reduce $\eta$.
  • Feature scaling before GD: Standardize features for faster convergence (isotropic landscape)
  • Mini-batch size: Powers of 2 (32, 64, 128, 256) for GPU efficiency
  • For convex problems: GD guaranteed to converge; for non-convex (deep learning), may find local minimum
  • Check convexity: If Hessian is PSD everywhere, problem is convex → any local min is global
Common Mistakes:
  • Confusing steepest descent direction ($-\nabla L$) with the gradient ($\nabla L$)
  • Forgetting $\lambda \geq 0$ for inequality constraints in KKT
  • Not checking complementary slackness
  • Using batch GD on large datasets (too slow)
  • Confusing epoch with iteration (1 epoch = 1 pass through all data)

5. Example Problems (Solved Exam Questions)

Problem 1: GD at Saddle Points / Local Maxima [Mid-Term Q7]

Question: $f(x) = x^4 - 2x^2$, $\eta = 0.1$. Perform two steps of gradient descent for: (a) $x_0 = 0$, (b) $x_0 = 0.1$, (c) $x_0 = -0.1$. (d) Is $x=0$ a local minimum? How do the updates in (b) and (c) differ?

Recall: Gradient descent update: $x_{t+1} = x_t - \eta \cdot f'(x_t)$

Setup: $f'(x) = 4x^3 - 4x$, $\quad f''(x) = 12x^2 - 4$

(a) $x_0 = 0$:

Step 1: $f'(0) = 4(0)^3 - 4(0) = 0$

$$x_1 = 0 - 0.1(0) = 0$$

Step 2: $f'(0) = 0$ again

$$x_2 = 0 - 0.1(0) = 0$$

GD stalls forever at $x = 0$. No update occurs because the gradient is exactly zero!

(b) $x_0 = 0.1$:

Step 1: $f'(0.1) = 4(0.1)^3 - 4(0.1) = 0.004 - 0.4 = -0.396$

$$x_1 = 0.1 - 0.1(-0.396) = 0.1 + 0.0396 = 0.1396$$

Step 2: $f'(0.1396) = 4(0.1396)^3 - 4(0.1396) = 0.0109 - 0.5584 = -0.5475$

$$x_2 = 0.1396 - 0.1(-0.5475) = 0.1396 + 0.0548 = \boxed{0.1944}$$

Moving toward the positive minimum at $x = +1$.

(c) $x_0 = -0.1$:

Step 1: $f'(-0.1) = 4(-0.1)^3 - 4(-0.1) = -0.004 + 0.4 = 0.396$

$$x_1 = -0.1 - 0.1(0.396) = -0.1 - 0.0396 = -0.1396$$

Step 2: $f'(-0.1396) = 4(-0.1396)^3 - 4(-0.1396) = -0.0109 + 0.5584 = 0.5475$

$$x_2 = -0.1396 - 0.1(0.5475) = -0.1396 - 0.0548 = \boxed{-0.1944}$$

Moving toward the negative minimum at $x = -1$.

(d) Interpretation:

  • Is $x=0$ a local minimum? No! $f''(0) = 12(0)^2 - 4 = -4 < 0$ → it's a local maximum. GD stalls there because the gradient is zero, NOT because it's a minimum.
  • How do (b) and (c) differ? They diverge to different local minima:
    • $x_0 = +0.1$ → gradient is negative → moves right → converges to $x = +1$
    • $x_0 = -0.1$ → gradient is positive → moves left → converges to $x = -1$
    The sign of $x_0$ determines which basin of attraction GD falls into.
  • GD stalls at ANY stationary point ($f'(x) = 0$) — whether minimum, maximum, or saddle point. This is a known failure mode for non-convex optimization.
Key Lessons:
  • $\nabla f = 0$ does NOT guarantee a minimum. Must check second-order conditions ($f'' > 0$ for 1D, Hessian PD for multi-D).
  • Non-convex functions have multiple stationary points (minima, maxima, saddle points).
  • GD can get trapped at saddle points. In practice: use random restarts, momentum, or noise (SGD) to escape.
  • Convex functions have NO saddle points or local maxima → GD always converges to global minimum.

Problem 2: Constrained Optimization [Mid-Term Q1]

Question: Minimize $f(x_1,x_2) = x_1^2 + x_2^2$ subject to $x_1 + 2x_2 \leq -1$.

(a) Convexity: Hessian = $\begin{bmatrix}2&0\\0&2\end{bmatrix}$, PD → strictly convex.

(b) Feasible Region: Half-plane below line $x_1 + 2x_2 = -1$.

(c) Solution via Lagrange multipliers:

Rewrite constraint: $g(x) = x_1 + 2x_2 + 1 \leq 0$

$$\mathcal{L} = x_1^2 + x_2^2 + \lambda(x_1 + 2x_2 + 1)$$

Stationarity:

$$\frac{\partial \mathcal{L}}{\partial x_1} = 2x_1 + \lambda = 0 \implies x_1 = -\lambda/2$$ $$\frac{\partial \mathcal{L}}{\partial x_2} = 2x_2 + 2\lambda = 0 \implies x_2 = -\lambda$$

Constraint active (unconstrained min at origin is infeasible): $x_1 + 2x_2 = -1$

$$-\frac{\lambda}{2} + 2(-\lambda) = -1 \implies -\frac{5\lambda}{2} = -1 \implies \lambda = \frac{2}{5}$$ $$x_1^* = -\frac{1}{5}, \quad x_2^* = -\frac{2}{5}$$

Verify KKT: $\lambda = 2/5 > 0$ ✓, $g(x^*) = -1/5 - 4/5 + 1 = 0$ ✓ (active)

Optimal value: $f^* = 1/25 + 4/25 = \boxed{1/5 = 0.2}$

Problem 3: Gradient Descent Update Step [Quiz Q6]

Question: Consider minimizing the loss function $L(\theta) = (\theta - 4)^2$ using gradient descent. Starting at $\theta_0 = 6$ with learning rate $\eta = 0.25$, compute the value of $\theta$ after one gradient descent update ($\theta_1$). Where is the true minimum, and are we moving toward it?

Recall: The gradient descent update rule: $\theta_{t+1} = \theta_t - \eta \cdot \nabla L(\theta_t)$. We subtract the gradient (scaled by $\eta$) because the gradient points "uphill" and we want to go "downhill."

Step 1: Compute the gradient (derivative) of $L$

$$\frac{dL}{d\theta} = 2(\theta - 4)$$

Step 2: Evaluate the gradient at the current point $\theta_0 = 6$

$$\nabla L\big|_{\theta=6} = 2(6-4) = 2 \times 2 = 4$$

The gradient is positive (= 4), meaning the loss is increasing at $\theta = 6$. We need to DECREASE $\theta$ to go downhill.

Step 3: Apply the update rule

$$\theta_1 = \theta_0 - \eta \cdot \nabla L = 6 - 0.25 \times 4 = 6 - 1 = \boxed{5}$$

Verification: The true minimum is at $\theta^* = 4$ (where derivative = 0). We moved from 6 to 5, which is toward 4 ✔. The learning rate 0.25 keeps us stable — we move 1 unit per step. After one more step: $\nabla L|_5 = 2(5-4) = 2$, $\theta_2 = 5 - 0.25(2) = 4.5$, getting closer each time.

Problem 4: Gradient of a Multivariable Function [Quiz Q7]

Question: Given the multivariable loss function $L(\theta_1, \theta_2) = \theta_1^2 + 4\theta_2^2$, compute the gradient vector $\nabla L$. What direction would gradient descent move, and why might this function be problematic for standard GD?

Recall: For a function of multiple variables, the gradient is the vector of partial derivatives: $\nabla L = \left(\frac{\partial L}{\partial \theta_1}, \frac{\partial L}{\partial \theta_2}\right)$. It points in the direction of steepest increase.

Step 1: Compute each partial derivative

$$\frac{\partial L}{\partial \theta_1} = 2\theta_1 \quad \text{(derivative of $\theta_1^2$ treating $\theta_2$ as constant)}$$ $$\frac{\partial L}{\partial \theta_2} = 8\theta_2 \quad \text{(derivative of $4\theta_2^2$ treating $\theta_1$ as constant)}$$

Step 2: Assemble the gradient vector

$$\nabla L = \begin{pmatrix} 2\theta_1 \\ 8\theta_2 \end{pmatrix} = \boxed{(2\theta_1,\; 8\theta_2)}$$

Step 3: The steepest descent direction is $-\nabla L$

$$-\nabla L = (-2\theta_1,\; -8\theta_2)$$

Why this is problematic for GD: The loss is 4× steeper in the $\theta_2$ direction than $\theta_1$. This creates elongated elliptical contours. A single learning rate $\eta$ that works for $\theta_2$ (steep) will be too slow for $\theta_1$ (gentle), and vice versa. This causes oscillation or slow convergence. Solutions: use adaptive learning rates (Adam, RMSprop) or feature scaling.

Problem 5: Gradient Descent with $(x+4)^2$ [End-Term Q6]

Question: Consider the function $f(x) = (x+4)^2$. We want to minimize it using gradient descent starting at $x_0 = 3$ with learning rate $\eta = 0.01$.

(a) Compute the gradient of $f$ at the initial point $x_0 = 3$.
(b) What is the updated value $x_1$ after one gradient descent step?

Recall: The gradient descent update rule is: $x_{t+1} = x_t - \eta \cdot f'(x_t)$. We move in the direction opposite to the gradient (downhill), with step size controlled by $\eta$.

(a) Computing the gradient at $x_0 = 3$:

First, find the derivative of $f(x) = (x+4)^2$ using the chain rule:

$$f'(x) = 2(x+4) \cdot \frac{d}{dx}(x+4) = 2(x+4)$$

Now evaluate at $x_0 = 3$:

$$f'(3) = 2(3+4) = 2 \times 7 = \boxed{14}$$

The gradient is positive (= 14), meaning the function is increasing at $x=3$. We should move LEFT (decrease $x$) to go downhill.

(b) One gradient descent update:

$$x_1 = x_0 - \eta \cdot f'(x_0) = 3 - 0.01 \times 14 = 3 - 0.14 = \boxed{2.86}$$

Sanity check: The minimum of $(x+4)^2$ is at $x^* = -4$. We started at $x=3$ and moved to $x=2.86$ — that's moving toward $-4$ (correct direction!). The step is very small because $\eta = 0.01$ is conservative. It would take many iterations to reach $-4$.

Problem 6: Batch vs Mini-Batch [End-Term Q7]

Question: Dataset = 1000 samples. (a) Batch GD uses how many per iteration? (b) Mini-batch size 50 — how many per iteration? (c) Total samples in 10 iterations?

(a) Batch GD: Uses all 1000 samples per iteration.

(b) Mini-batch (size 50): Uses 50 samples per iteration.

(c) 10 iterations × 50 samples = 500 total samples processed.

This is only half an epoch (1 epoch = 1000 samples = 20 mini-batch iterations).

BatchMini-batch (50)SGD
Samples/iteration1000501
Iterations/epoch1201000
10 iterations use10,000 (10 epochs)500 (0.5 epoch)10 (0.01 epoch)

Problem 7: Direction of Steepest Descent [Quiz Q8]

Question: In gradient descent, what is the direction of steepest descent at a point $\theta$? Express in terms of the loss function $L(\theta)$.

Recall: The gradient $\nabla L(\theta)$ is a vector that points in the direction where the function INCREASES the fastest. To minimize, we want to go the OPPOSITE way.

Answer: The direction of steepest descent is $\boxed{-\nabla L(\theta)}$ (the negative gradient).

Why this is true (step-by-step reasoning):

  1. The gradient $\nabla L$ points "uphill" — toward steepest increase
  2. The negative gradient $-\nabla L$ points "downhill" — toward steepest decrease
  3. Gradient descent follows this direction: $\theta_{t+1} = \theta_t - \eta \nabla L(\theta_t)$

Formal justification: For a small step $\delta$ with $\|\delta\| \leq \epsilon$, we want to minimize $L(\theta + \delta)$. By Taylor expansion: $L(\theta + \delta) \approx L(\theta) + \nabla L^T \delta$. The dot product $\nabla L^T \delta$ is minimized when $\delta$ points opposite to $\nabla L$, giving $\delta = -\epsilon \frac{\nabla L}{\|\nabla L\|}$.

Problem 8: Closed-Form OLS [Quiz Q10]

Question: Derive the closed-form (analytical) solution to linear regression. What is the formula for the optimal parameter vector $\theta^*$ that minimizes the squared error loss?

Recall: In linear regression, we minimize $L(\theta) = \|X\theta - y\|^2$ (RSS). Instead of iterating with gradient descent, we can solve this EXACTLY by setting the gradient to zero and solving for $\theta$.

Step 1: Write the gradient of the loss

$$\nabla_\theta L = 2X^T(X\theta - y) = 2X^TX\theta - 2X^Ty$$

Step 2: Set gradient to zero (necessary condition for minimum)

$$X^TX\theta - X^Ty = 0$$ $$X^TX\theta = X^Ty \quad \text{← these are the "Normal Equations"}$$

Step 3: Solve for $\theta$ (multiply both sides by $(X^TX)^{-1}$)

$$\boxed{\theta^* = (X^TX)^{-1}X^Ty}$$

This requires $X^TX$ to be invertible (i.e., columns of $X$ are linearly independent, i.e., no perfect multicollinearity).

When to use closed-form vs gradient descent:

MethodWhen to useComputational cost
Closed-form$p$ moderate (up to ~$10^4$)$O(np^2 + p^3)$ for matrix inversion
Gradient Descent$p$ very large, regularized/non-linear models$O(np)$ per iteration

Problem 9: Multivariate Gradient Descent [Practice Q7]

Question: Consider $f(\theta_1, \theta_2) = (\theta_1 - 2)^2 + 4(\theta_2 + 1)^2$. Starting at $(\theta_1^{(0)}, \theta_2^{(0)}) = (4, 1)$ with $\eta = 0.1$:

(a) Compute $\nabla f$ at the starting point. (b) Perform one GD step. (c) What is the global minimum? Does GD converge for any positive $\eta$?

Recall: For multivariate GD: $\boldsymbol{\theta}^{(t+1)} = \boldsymbol{\theta}^{(t)} - \eta \nabla f(\boldsymbol{\theta}^{(t)})$

(a) Compute the gradient:

$$\nabla f = \begin{bmatrix}\frac{\partial f}{\partial \theta_1} \\ \frac{\partial f}{\partial \theta_2}\end{bmatrix} = \begin{bmatrix}2(\theta_1 - 2) \\ 8(\theta_2 + 1)\end{bmatrix}$$

At $(4, 1)$:

$$\nabla f(4, 1) = \begin{bmatrix}2(4-2) \\ 8(1+1)\end{bmatrix} = \boxed{\begin{bmatrix}4 \\ 16\end{bmatrix}}$$

(b) One GD step:

$$\begin{bmatrix}\theta_1^{(1)} \\ \theta_2^{(1)}\end{bmatrix} = \begin{bmatrix}4 \\ 1\end{bmatrix} - 0.1\begin{bmatrix}4 \\ 16\end{bmatrix} = \begin{bmatrix}4 - 0.4 \\ 1 - 1.6\end{bmatrix} = \boxed{\begin{bmatrix}3.6 \\ -0.6\end{bmatrix}}$$

Both components moved toward their optimal values ($\theta_1$: $4 \to 3.6$, closer to 2; $\theta_2$: $1 \to -0.6$, closer to $-1$).

(c) Global minimum and convergence:

Setting $\nabla f = 0$: $\theta_1 = 2, \theta_2 = -1$. Global minimum: $f(2, -1) = 0$.

The Hessian is $H = \text{diag}(2, 8)$ (constant, positive definite) → $f$ is strictly convex.

GD converges for $\eta < \frac{2}{\lambda_{\max}(H)} = \frac{2}{8} = 0.25$. For $\eta \geq 0.25$, the $\theta_2$ component oscillates and diverges.

Key insight: The function has different curvatures along different axes (2 vs 8). This makes the loss surface like an elongated ellipse, causing zig-zagging along the steep ($\theta_2$) direction — a classic motivation for adaptive learning rates (Adam, RMSprop).

Problem 10: Non-Convex Optimization — Stationary Points [Practice Q15]

Question: Consider $f(\theta) = \theta^4 - 4\theta^2$.

(a) Find ALL stationary points and classify each. (b) From $\theta_0 = 0.1$ with $\eta = 0.05$, where does GD converge? From $\theta_0 = -0.1$? (c) Why is this challenging for ML?

(a) Find and classify stationary points:

$$f'(\theta) = 4\theta^3 - 8\theta = 4\theta(\theta^2 - 2) = 0$$

Solutions: $\theta = 0, \;\theta = \sqrt{2} \approx 1.414, \;\theta = -\sqrt{2} \approx -1.414$

Classify using second derivative: $f''(\theta) = 12\theta^2 - 8$

Point$f''(\theta)$Classification$f(\theta)$
$\theta = 0$$12(0) - 8 = -8 < 0$Local Maximum$0$
$\theta = \sqrt{2}$$12(2) - 8 = 16 > 0$Local Minimum$4 - 8 = -4$
$\theta = -\sqrt{2}$$12(2) - 8 = 16 > 0$Local Minimum$4 - 8 = -4$

Both minima are global (same value $f = -4$). The local max at 0 separates the two basins.

(b) GD trajectories:

From $\theta_0 = 0.1$: $f'(0.1) = 4(0.1)(0.01-2) = -0.796 < 0$ → gradient pushes right → converges to $+\sqrt{2}$

From $\theta_0 = -0.1$: $f'(-0.1) = 4(-0.1)(0.01-2) = +0.796 > 0$ → gradient pushes left → converges to $-\sqrt{2}$

The sign of $\theta_0$ determines which minimum GD reaches. At exactly $\theta_0 = 0$, GD stalls forever (gradient = 0).

(c) Challenges for ML:

  • Multiple local minima: GD may find different solutions depending on initialization — no guarantee of finding the global optimum
  • Saddle points/local maxima: Flat gradients cause GD to stall. In high dimensions, saddle points far outnumber local minima
  • Practical strategies: (1) Multiple random restarts, (2) Momentum/Adam to escape flat regions, (3) SGD noise helps escape saddle points, (4) Learning rate warmup/scheduling

Created by Kushal Ghosh (gkushalg01@gmail.com)

Topic 6: Classification & SVM

Covers: What is Classification? • What is SVM? • Perceptron Learning Algorithm (PLA) • Hard Margin SVM • Soft Margin SVM (Slack Variables) • Dual Formulation • Support Vectors • Kernel Functions & Kernel Trick • Support Vector Regression (SVR)

1. Basic Understanding

1.1 What is Classification?

Classification is the task of predicting which category/class a data point belongs to. For example: is this email spam or not spam? Is this tumor malignant or benign?

In binary classification, there are exactly two classes, typically labeled $+1$ and $-1$ (or 1 and 0).

1.2 What is a Linear Classifier?

A linear classifier draws a straight line (in 2D), a plane (in 3D), or a hyperplane (in higher dimensions) to separate two classes.

Intuition: Imagine scattering red and blue dots on paper. A linear classifier tries to draw a single straight line so that all red dots are on one side and all blue dots are on the other.

Decision boundary equation: $\mathbf{w}^T\mathbf{x} + b = 0$ (hyperplane in $\mathbb{R}^d$)

Classification rule: $\hat{y} = \text{sign}(\mathbf{w}^T\mathbf{x} + b)$

  • If $\mathbf{w}^T\mathbf{x} + b > 0$ → predict class $+1$
  • If $\mathbf{w}^T\mathbf{x} + b < 0$ → predict class $-1$

Weight vector $\mathbf{w}$: Normal (perpendicular) to the decision boundary. It points toward the positive class side.

Bias $b$: Shifts the boundary away from the origin.

1.3 What is SVM (Support Vector Machine)?

The Big Idea: There can be MANY lines that separate two classes. Which one should we pick?

SVM says: pick the line that has the largest "gap" (margin) between the two classes.

Think of it like building a road between two neighborhoods — SVM builds the widest possible road so that both neighborhoods are far from the road edges.

Key Concepts:

  • Margin: The distance between the decision boundary and the nearest data point from either class. SVM maximizes this.
  • Support Vectors: The few critical points that are closest to the boundary — they "support" (define) the margin. If you removed all other points, the boundary wouldn't change!
  • Why maximize margin? A larger margin means the classifier is more confident and generalizes better to unseen data (less overfitting).

SVM Variants — The Three Levels:

VariantWhenWhat it Does
Hard Margin SVMData is perfectly linearly separableFinds max-margin hyperplane, NO points allowed inside margin
Soft Margin SVMData has noise/overlapAllows some points to violate margin (controlled by $C$)
Kernel SVMData is not linearly separableMaps data to higher dimensions where it becomes separable

1.4 Perceptron Learning Algorithm (PLA)

Before SVM, the simplest linear classifier is the Perceptron — it just finds ANY separating line (not necessarily the best one).

How it works (intuition): Start with a random line. Look at each point — if it's on the wrong side, nudge the line toward it. Repeat until all points are correctly classified.

Update rule: If point $(\mathbf{x}_n, y_n)$ is misclassified (i.e., $y_n \neq \text{sign}(\mathbf{w}^T\mathbf{x}_n)$):

$$\mathbf{w}_{t+1} = \mathbf{w}_t + y_n \mathbf{x}_n$$

(If using augmented vectors with bias: $\tilde{\mathbf{x}} = [1, x_1, x_2, \ldots]^T$, $\tilde{\mathbf{w}} = [b, w_1, w_2, \ldots]^T$)

Algorithm:

  1. Initialize $\mathbf{w}_0$ (e.g., zeros)
  2. Pick a misclassified point $(\mathbf{x}_n, y_n)$: $y_n(\mathbf{w}^T\mathbf{x}_n) \leq 0$
  3. Update: $\mathbf{w} \leftarrow \mathbf{w} + y_n\mathbf{x}_n$
  4. Repeat until no misclassified points (or max iterations)

Convergence: Guaranteed to converge if data is linearly separable (Perceptron Convergence Theorem). Does NOT converge for non-separable data.

PLA vs SVM: PLA finds any separator. SVM finds the best separator (max margin). PLA is simple but fragile; SVM generalizes better.

1.5 Hard Margin SVM

Goal: Find hyperplane that separates classes with MAXIMUM margin.

What is the margin? Imagine the decision boundary as a wall. Now push two parallel walls out — one toward the positive class, one toward the negative class — until they touch the nearest points. The distance between these two walls is the margin = $\frac{2}{\|\mathbf{w}\|}$.

Primal Formulation:

$$\min_{\mathbf{w},b} \frac{1}{2}\|\mathbf{w}\|^2$$ $$\text{s.t. } y_i(\mathbf{w}^T\mathbf{x}_i + b) \geq 1, \quad \forall i = 1,\ldots,n$$

Why $\frac{1}{2}\|w\|^2$? Maximizing margin $\frac{2}{\|w\|}$ is equivalent to minimizing $\|w\|^2$. The $\frac{1}{2}$ is just for cleaner derivatives.

What does the constraint mean? Every point must be on the correct side AND at least at distance 1 (in functional margin) from the boundary. No exceptions.

Requirement: Data must be linearly separable — otherwise no solution exists.

1.6 Soft Margin SVM (C-SVM)

Motivation: Real data is messy. What if classes overlap or one noisy point falls on the wrong side? Hard margin SVM would fail completely. Soft margin SVM relaxes the rules: it says "try to get a big margin, but it's OK if some points violate it — just pay a penalty."

Slack variables $\xi_i \geq 0$ measure "how badly" each point violates the margin:

$$\min_{\mathbf{w},b,\boldsymbol{\xi}} \frac{1}{2}\|\mathbf{w}\|^2 + C\sum_{i=1}^n \xi_i$$ $$\text{s.t. } y_i(\mathbf{w}^T\mathbf{x}_i + b) \geq 1 - \xi_i, \quad \xi_i \geq 0$$

Interpretation of $\xi_i$ (IMPORTANT for exams):

  • $\xi_i = 0$: Point is correctly classified AND outside the margin ✓ (happy point)
  • $0 < \xi_i \leq 1$: Point is inside the margin but still on the correct side (mild violation)
  • $\xi_i > 1$: Point is on the WRONG side = misclassified

What is $C$? (The tolerance knob)

  • Large $C$ (e.g., 1000): "I hate violations!" → penalizes margin violators heavily → narrow margin, complex boundary (may overfit)
  • Small $C$ (e.g., 0.01): "Violations are fine" → allows many points inside margin → wide margin, simple boundary (may underfit)
  • $C = \infty$: Same as Hard Margin SVM (zero tolerance for violations)

1.7 Dual Formulation

Why go dual? The primal problem has $d$ variables (one per feature dimension). The dual has $n$ variables (one per data point). When we use kernels (next section), the dual lets us work in infinite-dimensional spaces without ever computing coordinates there!

Dual of Hard Margin SVM:

$$\max_{\boldsymbol{\alpha}} \sum_{i=1}^n \alpha_i - \frac{1}{2}\sum_{i,j}\alpha_i\alpha_j y_i y_j \mathbf{x}_i^T\mathbf{x}_j$$ $$\text{s.t. } \alpha_i \geq 0, \quad \sum_i \alpha_i y_i = 0$$

Dual of Soft Margin SVM: (only difference: $\alpha_i$ is capped at $C$)

$$\max_{\boldsymbol{\alpha}} \sum_{i=1}^n \alpha_i - \frac{1}{2}\sum_{i,j}\alpha_i\alpha_j y_i y_j \mathbf{x}_i^T\mathbf{x}_j$$ $$\text{s.t. } 0 \leq \alpha_i \leq C, \quad \sum_i \alpha_i y_i = 0$$

Key insight: Solution depends on data ONLY through dot products $\mathbf{x}_i^T\mathbf{x}_j$ → enables kernel trick!

Recovery of $\mathbf{w}$: $\mathbf{w} = \sum_i \alpha_i y_i \mathbf{x}_i$ (weighted sum of support vectors only, since non-SVs have $\alpha_i = 0$)

1.8 Support Vectors

What are support vectors? They are the "important" data points — the ones sitting right on the edge of the margin. Think of them as the border guards that define where the boundary goes.

Formally: Points with $\alpha_i > 0$. They are:

  • Points exactly on the margin boundary ($y_i(\mathbf{w}^T\mathbf{x}_i + b) = 1$) if $\alpha_i < C$
  • Points inside margin or misclassified ($\xi_i > 0$) if $\alpha_i = C$

Key properties (frequently tested!):

  • Only support vectors determine the decision boundary — all other points are irrelevant
  • Removing a non-support-vector ($\alpha_i = 0$) doesn't change the classifier
  • Removing a support vector DOES change the classifier
  • $\mathbf{w}$ is a linear combination of ONLY support vectors
  • Typically very few points are support vectors (sparse solution)

1.9 Kernel Functions (The "Magic" of SVM)

The Problem: What if data isn't linearly separable? For example, class +1 forms a circle surrounded by class -1. No straight line can separate them!

The Solution: Map data to a HIGHER dimensional space where it becomes separable. Example: 2D points $(x_1, x_2)$ → 3D by adding $x_1^2 + x_2^2$ as a third coordinate. The circle becomes separable by a plane in 3D!

The Kernel Trick: Computing in high dimensions is expensive. But the dual formulation only needs dot products $\mathbf{x}_i^T\mathbf{x}_j$. A kernel function computes the dot product in the higher space WITHOUT actually going there:

$$K(\mathbf{x}_i, \mathbf{x}_j) = \phi(\mathbf{x}_i)^T\phi(\mathbf{x}_j)$$

We never compute $\phi(\mathbf{x})$ explicitly — just the kernel value. This is computationally cheap even for infinite-dimensional spaces!

Common kernels:

Kernel$K(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{x}')$Feature space dimWhen to use
Linear$\mathbf{x}^T\mathbf{x}'$$d$Data is linearly separable
Polynomial (degree $p$)$(\mathbf{x}^T\mathbf{x}' + c)^p$$\binom{d+p}{p}$Polynomial-like boundaries
RBF/Gaussian$\exp(-\gamma\|\mathbf{x}-\mathbf{x}'\|^2)$$\infty$Default choice for non-linear
Sigmoid$\tanh(\kappa\mathbf{x}^T\mathbf{x}' + c)$—Neural-network-like

Mercer's condition: A function $K$ is a valid kernel iff the kernel matrix $K_{ij} = K(\mathbf{x}_i, \mathbf{x}_j)$ is positive semi-definite for any set of points. (This guarantees a corresponding feature space exists.)

1.10 Support Vector Regression (SVR)

What is SVR? SVM is for classification. SVR adapts the same idea for regression. Instead of maximizing the margin between classes, SVR fits a tube around the data — errors inside the tube are ignored (considered "good enough").

ε-insensitive loss: Only penalize predictions that are more than $\epsilon$ away from the truth:

$$L_\epsilon(y, f(x)) = \max(0, |y - f(x)| - \epsilon)$$

Points inside the ε-tube contribute ZERO loss. This makes SVR robust to small noise.

SVR Formulation:

$$\min_{\mathbf{w},b} \frac{1}{2}\|\mathbf{w}\|^2 + C\sum_i(\xi_i + \xi_i^*)$$ $$\text{s.t. } y_i - \mathbf{w}^T\mathbf{x}_i - b \leq \epsilon + \xi_i$$ $$\mathbf{w}^T\mathbf{x}_i + b - y_i \leq \epsilon + \xi_i^*$$ $$\xi_i, \xi_i^* \geq 0$$

Key property: Points inside the ε-tube have ZERO loss → sparse solution (many $\alpha_i = 0$). This also means outliers have limited influence — if $C$ is moderate, extreme outliers are penalized but don't dominate like in ordinary least squares.

2. When to Use

MethodUse WhenAvoid When
PLAData is linearly separable, simple baselineNon-separable data (won't converge)
Hard Margin SVMClean, linearly separable dataNoisy data, outliers
Soft Margin SVMDefault for classification with SVMVery large datasets (slow: $O(n^2)$-$O(n^3)$)
Kernel SVMNon-linear decision boundariesVery large $n$ (kernel matrix $n\times n$)
SVRRegression with robustness to outliersWhen all errors matter equally

3. All Formulae

PLA

$$\mathbf{w}_{t+1} = \mathbf{w}_t + y_n\mathbf{x}_n \quad \text{(when } y_n(\mathbf{w}_t^T\mathbf{x}_n) \leq 0\text{)}$$

Hard Margin SVM (Primal)

$$\min \frac{1}{2}\|\mathbf{w}\|^2 \quad \text{s.t. } y_i(\mathbf{w}^T\mathbf{x}_i + b) \geq 1$$ $$\text{Margin} = \frac{2}{\|\mathbf{w}\|}$$

Soft Margin SVM

$$\min \frac{1}{2}\|\mathbf{w}\|^2 + C\sum_i\xi_i \quad \text{s.t. } y_i(\mathbf{w}^T\mathbf{x}_i+b) \geq 1-\xi_i, \; \xi_i \geq 0$$ $$\xi_i = 0 \text{ (correct, outside margin)}, \; 0<\xi_i\leq 1 \text{ (in margin)}, \; \xi_i>1 \text{ (misclassified)}$$

Dual (Kernel) SVM

$$\max_\alpha \sum_i\alpha_i - \frac{1}{2}\sum_{i,j}\alpha_i\alpha_j y_i y_j K(\mathbf{x}_i,\mathbf{x}_j)$$ $$\text{s.t. } 0 \leq \alpha_i \leq C, \; \sum_i\alpha_iy_i = 0$$ $$\mathbf{w} = \sum_i\alpha_iy_i\phi(\mathbf{x}_i), \quad f(\mathbf{x}) = \sum_i\alpha_iy_iK(\mathbf{x}_i,\mathbf{x}) + b$$

Kernels

$$K_{\text{poly}}(\mathbf{x},\mathbf{x}') = (\mathbf{x}^T\mathbf{x}' + c)^p$$ $$K_{\text{RBF}}(\mathbf{x},\mathbf{x}') = \exp(-\gamma\|\mathbf{x}-\mathbf{x}'\|^2) = \exp\left(-\frac{\|\mathbf{x}-\mathbf{x}'\|^2}{2\sigma^2}\right)$$

SVR

$$L_\epsilon = \max(0, |y-f(x)|-\epsilon)$$ $$f(\mathbf{x}) = \sum_i(\alpha_i - \alpha_i^*)K(\mathbf{x}_i,\mathbf{x}) + b$$

4. Best Practices

  • Always scale features before SVM (margin depends on distances)
  • Start with RBF kernel as default non-linear kernel
  • Tune C and γ jointly via grid search with CV
  • Large C → overfitting (hard margin behavior)
  • Large γ (RBF) → overfitting (each point creates its own cluster)
  • Small γ → underfitting (everything looks similar)
  • Check if linear kernel suffices first (much faster)
Common Mistakes:
  • "Weight vector is parallel to support vector" → FALSE. $\mathbf{w}$ is normal to the hyperplane.
  • Confusing $\alpha_i > 0$ (support vector) with $\alpha_i = C$ (margin violator)
  • Thinking removing any point changes the classifier (only SVs matter)
  • Applying hard margin SVM to noisy data (infeasible or bad generalization)
  • Not scaling features (SVM is not scale-invariant)

5. Example Problems (Solved Exam Questions)

Problem 1: PLA Update [End-Term Q5b]

Question: Given data: $(1,1,+1), (0,3,+1), (6,0,-1), (5,4,-1), (2,1,+1), (7,9,-1)$. With initial guesses $w_1 = 0.7$, $w_2 = 0.5$, $w_0 = -1$, perform one PLA update iteration on any one misclassified point. Show $\mathbf{w}_t$ and $\mathbf{w}_{t+1}$.

Recall: PLA update rule: $\mathbf{w}_{t+1} = \mathbf{w}_t + y_n \cdot \mathbf{x}_n$ (only applied to a misclassified point)

A point is misclassified when $\text{sign}(\mathbf{w}^T \mathbf{x}) \neq y_n$.

Step 1: Identify $\mathbf{w}_t$

$$\mathbf{w}_t = [w_0, w_1, w_2] = [-1, 0.7, 0.5]$$

Step 2: Find misclassified points

Compute $s = w_0 + w_1 x_1 + w_2 x_2$ for each point:

  • Point $(1,1)$, $y=+1$: $s = -1 + 0.7 + 0.5 = 0.2 > 0$ → predict $+1$ ✓
  • Point $(0,3)$, $y=+1$: $s = -1 + 0 + 1.5 = 0.5 > 0$ → predict $+1$ ✓
  • Point $(6,0)$, $y=-1$: $s = -1 + 4.2 + 0 = 3.2 > 0$ → predict $+1$ ✗ MISCLASSIFIED
  • Point $(5,4)$, $y=-1$: $s = -1 + 3.5 + 2 = 4.5 > 0$ → predict $+1$ ✗ MISCLASSIFIED
  • Point $(2,1)$, $y=+1$: $s = -1 + 1.4 + 0.5 = 0.9 > 0$ → predict $+1$ ✓
  • Point $(7,9)$, $y=-1$: $s = -1 + 4.9 + 4.5 = 8.4 > 0$ → predict $+1$ ✗ MISCLASSIFIED

Step 3: Pick any misclassified point and update

Choose point $(6, 0)$ with $y = -1$. Augmented input: $\mathbf{x}_n = [1, 6, 0]$ (prepend 1 for bias).

$$\mathbf{w}_{t+1} = \mathbf{w}_t + y_n \cdot \mathbf{x}_n = [-1, 0.7, 0.5] + (-1)[1, 6, 0]$$ $$= [-1 - 1,\; 0.7 - 6,\; 0.5 - 0] = \boxed{[-2, -5.3, 0.5]}$$

Interpretation: The weight vector shifted dramatically because point $(6,0)$ is far from the current boundary. The large negative $w_1$ will now strongly penalize large $x_1$ values, helping classify the negative-class points that lie at higher $x_1$.

Problem 2: Hard Margin SVM Constraints [End-Term Q5c]

Question: Data: $(1,1,+1), (0,3,+1), (6,0,-1), (5,4,-1), (2,1,+1), (7,9,-1)$. Write hard margin SVM constraints.

Objective: $\min \frac{1}{2}(w_1^2 + w_2^2)$

Constraints: $y_i(w_1x_{i1} + w_2x_{i2} + b) \geq 1$:

$$+1: \quad w_1(1) + w_2(1) + b \geq 1$$ $$+1: \quad w_1(0) + w_2(3) + b \geq 1$$ $$-1: \quad -(w_1(6) + w_2(0) + b) \geq 1 \implies -6w_1 - b \geq 1$$ $$-1: \quad -(w_1(5) + w_2(4) + b) \geq 1 \implies -5w_1 - 4w_2 - b \geq 1$$ $$+1: \quad w_1(2) + w_2(1) + b \geq 1$$ $$-1: \quad -(w_1(7) + w_2(9) + b) \geq 1 \implies -7w_1 - 9w_2 - b \geq 1$$

Problem 3: Soft Margin SVM Analysis [Mid-Term Q4]

Question: Given dual variables and slack:

Sample$\alpha_i^*$$\xi_i^*$
100
200
30.50
40.50
511.5
600

(Assuming $C = 1$)

(a) Fraction correctly classified:

$\xi_i > 1$ means misclassified. Only sample 5 has $\xi_5 = 1.5 > 1$.

Correctly classified: $5/6 = \boxed{5/6 \approx 83.3\%}$

(b) Support vectors:

Support vectors have $\alpha_i > 0$: Samples 3, 4, 5.

  • Samples 3, 4: $\alpha < C$, $\xi = 0$ → exactly on margin boundary
  • Sample 5: $\alpha = C = 1$ → margin violator (inside margin or misclassified)

(c) Remove sample 5 — classifier changes?

Yes. Sample 5 is a support vector ($\alpha_5 > 0$). Removing it changes the optimization → different $\mathbf{w}, b$.

(d) Remove sample 6 — classifier changes?

No. Sample 6 has $\alpha_6 = 0$ → not a support vector → doesn't influence the boundary at all.

Problem 4: Separability [Mid-Term Q3]

Question: (a) Is data separable with max-margin (hard) SVM? (b) With kernel SVM?

(a) Hard margin SVM: Only works if data is linearly separable. If any overlap exists between classes, hard margin SVM is infeasible (no solution exists). Must check if a hyperplane can perfectly separate the classes.

(b) Kernel SVM: With an appropriate kernel (e.g., RBF), can separate virtually ANY dataset. The RBF kernel maps to infinite-dimensional space where data becomes linearly separable (Cover's theorem: randomly labeled data is more likely separable in higher dimensions).

General principle: If data is not linearly separable → use either soft margin SVM (allows violations) or kernel SVM (maps to higher-dim space where it becomes separable).

Problem 5: Separating Hyperplane [End-Term Q5a]

Question: Data: $(1,1,+1), (0,3,+1), (6,0,-1), (5,4,-1), (2,1,+1), (7,9,-1)$. Using a 2D visual representation, find an equation of any separating hyperplane that correctly classifies all points. Show the weight vector.

Recall: A separating hyperplane satisfies $w_1x_1 + w_2x_2 + b = 0$ such that $\text{sign}(w_1x_1 + w_2x_2 + b) = y_i$ for all points.

Step 1: Visual inspection

Positive points: $(1,1), (0,3), (2,1)$ — all have $x_1 \leq 2$ (left side).

Negative points: $(6,0), (5,4), (7,9)$ — all have $x_1 \geq 5$ (right side).

There's a clear gap between $x_1 = 2$ and $x_1 = 5$. We can separate with $x_1 + x_2 = 4$.

Step 2: One valid solution: $-x_1 - x_2 + 4 = 0$

Weights: $w_1 = -1, \; w_2 = -1, \; b = 4$

Decision: predict $+1$ when $-x_1 - x_2 + 4 > 0$ (i.e., $x_1 + x_2 < 4$)

Step 3: Verify on ALL points

  • $(1,1)$, $y=+1$: $-1 - 1 + 4 = 2 > 0$ → predict $+1$ ✓
  • $(0,3)$, $y=+1$: $0 - 3 + 4 = 1 > 0$ → predict $+1$ ✓
  • $(2,1)$, $y=+1$: $-2 - 1 + 4 = 1 > 0$ → predict $+1$ ✓
  • $(6,0)$, $y=-1$: $-6 - 0 + 4 = -2 < 0$ → predict $-1$ ✓
  • $(5,4)$, $y=-1$: $-5 - 4 + 4 = -5 < 0$ → predict $-1$ ✓
  • $(7,9)$, $y=-1$: $-7 - 9 + 4 = -12 < 0$ → predict $-1$ ✓

Weight vector: $\mathbf{w} = [-1, -1]^T$, bias $b = 4$.

Note: Many valid hyperplanes exist (e.g., $x_1 = 3$, $x_1 + x_2 = 5$). The SVM finds the one maximizing margin.

Problem 6: True/False — Weight Vector Direction [End-Term Q1a]

Statement: "In SVC, the weight vector is always parallel to the vector joining origin to the support vector."

FALSE.

The weight vector $\mathbf{w}$ is normal (perpendicular) to the decision hyperplane. It points in the direction that maximally separates the classes.

The vector from origin to a support vector can be in ANY direction — it depends on where the data happens to lie. There is no geometric requirement for $\mathbf{w}$ to be parallel to $\mathbf{x}_{\text{SV}}$.

$\mathbf{w} = \sum_i \alpha_i y_i \mathbf{x}_i$ is a weighted combination of support vectors, but that doesn't make it parallel to any individual support vector.

Created by Kushal Ghosh (gkushalg01@gmail.com)

Topic 7: Regularization

Covers: Ridge Regression (L2), LASSO (L1), Elastic Net, Kernelized Ridge, Bayesian Interpretation, Regularization Path, Effect on Bias-Variance, Geometric Interpretation

What is Regularization? It's a way to prevent your model from being "too clever" — from fitting the noise in your training data. The idea: add a penalty that discourages large coefficient values. This forces the model to find SIMPLER explanations, which generalize better to new data.

Analogy: Imagine writing an essay with a word limit. Without the limit, you might ramble and include irrelevant details (overfitting). The word limit forces you to include only what truly matters (regularization). A strict word limit ($\lambda$ large) makes your essay shorter and simpler; a lenient one ($\lambda$ small) lets you include more.

Key trade-off: More regularization = simpler model = more bias but less variance. Less regularization = more complex model = less bias but more variance.

1. Basic Understanding

1.1 Why Regularization?

OLS overfits when $p$ is large relative to $n$ (high variance). Regularization adds a penalty to prevent coefficients from becoming too large, trading increased bias for reduced variance.

When do you NEED regularization?

  • Many features relative to samples ($p$ close to or larger than $n$)
  • Multicollinearity (features are correlated — coefficients blow up)
  • Model is overfitting (low training error, high test error)

1.2 Ridge Regression (L2)

How Ridge works: Ridge adds the SUM OF SQUARES of all coefficients as a penalty. This shrinks ALL coefficients toward zero but never makes them exactly zero. It's like saying "keep all features, but don't let any one dominate too much."

Analogy: You have a budget for how "extreme" your weights can be. Ridge distributes the budget evenly — it prefers many small weights over a few large ones.

$$\hat{\beta}_{\text{ridge}} = \arg\min_\beta \left[\|\mathbf{y} - X\beta\|^2 + \lambda\|\beta\|_2^2\right]$$

Closed-form solution:

$$\hat{\beta}_{\text{ridge}} = (X^TX + \lambda I)^{-1}X^T\mathbf{y}$$

Properties:

  • Shrinks all coefficients toward zero but never exactly to zero
  • $(X^TX + \lambda I)$ is always invertible (even when $X^TX$ is singular!)
  • Handles multicollinearity
  • Does NOT perform feature selection

Effect of $\lambda$:

  • $\lambda = 0$: OLS solution (no regularization)
  • $\lambda \to \infty$: $\hat{\beta} \to 0$ (maximum shrinkage)
  • Increasing $\lambda$: Increases bias, decreases variance

1.3 LASSO (L1)

How LASSO works: LASSO adds the SUM OF ABSOLUTE VALUES of coefficients as penalty. Unlike Ridge, LASSO can push coefficients all the way to EXACTLY zero — effectively removing features from the model. This makes it a feature selection tool!

Analogy: If Ridge is "keep everyone on the team but reduce their playing time equally," LASSO is "cut players who don't contribute enough."

$$\hat{\beta}_{\text{lasso}} = \arg\min_\beta \left[\|\mathbf{y} - X\beta\|^2 + \lambda\|\beta\|_1\right]$$

where $\|\beta\|_1 = \sum_j|\beta_j|$

Properties:

  • Can shrink coefficients exactly to zero → performs feature selection
  • No closed-form solution (use coordinate descent, LARS)
  • Produces sparse models
  • Effectively yields a lower-dimensional feature space

Soft-thresholding operator:

$$\hat{\beta}_j = \text{sign}(\hat{\beta}_j^{OLS})\max(|\hat{\beta}_j^{OLS}| - \lambda/2, 0)$$

(for orthogonal design)

1.4 Elastic Net

Best of both worlds: Elastic Net combines L1 (sparsity/feature selection) and L2 (stability/grouping). Use it when features are correlated — LASSO might randomly pick one from a group, while Elastic Net keeps the whole group together.

$$\hat{\beta}_{\text{EN}} = \arg\min_\beta \left[\|\mathbf{y} - X\beta\|^2 + \lambda_1\|\beta\|_1 + \lambda_2\|\beta\|_2^2\right]$$

Or equivalently with mixing parameter $\alpha \in [0,1]$:

$$\min_\beta \|\mathbf{y} - X\beta\|^2 + \lambda[\alpha\|\beta\|_1 + (1-\alpha)\|\beta\|_2^2]$$
  • $\alpha = 1$: LASSO
  • $\alpha = 0$: Ridge
  • Combines sparsity (L1) with grouping effect (L2)
  • Better than LASSO when features are correlated (selects groups together)

1.5 Geometric Interpretation

Visual intuition: Think of it as the OLS solution being a point on a map, and regularization defining a "budget zone" you must stay within:

  • Ridge: Budget zone is a CIRCLE. The OLS ellipses hit the circle at a smooth point → coefficients shrink but rarely hit zero
  • LASSO: Budget zone is a DIAMOND (with pointy corners on the axes). The OLS ellipses hit the corners → coefficients are pushed to exactly zero (sparsity!)

This geometric picture explains WHY LASSO gives sparse solutions and Ridge doesn't.

Equivalent constrained formulation:

$$\text{Ridge: } \min \|y - X\beta\|^2 \text{ s.t. } \|\beta\|_2^2 \leq t$$ $$\text{LASSO: } \min \|y - X\beta\|^2 \text{ s.t. } \|\beta\|_1 \leq t$$

Geometry: OLS contours (ellipses) intersect the constraint region:

  • Ridge: constraint is a circle/sphere → intersection rarely at axes → non-zero coefficients
  • LASSO: constraint is a diamond/octahedron → corners at axes → intersection often at corners → zero coefficients (sparsity)

1.6 Bayesian Interpretation

Beautiful connection: Regularization isn't just a "trick" — it has a deep statistical meaning. Adding an L2 penalty is EQUIVALENT to assuming your weights come from a Gaussian distribution centered at zero (your prior belief is "weights should be small"). Adding an L1 penalty is equivalent to a Laplace prior (belief: "most weights should be exactly zero"). This connects frequentist regularization to Bayesian statistics!

Ridge = MAP with Gaussian prior:

$$P(\beta) \propto \exp(-\lambda\|\beta\|_2^2) \quad \text{(Gaussian prior } \beta_j \sim N(0, 1/\lambda)\text{)}$$

LASSO = MAP with Laplace prior:

$$P(\beta) \propto \exp(-\lambda\|\beta\|_1) \quad \text{(Laplace prior: heavy tails, peaked at 0)}$$

Laplace prior concentrates mass at zero → encourages exactly-zero solutions.

1.7 Kernelized Ridge Regression

What is this? Normal Ridge is linear. But what if the true relationship is nonlinear? The kernel trick lets you implicitly work in a high-dimensional feature space (where the relationship IS linear) without ever computing those features explicitly. Kernelized Ridge = nonlinear regression with regularization.

Using the kernel trick:

$$\hat{f}(\mathbf{x}) = \mathbf{k}(\mathbf{x})^T(K + \lambda I)^{-1}\mathbf{y}$$

where $K_{ij} = K(\mathbf{x}_i, \mathbf{x}_j)$ and $\mathbf{k}(\mathbf{x})_i = K(\mathbf{x}_i, \mathbf{x})$

Allows non-linear regression with regularization, without explicitly computing features.

1.8 Ridge vs LASSO Comparison

AspectRidge (L2)LASSO (L1)
Penalty$\lambda\sum\beta_j^2$$\lambda\sum|\beta_j|$
Feature SelectionNo (all coefficients non-zero)Yes (some exactly zero)
Closed-formYes: $(X^TX+\lambda I)^{-1}X^Ty$No (iterative methods)
Correlated featuresShares weight among correlatedPicks one, zeros others
When $p > n$Selects all $p$ featuresSelects at most $n$ features
Bayesian priorGaussianLaplace
GeometryCircle constraintDiamond constraint

2. When to Use

  • Ridge: Many features all somewhat relevant. Multicollinearity. Don't need feature selection.
  • LASSO: Believe many features are irrelevant. Need interpretability (sparse model).
  • Elastic Net: Many correlated features. Want sparsity but also grouping.
  • Always standardize features before regularization (penalty treats all equally).

3. All Formulae

Ridge

$$\hat{\beta}_{\text{ridge}} = (X^TX + \lambda I)^{-1}X^Ty$$ $$\text{Effective df: } \text{df}(\lambda) = \text{tr}[X(X^TX+\lambda I)^{-1}X^T] = \sum_j\frac{d_j^2}{d_j^2+\lambda}$$

where $d_j$ are singular values of $X$

LASSO

$$\min_\beta \frac{1}{2n}\|y-X\beta\|^2 + \lambda\|\beta\|_1$$ $$\text{Soft threshold: } S_\lambda(z) = \text{sign}(z)\max(|z|-\lambda, 0)$$

Elastic Net

$$\min_\beta \frac{1}{2n}\|y-X\beta\|^2 + \lambda[\alpha\|\beta\|_1 + (1-\alpha)\|\beta\|_2^2]$$

Bias-Variance Effect

$$\text{As } \lambda \uparrow: \quad \text{Bias} \uparrow, \quad \text{Variance} \downarrow$$ $$\text{Optimal } \lambda: \text{ minimizes Bias}^2 + \text{Variance (via CV)}$$

4. Best Practices

  • ALWAYS standardize features before Ridge/LASSO (otherwise penalty is unfair)
  • Don't regularize the intercept (most implementations exclude it by default)
  • Select $\lambda$ via CV (typically 5-fold or 10-fold)
  • Use regularization path to see how coefficients change with $\lambda$
  • LASSO for interpretability: Resulting sparse model is easier to explain
  • Elastic Net when features are correlated: LASSO arbitrarily selects one from a group
Common Mistakes:
  • Not standardizing → features with large scale get penalized less
  • Thinking Ridge does feature selection (it doesn't — all coefficients stay non-zero)
  • Using too large $\lambda$ → all coefficients near zero → underfitting
