  • Forgetting that LASSO has no closed-form solution
  • Comparing Ridge and LASSO coefficients without same $\lambda$ scale

5. Example Problems (Solved Exam Questions)

Problem 1: LASSO Regression and Dimensionality Reduction [End-Term Q1b]

Statement: "A LASSO regression model effectively yields a modified, lower dimensional feature space." TRUE or FALSE? Explain how LASSO's L1 penalty achieves feature selection (automatic variable selection) and why Ridge regression (L2 penalty) cannot do the same.

Answer: TRUE.

Recall: LASSO (Least Absolute Shrinkage and Selection Operator) minimizes:

$$\hat{\beta}_{\text{LASSO}} = \arg\min_\beta \left\{ \|y - X\beta\|^2 + \lambda\sum_{j=1}^p |\beta_j| \right\}$$

The key feature is the L1 penalty $\lambda\sum|\beta_j|$.

Why LASSO reduces dimensionality:

  1. The L1 penalty can drive coefficients to exactly zero ($\hat{\beta}_j = 0$)
  2. Features with zero coefficients are effectively removed from the model
  3. If the original model has $p$ features and LASSO zeros out $k$ of them, the effective feature space has dimension $p - k$

Why L1 gives exact zeros but L2 (Ridge) does not:

  • L1 geometry: The L1 penalty has non-differentiable "corners" at zero. The subgradient optimality condition allows the solution to "stick" at zero when the gradient of the loss is small enough.
  • L2 geometry: The L2 penalty $\lambda\beta_j^2$ is smooth everywhere. Its gradient $2\lambda\beta_j$ approaches zero as $\beta_j \to 0$, so it always pushes coefficients TOWARD zero but never quite reaches it. Ridge shrinks but never selects.

Practical implication: Use LASSO when you believe many features are irrelevant and want the model to automatically identify the important ones. The resulting sparse model is more interpretable.

Problem 2: Choosing Regularization for Correlated Features

Question: You have a dataset with 10 features, where features naturally come in 5 pairs of highly correlated variables (e.g., height in cm and height in inches). Which regularization method should you use: Ridge (L2), LASSO (L1), or Elastic Net? Explain the behavior of each method when features are correlated.

Recall: Correlated features create a challenge for regularization because they carry redundant information. The regularizer must decide how to distribute coefficients among correlated features.

Answer: Elastic Net (or Ridge if no sparsity is needed)

Why each method behaves differently with correlated features:

  • LASSO's problem (L1): Among a group of correlated features, LASSO arbitrarily picks ONE and zeros out the others. This is unstable — different random train/test splits may select different features from the group. The selected feature is essentially random, which hurts interpretability and stability.
  • Ridge (L2): Distributes weight EQUALLY among all correlated features (gives them similar coefficients). This is stable and predictable, but Ridge never produces exact zeros, so you don't get a sparse/interpretable model.
  • Elastic Net ($\alpha \cdot L1 + (1-\alpha) \cdot L2$): The L2 component creates a "grouping effect" — correlated features receive similar coefficients. The L1 component still provides sparsity (may zero out entire groups if they're irrelevant). Best of both worlds for correlated features.

Rule of thumb: If you suspect feature correlations, prefer Elastic Net over pure LASSO.

Problem 3: Effect of Regularization Strength $\lambda$ on Bias-Variance Tradeoff

Question: In Ridge regression $\hat{\beta}_{\text{Ridge}} = \arg\min \{\text{RSS} + \lambda\|\beta\|^2\}$, how does increasing the regularization parameter $\lambda$ affect: (a) the model coefficients, (b) bias, (c) variance, (d) training error, and (e) test error? What is the optimal $\lambda$ and how do you find it?

Recall: $\lambda$ controls the strength of the penalty. $\lambda = 0$ gives OLS (no regularization). $\lambda \to \infty$ forces all coefficients toward zero (maximum regularization).

How increasing $\lambda$ changes the model (step by step):

$\lambda$ valueCoefficientsBiasVarianceTrain ErrorTest Error
$\lambda = 0$ (OLS)Unrestricted (large)LowHighLowMay be high (overfitting)
$\lambda$ moderateShrunk toward zeroModerateModerateModerateLowest (sweet spot)
$\lambda \to \infty$$\approx 0$ (all suppressed)High (model $\approx \bar{y}$)$\approx 0$HighHigh (underfitting)

Intuition: Regularization trades increased bias for reduced variance. At the sweet spot, the variance reduction outweighs the bias increase, giving the best generalization.

Finding optimal $\lambda$: Use K-fold cross-validation (typically 5 or 10 folds). Try a grid of $\lambda$ values (often logarithmically spaced, e.g., $10^{-4}, 10^{-3}, \ldots, 10^4$), evaluate CV error for each, and pick the $\lambda$ with lowest mean CV error.

Problem 4: Why You Must Standardize Features Before Regularization

Question: Unlike OLS (which is scale-invariant), Ridge and LASSO regression produce DIFFERENT results depending on feature scaling. Why is feature standardization (zero mean, unit variance) essential before applying regularized regression? Give a concrete example showing what goes wrong without standardization.

Recall: The Ridge penalty is $\lambda\|\beta\|_2^2 = \lambda(\beta_1^2 + \beta_2^2 + \ldots + \beta_p^2)$. It penalizes ALL coefficients equally, regardless of what units each feature is measured in.

The problem (concrete example):

Suppose $x_1$ = distance in meters and $x_2$ = distance in kilometers, measuring the same physical relationship with $y$:

  • $\beta_1$ (meters) would be ~0.001 (small coefficient for large-scale feature)
  • $\beta_2$ (km) would be ~1.0 (larger coefficient for smaller-scale feature)
  • The penalty $\lambda(\beta_1^2 + \beta_2^2)$ hits $\beta_2$ much harder than $\beta_1$
  • This is arbitrary and unfair — the two features carry the same information!

After standardization (mean=0, std=1):

All features are on the same scale → penalty treats all equally → fair regularization that penalizes true complexity, not arbitrary units.

Important distinction:

  • OLS: Predictions DON'T change with feature scaling (the model compensates by adjusting coefficients)
  • Ridge/LASSO: Predictions DO change because the penalty interacts with scale. Scaling determines which features get penalized more!

Practical rule: ALWAYS standardize features before regularized regression. Most implementations (e.g., sklearn's Ridge/Lasso) have a `normalize` or `StandardScaler` option for this.

Topic 8: Probabilistic Models

Covers: Logistic Regression, Gaussian Discriminant Analysis (LDA/QDA), Naive Bayes, Generative vs Discriminative Models

What are Probabilistic Models? Instead of just predicting "cat" or "dog," probabilistic classifiers say "I'm 87% confident this is a cat." They model the PROBABILITY of each class, which gives you:

  • Confidence scores (not just hard decisions)
  • A principled way to handle uncertainty
  • The ability to set custom thresholds (e.g., "only flag as fraud if >95% sure")

Two philosophies:

  • Discriminative (Logistic Regression): Directly learns the boundary between classes — "what separates cats from dogs?"
  • Generative (GDA, Naive Bayes): Learns what each class "looks like" separately, then uses Bayes' theorem — "what does a typical cat look like? A typical dog? Which does this look more like?"

1. Basic Understanding

1.1 Generative vs Discriminative

AspectDiscriminativeGenerative
Models$P(y|\mathbf{x})$ directly$P(\mathbf{x}|y)$ and $P(y)$, uses Bayes' rule
ExamplesLogistic Regression, SVMGDA (LDA/QDA), Naive Bayes
AdvantagesOften better accuracy with large $n$Works with small data, can generate samples
AssumptionsFewer distributional assumptionsStrong distributional assumptions

1.2 Logistic Regression

What is Logistic Regression? Despite the name, it's a CLASSIFICATION algorithm (not regression!). It takes the linear regression formula ($\theta^T\mathbf{x}$) and squishes it through a sigmoid function to get a probability between 0 and 1. It answers: "What's the probability this belongs to class 1?"

Analogy: Linear regression gives you any number (−∞ to +∞). The sigmoid acts like a "confidence converter" — very negative values → near 0% confidence, very positive → near 100%, zero → 50/50.

Model:

$$P(y=1|\mathbf{x};\theta) = \sigma(\theta^T\mathbf{x}) = \frac{1}{1 + e^{-\theta^T\mathbf{x}}}$$

where $\sigma(z) = \frac{1}{1+e^{-z}}$ is the sigmoid/logistic function.

Properties of sigmoid:

  • $\sigma(z) \in (0,1)$ for all $z$
  • $\sigma(0) = 0.5$
  • $\sigma(-z) = 1 - \sigma(z)$
  • $\sigma'(z) = \sigma(z)(1-\sigma(z))$

Log-odds (logit):

$$\log\frac{P(y=1|\mathbf{x})}{P(y=0|\mathbf{x})} = \theta^T\mathbf{x}$$

Linear in features → decision boundary is a hyperplane at $\theta^T\mathbf{x} = 0$

Cost Function (Cross-Entropy / Log-Loss)

$$J(\theta) = -\frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n\left[y_i\log h_\theta(x_i) + (1-y_i)\log(1-h_\theta(x_i))\right]$$

where $h_\theta(x) = \sigma(\theta^Tx)$

For single sample:

$$\text{cost}(h_\theta(x), y) = \begin{cases}-\log(h_\theta(x)) & \text{if } y=1 \\ -\log(1-h_\theta(x)) & \text{if } y=0\end{cases}$$

Gradient:

$$\frac{\partial J}{\partial \theta_j} = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n(h_\theta(x_i) - y_i)x_{ij}$$

Same form as linear regression gradient! (But $h_\theta$ is sigmoid, not linear.)

1.3 Gaussian Discriminant Analysis (GDA)

Definition (Maximum Likelihood Estimation): MLE is a parameter estimation method that finds the parameter values $\hat{\theta}$ that maximize the probability (likelihood) of observing the given data:

$$\hat{\theta}_{\text{MLE}} = \arg\max_\theta \prod_{i=1}^n P(x_i | \theta) = \arg\max_\theta \sum_{i=1}^n \log P(x_i | \theta)$$

The log-likelihood is used in practice because sums are numerically stable and easier to differentiate than products. All cost functions in this file (logistic regression's cross-entropy, GDA's parameter estimates, Naive Bayes counts) are derived from MLE.

What is GDA? A generative classifier that assumes each class's data follows a Gaussian (bell-curve) distribution. It learns the mean and spread (covariance) of each class, then classifies new points by asking "which Gaussian is this point more likely to have come from?"

LDA vs QDA:

  • LDA (Linear DA): Assumes all classes share the SAME covariance → linear decision boundary (simpler, fewer parameters)
  • QDA (Quadratic DA): Each class gets its OWN covariance → quadratic (curved) decision boundary (more flexible, more parameters)

Generative model: Assume class-conditional distributions are Gaussian:

$$P(\mathbf{x}|y=k) = \mathcal{N}(\mathbf{x}|\boldsymbol{\mu}_k, \Sigma_k)$$ $$P(y=k) = \pi_k \quad \text{(class prior)}$$

Posterior via Bayes' rule:

$$P(y=k|\mathbf{x}) = \frac{P(\mathbf{x}|y=k)P(y=k)}{\sum_j P(\mathbf{x}|y=j)P(y=j)} = \frac{\pi_k \mathcal{N}(\mathbf{x}|\mu_k,\Sigma_k)}{\sum_j \pi_j \mathcal{N}(\mathbf{x}|\mu_j,\Sigma_j)}$$

LDA (Linear Discriminant Analysis)

Assumption: All classes share same covariance: $\Sigma_1 = \Sigma_2 = \cdots = \Sigma$

Decision boundary: Linear (log-ratio is linear in $\mathbf{x}$)

MLE estimates:

$$\hat{\mu}_k = \frac{1}{n_k}\sum_{i:y_i=k}\mathbf{x}_i, \quad \hat{\pi}_k = \frac{n_k}{n}$$ $$\hat{\Sigma} = \frac{1}{n-K}\sum_{k=1}^K\sum_{i:y_i=k}(\mathbf{x}_i-\hat{\mu}_k)(\mathbf{x}_i-\hat{\mu}_k)^T$$

(pooled within-class covariance)

QDA (Quadratic Discriminant Analysis)

Assumption: Each class has its own covariance: $\Sigma_k$ (different per class)

Decision boundary: Quadratic (conic sections — ellipses, parabolas, hyperbolas)

More parameters: Each class needs $d(d+1)/2$ covariance parameters → needs more data

1.4 Naive Bayes

What is Naive Bayes? A super-simple yet surprisingly effective classifier. It assumes all features are INDEPENDENT given the class (the "naive" assumption). Each feature "votes" independently for each class, and the class with the most votes wins.

Analogy: Imagine classifying emails as spam/not-spam. Naive Bayes looks at each word independently: "money" → votes spam, "meeting" → votes not-spam, "free" → votes spam. It multiplies all the votes together. Despite the naive assumption (words ARE correlated!), this works surprisingly well in practice.

Why "naive"? Because features are almost never truly independent. But the simplification makes computation extremely fast and works well with small datasets.

Key assumption: Features are conditionally independent given class:

$$P(\mathbf{x}|y=k) = \prod_{j=1}^d P(x_j|y=k)$$

Posterior:

$$P(y=k|\mathbf{x}) \propto P(y=k)\prod_{j=1}^d P(x_j|y=k)$$

Classification rule (MAP):

$$\hat{y} = \arg\max_k P(y=k)\prod_{j=1}^d P(x_j|y=k)$$

In log space:

$$\hat{y} = \arg\max_k \left[\log P(y=k) + \sum_{j=1}^d \log P(x_j|y=k)\right]$$

Types of Naive Bayes

Feature typeModel for $P(x_j|y)$Example
CategoricalMultinomial/Categorical distribution$P(x_j = \text{value}|y=k) = \frac{\text{count}}{n_k}$
BinaryBernoulli$P(x_j=1|y=k) = p_{jk}$
ContinuousGaussian$P(x_j|y=k) = \mathcal{N}(x_j|\mu_{jk},\sigma_{jk}^2)$

2. When to Use

ModelUse WhenLimitations
Logistic RegressionBinary classification baseline, interpretability, approximately linear boundaryCan't capture complex non-linear boundaries (without feature engineering)
LDAClasses roughly Gaussian with equal covariance, small datasetLinear boundary only, sensitive to Gaussian assumption
QDADifferent class covariances, enough data per class$O(d^2)$ parameters per class
Naive BayesVery small data, text classification, many features, fast baselineIndependence assumption rarely holds, poor probability calibration

3. All Formulae

Logistic Regression

$$h_\theta(x) = \sigma(\theta^Tx) = \frac{1}{1+e^{-\theta^Tx}}$$ $$J(\theta) = -\frac{1}{n}\sum_i[y_i\log h_\theta(x_i) + (1-y_i)\log(1-h_\theta(x_i))]$$ $$\nabla_\theta J = \frac{1}{n}X^T(h_\theta(X) - \mathbf{y})$$ $$\text{Decision boundary: } \theta^T\mathbf{x} = 0$$

Multivariate Gaussian

$$\mathcal{N}(\mathbf{x}|\mu,\Sigma) = \frac{1}{(2\pi)^{d/2}|\Sigma|^{1/2}}\exp\left(-\frac{1}{2}(\mathbf{x}-\mu)^T\Sigma^{-1}(\mathbf{x}-\mu)\right)$$

GDA Posterior (2 classes)

$$P(y=1|\mathbf{x}) = \frac{\pi_1\mathcal{N}(\mathbf{x}|\mu_1,\Sigma_1)}{\pi_0\mathcal{N}(\mathbf{x}|\mu_0,\Sigma_0) + \pi_1\mathcal{N}(\mathbf{x}|\mu_1,\Sigma_1)}$$

LDA Discriminant Function

$$\delta_k(\mathbf{x}) = \mathbf{x}^T\Sigma^{-1}\mu_k - \frac{1}{2}\mu_k^T\Sigma^{-1}\mu_k + \log\pi_k$$ $$\text{Classify to: } \arg\max_k \delta_k(\mathbf{x})$$

Naive Bayes (Categorical)

$$P(y=k|\mathbf{x}) = \frac{\pi_k\prod_j P(x_j|y=k)}{\sum_c \pi_c\prod_j P(x_j|y=c)}$$ $$P(x_j = v|y=k) = \frac{n_{k,j,v}}{n_k} \quad \text{(with Laplace smoothing: } \frac{n_{k,j,v}+1}{n_k+V_j}\text{)}$$

4. Best Practices

  • Logistic Regression: Use regularization (L2 by default in sklearn). Standardize features. Check calibration.
  • LDA: Check if equal covariance assumption holds (if not, use QDA). Good when classes are well-separated.
  • Naive Bayes: Use Laplace smoothing to avoid zero probabilities. Good for text/spam classification.
  • GDA vs Logistic: If Gaussian assumption holds, GDA reaches optimal faster (fewer samples). If not, Logistic is more robust.
  • When computing posteriors numerically, use log-sum-exp trick to avoid underflow.

5. Example Problems (Solved Exam Questions)

Problem 1: Logistic Regression — Hypothesis and Cost Computation [End-Term Q8]

Question: Consider a binary classification problem with the logistic regression hypothesis:

$$h_\theta(x) = \frac{1}{1 + e^{-\theta^T x}}$$

Given parameter vector $\theta = \begin{bmatrix}0.5 \\ -0.25\end{bmatrix}$, input vector $x = \begin{bmatrix}1 \\ 2\end{bmatrix}$, and true label $y = 1$:

(a) Compute $h_\theta(x)$.
(b) Calculate the logistic regression cost (log-loss) for this example.

Recall: The logistic regression hypothesis $h_\theta(x) = \sigma(\theta^Tx)$ outputs the predicted probability that $y = 1$. The sigmoid function $\sigma(z) = \frac{1}{1+e^{-z}}$ maps any real number to $(0, 1)$. The cost function (log-loss / cross-entropy) for a single example is:

$$\text{cost}(h_\theta(x), y) = \begin{cases}-\log(h_\theta(x)) & \text{if } y=1 \\ -\log(1-h_\theta(x)) & \text{if } y=0\end{cases}$$

Or equivalently: $J = -y\log(h_\theta(x)) - (1-y)\log(1-h_\theta(x))$

Part (a): Compute $h_\theta(x)$

Step 1: Compute the linear combination $\theta^Tx$ (the "logit")

This is the dot product of the parameter vector with the input:

$$\theta^Tx = \theta_1 \cdot x_1 + \theta_2 \cdot x_2 = 0.5 \times 1 + (-0.25) \times 2 = 0.5 - 0.5 = 0$$

Step 2: Apply the sigmoid function

The sigmoid squishes the logit into a probability between 0 and 1:

$$h_\theta(x) = \sigma(\theta^Tx) = \sigma(0) = \frac{1}{1+e^{-0}} = \frac{1}{1+1} = \boxed{0.5}$$

Interpretation: The model predicts a 50% probability of class 1 — this is maximum uncertainty. The model "can't decide."

Part (b): Calculate the logistic regression cost

Step 3: Apply the cost function for $y = 1$

Since the true label is $y = 1$, we use $\text{cost} = -\log(h_\theta(x))$:

$$J(\theta) = -\log(h_\theta(x)) = -\log(0.5) = -(-\ln 2) = \ln(2) \approx \boxed{0.693}$$

Interpretation: The cost is $\ln(2) \approx 0.693$, which is relatively high (the maximum possible cost for $y=1$ is $\infty$ when $h \to 0$, and the minimum is 0 when $h = 1$). This makes sense — the model is completely uncertain (50/50 prediction), yet the correct answer is definitely class 1. A well-trained model should output $h_\theta(x)$ close to 1 for $y=1$ examples, giving cost close to 0.

Summary:

QuantityValueMeaning
$\theta^Tx$0Logit (raw score) — right at the decision boundary
$h_\theta(x)$0.5Predicted probability of class 1 — maximum uncertainty
Cost $J(\theta)$$\ln 2 \approx 0.693$High cost — model needs better parameters

Problem 2: Gaussian Discriminant Analysis — Likelihoods and Posterior [End-Term Q9]

Question: Consider a binary classification problem with scalar feature $x \in \mathbb{R}$ modeled by Gaussian Discriminant Analysis (GDA). The class-conditional densities are:

$$p(x|y=0) = \mathcal{N}(1, 1), \quad p(x|y=1) = \mathcal{N}(3, 1)$$

and the class priors are:

$$P(y=1) = 0.4, \quad P(y=0) = 0.6$$

Given an observed feature value $x = 2$:

(a) Compute the likelihoods $p(x=2|y=0)$ and $p(x=2|y=1)$.
(b) Compute the posterior probability $P(y=1|x=2)$.

Recall: In GDA, each class has a Gaussian (normal) distribution. The 1D Gaussian PDF is:

$$p(x|\mu, \sigma^2) = \frac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi\sigma^2}} \exp\left(-\frac{(x-\mu)^2}{2\sigma^2}\right)$$

We use Bayes' theorem to compute the posterior: $P(y=k|x) = \frac{p(x|y=k) \cdot P(y=k)}{p(x)}$

Part (a): Compute the likelihoods

Step 1: Compute $p(x=2|y=0)$ from $\mathcal{N}(\mu=1, \sigma^2=1)$

Class 0 has mean $\mu_0 = 1$ and variance $\sigma^2 = 1$. The point $x=2$ is 1 standard deviation away from the mean:

$$p(x=2|y=0) = \frac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi \cdot 1}} \exp\left(-\frac{(2-1)^2}{2 \cdot 1}\right) = \frac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi}} \exp\left(-\frac{1}{2}\right)$$ $$= \frac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi}} \cdot e^{-0.5} = \frac{1}{2.5066} \cdot 0.6065 = \boxed{0.2420}$$

Step 2: Compute $p(x=2|y=1)$ from $\mathcal{N}(\mu=3, \sigma^2=1)$

Class 1 has mean $\mu_1 = 3$ and variance $\sigma^2 = 1$. The point $x=2$ is also 1 standard deviation away from this mean:

$$p(x=2|y=1) = \frac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi \cdot 1}} \exp\left(-\frac{(2-3)^2}{2 \cdot 1}\right) = \frac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi}} \exp\left(-\frac{1}{2}\right)$$ $$= \frac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi}} \cdot e^{-0.5} = \boxed{0.2420}$$

Key observation: Both likelihoods are EQUAL! This is because $x = 2$ is exactly equidistant from $\mu_0 = 1$ and $\mu_1 = 3$ (both are 1 unit away), and both classes have the same variance $\sigma^2 = 1$.

Part (b): Compute the posterior $P(y=1|x=2)$

Step 3: Apply Bayes' theorem

$$P(y=1|x=2) = \frac{p(x=2|y=1) \cdot P(y=1)}{p(x=2|y=0) \cdot P(y=0) + p(x=2|y=1) \cdot P(y=1)}$$

Step 4: Substitute values

$$= \frac{0.2420 \times 0.4}{0.2420 \times 0.6 + 0.2420 \times 0.4}$$

Since the likelihoods are equal (both 0.2420), they factor out and cancel:

$$= \frac{0.2420 \times 0.4}{0.2420 \times (0.6 + 0.4)} = \frac{0.4}{1.0} = \boxed{0.4}$$

Interpretation: The posterior $P(y=1|x=2) = 0.4$ is exactly equal to the prior $P(y=1) = 0.4$! This makes perfect sense: the observation $x=2$ provides NO discriminating information because it is equally likely under both classes (equal likelihoods). When the data is uninformative, Bayes' theorem simply returns the prior. The classification decision would be $\hat{y} = 0$ (since $P(y=0|x) = 0.6 > 0.4$), driven entirely by the prior class imbalance.

General principle: Posterior = Prior when likelihoods are equal. The data only updates our belief when one class makes the observation more probable than the other.

Problem 3: Naive Bayes Classification — Play Tennis [End-Term Q10]

Question: Given a dataset with two features: Weather (Sunny, Rainy) and Temperature (Hot, Mild), and a binary class label: Play Tennis (Yes or No). Assume the Naive Bayes classifier assumption that features are conditionally independent given the class.

WeatherTemperaturePlay Tennis
SunnyHotNo
SunnyHotNo
RainyMildYes
SunnyMildYes
RainyHotYes

(a) Explain why Naive Bayes assumes independence between Weather and Temperature given Play Tennis, and discuss one limitation of this assumption.

(b) Calculate the posterior probabilities $P(\text{Yes} | \text{Sunny, Mild})$ and $P(\text{No} | \text{Sunny, Mild})$. Which class is predicted?

Recall: Naive Bayes classifies using Bayes' theorem with the "naive" conditional independence assumption:

$$P(y|\mathbf{x}) \propto P(y) \cdot \prod_{j=1}^d P(x_j|y)$$

The "naive" part: we assume $P(x_1, x_2 | y) = P(x_1|y) \cdot P(x_2|y)$ — features are independent WITHIN each class.

Part (a): The Independence Assumption

What it means: Naive Bayes assumes that, given the class label (Yes or No), knowing the Weather tells you NOTHING about the Temperature (and vice versa). Mathematically:

$$P(\text{Weather, Temperature} | \text{Play Tennis}) = P(\text{Weather} | \text{Play Tennis}) \cdot P(\text{Temperature} | \text{Play Tennis})$$

This allows us to estimate each feature's probability separately, which dramatically reduces the number of parameters to learn.

One limitation: In reality, Weather and Temperature are often correlated (e.g., Sunny days tend to be Hot, Rainy days tend to be Mild). By assuming independence, Naive Bayes ignores this correlation and may "double count" evidence — a (Sunny, Hot) combination would get probability $P(\text{Sunny}|y) \cdot P(\text{Hot}|y)$, which may overestimate the joint probability if Sunny and Hot are redundant signals. Despite this, Naive Bayes often works well in practice because the classification decision only needs the RANKING of posteriors to be correct, not their exact values.

Part (b): Compute posteriors for (Weather=Sunny, Temperature=Mild)

Step 1: Count class frequencies to get priors

From the 5 training examples:

  • $P(\text{Yes}) = 3/5$ (3 rows with Play Tennis = Yes)
  • $P(\text{No}) = 2/5$ (2 rows with Play Tennis = No)

Step 2: Compute conditional probabilities for each feature value

Among the "Yes" class (3 examples: Rainy-Mild, Sunny-Mild, Rainy-Hot):

  • $P(\text{Sunny}|\text{Yes}) = 1/3$ (only 1 of the 3 Yes-examples is Sunny)
  • $P(\text{Mild}|\text{Yes}) = 2/3$ (2 of the 3 Yes-examples are Mild)

Among the "No" class (2 examples: Sunny-Hot, Sunny-Hot):

  • $P(\text{Sunny}|\text{No}) = 2/2 = 1$ (both No-examples are Sunny)
  • $P(\text{Mild}|\text{No}) = 0/2 = 0$ (neither No-example is Mild)

Step 3: Compute unnormalized posteriors using the Naive Bayes formula

For class "Yes":

$$P(\text{Yes}|\text{Sunny, Mild}) \propto P(\text{Yes}) \cdot P(\text{Sunny}|\text{Yes}) \cdot P(\text{Mild}|\text{Yes})$$ $$= \frac{3}{5} \cdot \frac{1}{3} \cdot \frac{2}{3} = \frac{3}{5} \cdot \frac{2}{9} = \frac{6}{45} = \frac{2}{15} \approx 0.1333$$

For class "No":

$$P(\text{No}|\text{Sunny, Mild}) \propto P(\text{No}) \cdot P(\text{Sunny}|\text{No}) \cdot P(\text{Mild}|\text{No})$$ $$= \frac{2}{5} \cdot 1 \cdot 0 = \boxed{0}$$

Step 4: Normalize to get final posteriors

$$P(\text{Yes}|\text{Sunny, Mild}) = \frac{2/15}{2/15 + 0} = \frac{2/15}{2/15} = \boxed{1.0}$$ $$P(\text{No}|\text{Sunny, Mild}) = \frac{0}{2/15 + 0} = \boxed{0.0}$$

Prediction: $\boxed{\text{Yes}}$ — Play Tennis

Interpretation: The model predicts "Yes" with 100% confidence. Why? Because in the training data, EVERY "No" example had Temperature=Hot. There is NO "No" example with Mild temperature, so $P(\text{Mild}|\text{No}) = 0$, which makes the entire "No" posterior zero. The model has essentially learned: "If it's Mild, it's always Yes."

Zero-probability problem: Having $P(\text{Mild}|\text{No}) = 0$ is a known issue with Naive Bayes on small datasets. A single unseen feature-class combination makes the entire posterior zero. The fix is Laplace smoothing: add a small count (usually 1) to every feature-class combination: $$P(\text{Mild}|\text{No}) = \frac{0 + 1}{2 + 2} = \frac{1}{4} = 0.25$$ (adding 1 to numerator, number-of-categories to denominator). With smoothing, "No" would get a small nonzero posterior, giving a less extreme prediction.

Problem 4: Logistic Regression — Decision Boundary

Question: In logistic regression, the decision boundary is the set of points where $P(y=1|\mathbf{x}) = 0.5$, which occurs when $\theta^T\mathbf{x} = 0$. Given the augmented parameter vector $\theta = [1, 2, -3]^T$ (where the first element is the bias/intercept term), find the equation of the decision boundary and describe what it looks like geometrically.

Recall: In logistic regression, we predict $P(y=1) = \sigma(\theta^T\mathbf{x})$. Since $\sigma(0) = 0.5$, the decision boundary is where $\theta^T\mathbf{x} = 0$. With augmented notation, $\mathbf{x} = [1, x_1, x_2]^T$ (first entry is always 1 for the bias).

Step 1: Write out the decision boundary equation $\theta^T\mathbf{x} = 0$

$$\theta^T\mathbf{x} = \theta_0 \cdot 1 + \theta_1 \cdot x_1 + \theta_2 \cdot x_2 = 1 + 2x_1 - 3x_2 = 0$$

Step 2: Solve for $x_2$ in terms of $x_1$ (standard line equation)

$$3x_2 = 1 + 2x_1$$ $$x_2 = \frac{1}{3} + \frac{2}{3}x_1$$

Step 3: Interpret geometrically

This is a straight line in the $x_1$-$x_2$ plane with slope $2/3$ and $x_2$-intercept $1/3$.

  • Points where $1 + 2x_1 - 3x_2 > 0$ → predict $y = 1$ (above sigmoid midpoint)
  • Points where $1 + 2x_1 - 3x_2 < 0$ → predict $y = 0$ (below sigmoid midpoint)
  • Points exactly ON the line → $P(y=1) = 0.5$ (maximum uncertainty)

Key takeaway: Logistic regression ALWAYS produces a linear decision boundary (a line in 2D, a plane in 3D, a hyperplane in general). This is why it's called a "linear classifier" even though it uses the nonlinear sigmoid function.

Problem 5: LDA vs QDA — When to Use Which

Question: Linear Discriminant Analysis (LDA) and Quadratic Discriminant Analysis (QDA) are both generative classifiers that model class-conditional distributions as Gaussians. What is the key difference between them, and when would you choose QDA over LDA?

Recall: LDA assumes all classes share the SAME covariance matrix $\Sigma$ → produces a linear decision boundary. QDA allows each class to have its OWN covariance $\Sigma_k$ → produces a quadratic (curved) decision boundary.

Choose QDA over LDA when:

  • Different class shapes: Classes have visibly different spreads or orientations (e.g., one class is round, another is elongated)
  • Sufficient data: You have enough training data per class — QDA estimates $d(d+1)/2$ parameters per class (full covariance matrix), so you need $n_k \gg d^2$
  • Non-linear boundary needed: A straight line/plane clearly can't separate the classes well

Choose LDA over QDA when:

  • Similar covariances: Classes appear to have similar spread/shape
  • Limited training data: Fewer parameters to estimate = less overfitting risk
  • Linear boundary suffices: Classes are separated well by a hyperplane

Analogy: LDA is to QDA as linear regression is to polynomial regression — simpler model with fewer parameters, lower variance but potentially higher bias. It's the classic bias-variance tradeoff applied to generative classifiers.

Problem 6: Logistic Regression — Gradient Update [Practice Q8]

Question: Model: $h_\theta(x) = \frac{1}{1+e^{-(\theta_0 + \theta_1 x)}}$. Training example $(x, y) = (1, 0)$, current parameters $\theta_0 = 0, \theta_1 = 1$.

(a) Compute $h_\theta(1)$. (b) Compute the binary cross-entropy loss. (c) Write the gradient update for $\theta_1$ with $\eta = 0.5$ and compute the new $\theta_1$.

Recall: Binary cross-entropy loss for one example: $L = -[y\ln h + (1-y)\ln(1-h)]$. Gradient: $\frac{\partial L}{\partial \theta_j} = (h_\theta(x) - y) \cdot x_j$

(a) Predicted probability:

$$h_\theta(1) = \frac{1}{1 + e^{-(0 + 1 \cdot 1)}} = \frac{1}{1 + e^{-1}} = \frac{1}{1 + 0.3679} = \boxed{0.7311}$$

The model predicts 73% probability of class 1, but the true label is $y = 0$. The model is quite wrong!

(b) Cross-entropy loss:

Since $y = 0$: $L = -[\cancel{y\ln h} + (1-0)\ln(1 - h)]= -\ln(1 - 0.7311) = -\ln(0.2689) = \boxed{1.313}$

This is a high loss (compare: perfect prediction would give $L = -\ln(1) = 0$).

(c) Gradient update for $\theta_1$:

Update rule: $\theta_1^{\text{new}} = \theta_1 - \eta \cdot (h_\theta(x) - y) \cdot x$

$$\theta_1^{\text{new}} = 1 - 0.5 \times (0.7311 - 0) \times 1 = 1 - 0.3656 = \boxed{0.6344}$$

The weight decreased — making sense because the model was too confident about class 1 when the truth was class 0. Reducing $\theta_1$ lowers the output for positive $x$ values.

Problem 7: Decision Boundary in Logistic Regression [Practice Q9]

Question: (a) Show that $h_\theta(x) = 0.5$ corresponds to $\theta^T x = 0$. (b) For $\theta = [-3, 1, 2]^T$ (with $x_0 = 1$ bias), write the decision boundary equation. (c) Predict the class for $x = [1, 0, 2]^T$.

(a) Derivation:

$$h_\theta(x) = 0.5 \implies \frac{1}{1 + e^{-\theta^T x}} = 0.5 \implies 1 + e^{-\theta^T x} = 2 \implies e^{-\theta^T x} = 1 \implies -\theta^T x = 0$$ $$\therefore \boxed{\theta^T x = 0}$$

The decision boundary is the set of all points where $\theta^T x = 0$. On one side ($\theta^T x > 0$) we predict class 1; on the other ($\theta^T x < 0$) we predict class 0.

(b) Decision boundary equation:

With $\theta = [-3, 1, 2]^T$ and $x = [1, x_1, x_2]^T$ (bias appended):

$$\theta^T x = -3(1) + 1(x_1) + 2(x_2) = 0$$ $$\boxed{x_1 + 2x_2 = 3} \quad \text{or equivalently } x_2 = \frac{3 - x_1}{2} = 1.5 - 0.5x_1$$

This is a line in the $x_1$-$x_2$ plane with slope $-0.5$ and $x_2$-intercept $1.5$.

(c) Predict class for $x = [1, 0, 2]^T$:

$$\theta^T x = -3(1) + 1(0) + 2(2) = -3 + 0 + 4 = 1 > 0$$ $$h_\theta(x) = \sigma(1) = \frac{1}{1+e^{-1}} \approx 0.731 > 0.5$$

Predict class $\boxed{1}$

Problem 8: GDA — MLE Estimates and Classification [Practice Q10]

Question: GDA model: $y \sim \text{Bernoulli}(\phi)$, $x|y=k \sim N(\mu_k, \Sigma)$ with shared covariance.

(a) Write MLE estimates for $\phi, \mu_0, \mu_1$. (b) Compute parameters for: Class 0: $\{1, 3\}$, Class 1: $\{5, 7\}$. (c) Classify $x = 4$.

(a) MLE estimates:

$$\hat{\phi} = \frac{n_1}{n}, \quad \hat{\mu}_0 = \frac{1}{n_0}\sum_{i: y_i=0} x_i, \quad \hat{\mu}_1 = \frac{1}{n_1}\sum_{i: y_i=1} x_i$$ $$\hat{\Sigma} = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n (x_i - \mu_{y_i})(x_i - \mu_{y_i})^T$$

(b) Compute from data:

$n = 4$, $n_0 = 2$ (samples 1, 3), $n_1 = 2$ (samples 5, 7)

$$\hat{\phi} = \frac{2}{4} = 0.5$$ $$\hat{\mu}_0 = \frac{1+3}{2} = 2, \quad \hat{\mu}_1 = \frac{5+7}{2} = 6$$ $$\hat{\sigma}^2 = \frac{1}{4}\left[(1-2)^2 + (3-2)^2 + (5-6)^2 + (7-6)^2\right] = \frac{1+1+1+1}{4} = \boxed{1}$$

(c) Classify $x = 4$:

Compute likelihoods:

$$p(x=4|y=0) = N(4; 2, 1) = \frac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi}}e^{-(4-2)^2/2} = \frac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi}}e^{-2} \approx 0.0540$$ $$p(x=4|y=1) = N(4; 6, 1) = \frac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi}}e^{-(4-6)^2/2} = \frac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi}}e^{-2} \approx 0.0540$$

Posteriors: $P(y=0|x) \propto 0.5 \times 0.0540 = 0.027$, $P(y=1|x) \propto 0.5 \times 0.0540 = 0.027$

$x = 4$ is EXACTLY on the decision boundary! Both posteriors are equal.

This makes geometric sense: with equal priors and equal variances, the GDA boundary is at $\frac{\mu_0 + \mu_1}{2} = \frac{2+6}{2} = 4$. Either class could be assigned (typically class 0 by convention).

Problem 9: Naive Bayes Spam Classifier + Laplace Smoothing [Practice Q11]

Question: Training data for spam classification based on whether "free" appears:

"free" appears?Class
YesSpam
YesSpam
NoSpam
NoNot Spam
NoNot Spam
YesNot Spam

(a) Estimate priors and likelihoods. (b) Classify a new email containing "free". (c) Explain zero-probability problem and Laplace smoothing.

(a) MLE estimates:

Spam emails: 3 total (Yes, Yes, No). Not Spam: 3 total (No, No, Yes).

$$P(\text{Spam}) = \frac{3}{6} = 0.5, \quad P(\text{Not Spam}) = 0.5$$ $$P(\text{"free"=Yes}|\text{Spam}) = \frac{2}{3}, \quad P(\text{"free"=Yes}|\text{Not Spam}) = \frac{1}{3}$$

(b) Classify email with "free"=Yes:

$$P(\text{Spam}|\text{Yes}) \propto P(\text{Yes}|\text{Spam}) \cdot P(\text{Spam}) = \frac{2}{3} \times \frac{1}{2} = \frac{1}{3}$$ $$P(\text{Not Spam}|\text{Yes}) \propto P(\text{Yes}|\text{Not Spam}) \cdot P(\text{Not Spam}) = \frac{1}{3} \times \frac{1}{2} = \frac{1}{6}$$

Normalize: total = $\frac{1}{3} + \frac{1}{6} = \frac{1}{2}$

$$P(\text{Spam}|\text{Yes}) = \frac{1/3}{1/2} = \boxed{\frac{2}{3} \approx 0.667}$$ $$P(\text{Not Spam}|\text{Yes}) = \frac{1/6}{1/2} = \frac{1}{3} \approx 0.333$$

Predict: Spam (2/3 > 1/3)

(c) Zero-probability problem and Laplace smoothing:

Problem: If "free" appears in ZERO spam emails, then $P(\text{"free"=Yes}|\text{Spam}) = 0$. This zeros out the ENTIRE posterior: $P(\text{Spam}|\text{"free"=Yes}) \propto 0 \times P(\text{Spam}) = 0$, regardless of all other features. One missing count completely dominates the prediction.

Laplace smoothing (add-$\alpha$): Add a pseudocount $\alpha$ (typically $\alpha = 1$) to each count:

$$P(\text{feature}=v|\text{class}) = \frac{\text{count}(v, \text{class}) + \alpha}{\text{count(class)} + \alpha \cdot V}$$

where $V$ = number of possible values for that feature. This ensures no probability is ever exactly zero while asymptotically approaching MLE as data grows.

Problem 10: Logistic Regression vs GDA — When to Use Which [Practice Q16]

Question: Classifying emails (bag-of-words $x \in \mathbb{R}^d$) as spam/not-spam.

(a) What assumption does GDA make that LR does not? (b) If GDA's assumption holds, which is better with small data? (c) For bag-of-words features, which is preferred?

(a) GDA's key assumption:

GDA assumes the class-conditional distributions are Gaussian: $p(x|y=k) \sim N(\mu_k, \Sigma)$. Logistic regression makes no distributional assumption — it directly models $P(y|x) = \sigma(\theta^T x)$ without specifying $p(x|y)$.

GDA's assumption holds exactly when features are continuous, roughly bell-shaped within each class, and have the same covariance structure across classes.

(b) With small training data and correct assumptions → GDA wins:

GDA is a generative model that uses the joint distribution $p(x, y) = p(x|y)p(y)$. By making the (correct) Gaussian assumption, it extracts MORE information per sample. It has a fixed number of parameters ($\mu_0, \mu_1, \Sigma, \phi$) estimated in closed form. With few data points, this structural assumption acts like a strong regularizer, giving lower variance estimates.

(c) For bag-of-words → Logistic Regression wins:

Bag-of-words features are sparse, discrete counts — fundamentally NOT Gaussian (they're non-negative integers with many zeros). GDA's Gaussian assumption is badly violated, leading to poor probability estimates. Logistic regression is robust to distributional misspecification since it only models $P(y|x)$ directly, making it the safer choice when the generative assumption fails.

Summary rule: Use GDA when features are continuous and approximately Gaussian (physical measurements, sensor data). Use LR when features are discrete, sparse, or you're uncertain about the distribution (text, categorical data, mixed features).

Problem 11: GDA ↔ Logistic Regression — Asymptotic Relationship [Practice Q17]

Question: Describe the relationship between GDA and logistic regression. If GDA assumptions hold exactly, which classifier achieves lower asymptotic error? What happens when assumptions do not hold?

The fundamental connection:

GDA models $P(x|y)$ as Gaussian with shared covariance $\Sigma$. Applying Bayes' rule to derive the posterior:

$$P(y=1|x) = \frac{P(x|y=1)P(y=1)}{P(x)} = \frac{1}{1 + \exp(-\theta^T x)}$$

This IS the logistic regression form. So GDA's posterior is logistic regression. But the converse is NOT true — logistic regression's form can hold without data being Gaussian.

If GDA's Gaussian assumption holds exactly:

  • Both GDA and LR converge to the same optimal decision boundary as $n \to \infty$ (same asymptotic error).
  • GDA converges faster — needs fewer samples to reach near-optimal. Its stronger assumption = fewer effective parameters = lower variance.
  • With small $n$: GDA has lower error (more statistically efficient).

If GDA's assumptions are violated (non-Gaussian data):

  • GDA is misspecified — it fits Gaussians to non-Gaussian data. Even with $n \to \infty$, it converges to the wrong boundary (biased).
  • LR still works because it only assumes the posterior is logistic (a weaker requirement). It converges to the true optimal logistic boundary regardless.
  • LR achieves lower asymptotic error when Gaussian assumption fails.
ScenarioGDALogistic Regression
Assumptions hold, small $n$Better (faster convergence)Higher variance
Assumptions hold, large $n$Same errorSame error
Assumptions violatedBiased (worse)Better (robust)
Key insight: GDA ⊂ LR (GDA is a special case of logistic regression). Stronger assumptions → faster learning but less robustness. This is the classic bias-variance tradeoff in model families.

Topic 9: Ensemble Methods

Covers: Bagging, Random Forests, Boosting (AdaBoost, Gradient Boosting), Stacking, Bias-Variance Analysis of Ensembles

The Big Idea: Wisdom of Crowds. One model might be wrong, but if you ask MANY models and take their average/vote, the errors tend to cancel out. This is why ensemble methods often win ML competitions — they're almost always better than any single model.

Real-world analogy: Ask 100 people to guess the number of jellybeans in a jar. Individual guesses vary wildly, but the AVERAGE is remarkably close to the true number. That's ensembles!

Two main strategies:

  • Bagging (parallel): Train many models independently on random subsets → reduces VARIANCE (makes predictions more stable)
  • Boosting (sequential): Train models one after another, each fixing the previous one's mistakes → reduces BIAS (makes predictions more accurate)

1. Basic Understanding

1.1 Ensemble Philosophy

Combine multiple "weak" learners to create a "strong" learner. The key insight: diverse models make different errors, and combining them reduces overall error.

Why ensembles work (variance reduction):

If $n$ models have variance $\sigma^2$ and pairwise correlation $\rho$:

$$\text{Var}(\bar{f}) = \rho\sigma^2 + \frac{1-\rho}{n}\sigma^2$$
  • If $\rho = 1$ (identical models): $\text{Var} = \sigma^2$ (no benefit)
  • If $\rho = 0$ (independent): $\text{Var} = \sigma^2/n$ (ideal!)
  • Lower correlation between models → greater variance reduction

1.2 Bagging (Bootstrap Aggregating)

How Bagging works: Create many slightly different datasets by randomly sampling WITH replacement (bootstrap). Train one model on each. For a new prediction, ask ALL models and take a vote (classification) or average (regression). Because each model sees slightly different data, they make different mistakes — averaging smooths these out.

Analogy: Instead of relying on one doctor's opinion, you get second, third, fourth opinions from doctors who each reviewed slightly different subsets of your medical history. The consensus is more reliable.

Algorithm:

  1. Create $B$ bootstrap samples (sample $n$ points with replacement from training data)
  2. Train one model on each bootstrap sample
  3. Aggregate predictions: majority vote (classification) or average (regression)

Key properties:

  • Reduces variance (does NOT reduce bias)
  • Works best with high-variance, low-bias base learners (e.g., deep trees)
  • Each bootstrap sample contains ~63.2% of unique training points (the rest are Out-of-Bag)
  • OOB error: For each point, average predictions from trees that did NOT train on it → free cross-validation estimate!

OOB fraction: $P(\text{point NOT in sample}) = (1-1/n)^n \to 1/e \approx 0.368$

1.3 Random Forests

What is a Random Forest? Bagging with decision trees PLUS an extra trick: at each split, only consider a RANDOM SUBSET of features. This forces trees to be more diverse (different trees focus on different features), which reduces correlation between them and makes the ensemble even better.

Why it's so popular: Almost no tuning needed, rarely overfits (more trees = better), handles messy real-world data, gives you feature importance for free, and consistently performs near the top on tabular data.

Random Forest = Bagging + Random Feature Selection at each split

Additional randomization: At each node split, only consider $m$ random features (not all $p$):

  • Classification: $m = \lfloor\sqrt{p}\rfloor$ (typical default)
  • Regression: $m = \lfloor p/3\rfloor$ (typical default)

Why? Reduces correlation $\rho$ between trees. Without this, all trees would split on the same strong features → high correlation → less variance reduction.

Properties:

  • Very low variance (decorrelated trees)
  • Rarely overfits (more trees = better, never worse)
  • Handles missing values, mixed feature types
  • Feature importance: permute feature $j$, measure OOB error increase

1.4 Boosting

How Boosting works: Train models SEQUENTIALLY. Each new model focuses on the examples the previous models got WRONG. It's like a student who keeps re-studying the problems they missed on practice tests.

Key difference from Bagging: Bagging trains models independently (parallel, reduces variance). Boosting trains them sequentially (each fixes predecessors' errors, reduces bias).

Philosophy: Sequentially train weak learners, each focusing on mistakes of previous ones.

Key difference from Bagging: Models are dependent (each corrects the previous). Reduces BIAS (and variance).

AdaBoost in plain English:

  1. Start: give every training point equal importance (weight)
  2. Train a simple model (e.g., a "stump" — a tree with just one split)
  3. Check which points it got WRONG → increase their weights (make them "louder")
  4. Train the next model — it will focus more on the hard (loud) examples
  5. Repeat, and combine all models with weighted voting (better models get more say)

AdaBoost

Algorithm:

  1. Initialize weights: $w_i = 1/n$ for all training points
  2. For $t = 1, \ldots, T$:
    • Train weak learner $h_t$ on weighted data
    • Compute weighted error: $\epsilon_t = \sum_{i: h_t(x_i)\neq y_i} w_i$
    • Compute learner weight: $\alpha_t = \frac{1}{2}\ln\frac{1-\epsilon_t}{\epsilon_t}$
    • Update sample weights: $w_i \leftarrow w_i \cdot \exp(-\alpha_t y_i h_t(x_i))$
    • Normalize weights so $\sum w_i = 1$
  3. Final classifier: $H(x) = \text{sign}\left(\sum_t \alpha_t h_t(x)\right)$

Key insights:

  • $\alpha_t$ is larger when $\epsilon_t$ is smaller (better learners get more vote)
  • Misclassified points get higher weight → next learner focuses on them
  • If $\epsilon_t > 0.5$: learner is worse than random → $\alpha_t < 0$ (flip it)
  • If $\epsilon_t = 0.5$: $\alpha_t = 0$ (learner contributes nothing)

Gradient Boosting in plain English: Instead of re-weighting samples (like AdaBoost), Gradient Boosting literally trains each new tree to predict the ERRORS (residuals) of the current ensemble. It's like proofreading: each pass corrects mistakes left by previous passes. XGBoost, LightGBM, and CatBoost are all variants of this idea and dominate ML competitions on tabular data.

Gradient Boosting

Algorithm:

  1. Initialize: $F_0(x) = \arg\min_c \sum_i L(y_i, c)$
  2. For $t = 1, \ldots, T$:
    • Compute pseudo-residuals: $r_{it} = -\frac{\partial L(y_i, F_{t-1}(x_i))}{\partial F_{t-1}(x_i)}$
    • Fit weak learner $h_t$ to pseudo-residuals
    • Update: $F_t(x) = F_{t-1}(x) + \eta \cdot h_t(x)$

For squared loss: pseudo-residuals = actual residuals $y_i - F_{t-1}(x_i)$

$\eta$ is the learning rate (shrinkage): smaller $\eta$ → more trees needed but better generalization.

1.5 Bagging vs Boosting Comparison

AspectBaggingBoosting
TrainingParallel (independent)Sequential (dependent)
ReducesVarianceBias (and variance)
Base learnersStrong/complex (e.g., deep trees)Weak (e.g., stumps, shallow trees)
OverfittingRarely overfitsCan overfit with too many rounds
Outlier sensitivityRobustSensitive (upweights hard points)
ParallelizableYesNo
ExampleRandom ForestAdaBoost, XGBoost, LightGBM

1.6 Stacking

What is Stacking? Train several DIFFERENT types of models (e.g., SVM, Random Forest, Logistic Regression), then train a "meta-learner" that learns the best way to combine their predictions. It's like having specialists (cardiologist, neurologist, radiologist) and then a general practitioner who knows which specialist to trust for which symptoms.

Idea: Train a meta-learner on outputs of base learners.

  1. Train diverse base models on training data
  2. Get predictions from base models (via CV to avoid overfitting)
  3. Train a meta-model using base model predictions as features

Combines strengths of different model types (e.g., SVM + RF + LR → meta-learner).

2. When to Use

  • Bagging/Random Forest: Default go-to for structured data. Almost always works well.
  • Boosting (XGBoost/LightGBM): When you need maximum accuracy and can tune. Competitions.
  • AdaBoost: When you want interpretability of boosting with simple weak learners.
  • Stacking: Kaggle competitions, when diverse models are available.

3. All Formulae

Bagging Variance

$$\text{Var}(\text{bagged}) = \rho\sigma^2 + \frac{(1-\rho)\sigma^2}{B}$$

where $\rho$ = avg pairwise correlation, $B$ = number of models

AdaBoost

$$\alpha_t = \frac{1}{2}\ln\frac{1-\epsilon_t}{\epsilon_t}$$ $$w_i^{(t+1)} = w_i^{(t)}\exp(-\alpha_t y_i h_t(x_i))$$ $$H(x) = \text{sign}\left(\sum_{t=1}^T \alpha_t h_t(x)\right)$$

Training error bound: $\text{err}_{\text{train}} \leq \exp\left(-2\sum_t\gamma_t^2\right)$ where $\gamma_t = 1/2 - \epsilon_t$

Gradient Boosting

$$F_t(x) = F_{t-1}(x) + \eta h_t(x)$$ $$r_{it} = -\left[\frac{\partial L(y_i, F(x_i))}{\partial F(x_i)}\right]_{F=F_{t-1}}$$

OOB Error

$$\text{OOB Error} = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n \mathbb{1}[\hat{y}_i^{OOB} \neq y_i]$$

where $\hat{y}_i^{OOB}$ uses only trees that did NOT train on point $i$.

4. Best Practices

  • Random Forest: Start with default hyperparameters — usually works well out of the box
  • Boosting: Use small learning rate ($\eta = 0.01$-$0.1$) with many trees + early stopping
  • Feature importance: Use permutation importance (more reliable than impurity-based)
  • Overfitting in boosting: Control via max depth, min samples, learning rate, n_estimators with early stopping
  • For imbalanced data: Bagging is more robust than boosting (boosting focuses on hard cases which may be noise)
Common Mistakes:
  • Thinking "more trees always overfits" — true for boosting, FALSE for bagging/RF
  • Using deep trees in AdaBoost (should be stumps or very shallow)
  • Not using early stopping with gradient boosting
  • Confusing which reduces bias vs variance (Bagging=variance, Boosting=bias)

5. Example Problems (Solved Exam Questions)

Problem 1: Bagging vs Boosting [End-Term Q11]

Question: (a) Explain the main conceptual difference between bagging and boosting in ensemble learning, focusing on how the base models are trained and combined. (b) Describe one advantage and one limitation of each method.

(a) Main conceptual difference:

BaggingBoosting
How base models are trainedIndependently and in parallel. Each model is trained on a different bootstrap sample (random subset with replacement) of the original data. Models have NO knowledge of each other.Sequentially and adaptively. Each model is trained to correct the errors of all previous models. Later models focus on hard-to-classify samples by upweighting misclassified points.
How models are combinedSimple averaging (regression) or majority vote (classification). All models have EQUAL weight.Weighted combination — more accurate models get HIGHER weight ($\alpha_t = \frac{1}{2}\ln\frac{1-\epsilon_t}{\epsilon_t}$). Poor learners contribute less.
Base learner typeComplex/strong learners (fully grown trees)Simple/weak learners (stumps, shallow trees)
What it reducesPrimarily variancePrimarily bias

(b) Advantage and limitation of each:

AdvantageLimitation
BaggingVery resistant to overfitting — more trees never hurts (monotonically improves or plateaus). Also easy to parallelize for speed.Cannot reduce bias — if individual base learners are biased (e.g., underfitting), averaging biased models still gives a biased result.
BoostingCan achieve very low training error by iteratively correcting mistakes. Often achieves higher accuracy than bagging on well-tuned problems.Prone to overfitting if too many iterations are used (especially on noisy data). Also sequential nature makes it slower and harder to parallelize.

Problem 2: AdaBoost Weight Update Computation

Question: In AdaBoost, we train weak learners sequentially, giving higher weight to previously misclassified points. Given: 10 training points (initially equal weights $w_i = 1/10$), the first weak learner achieves a weighted classification error of $\epsilon_1 = 0.3$ (misclassifies 30% of the weighted training set). Compute: (a) The learner weight $\alpha_1$ (how much this learner contributes to the final vote), (b) The updated weight for a misclassified point, (c) The updated weight for a correctly classified point.

Recall: AdaBoost's key formulas:

  • Learner weight: $\alpha_t = \frac{1}{2}\ln\frac{1 - \epsilon_t}{\epsilon_t}$ (larger $\alpha$ for more accurate learners)
  • Sample weight update: $w_i^{(t+1)} = w_i^{(t)} \cdot \exp(-\alpha_t \cdot y_i \cdot h_t(x_i))$
  • If correctly classified: $y_i h_t(x_i) = +1$ → weight decreases (multiply by $e^{-\alpha}$)
  • If misclassified: $y_i h_t(x_i) = -1$ → weight increases (multiply by $e^{+\alpha}$)

Step 1: Compute learner weight $\alpha_1$

With weighted error $\epsilon_1 = 0.3$ (better than random guessing at 0.5):

$$\alpha_1 = \frac{1}{2}\ln\frac{1-\epsilon_1}{\epsilon_1} = \frac{1}{2}\ln\frac{1-0.3}{0.3} = \frac{1}{2}\ln\frac{0.7}{0.3} = \frac{1}{2}\ln(2.333) = \frac{1}{2}(0.847) = \boxed{0.424}$$

Interpretation: $\alpha_1 > 0$ means this learner is better than random and will have positive weight in the final ensemble.

Step 2: Weight update for a MISCLASSIFIED point

For misclassified points, $y_i \cdot h_1(x_i) = -1$ (prediction disagrees with true label):

$$w_i^{\text{new}} = w_i^{\text{old}} \cdot \exp(+\alpha_1) = \frac{1}{10} \cdot e^{0.424} = \frac{1}{10} \cdot 1.528 = \boxed{0.1528}$$

The weight INCREASED from 0.1 to 0.1528 — this point becomes more important for the next learner.

Step 3: Weight update for a CORRECTLY classified point

For correct points, $y_i \cdot h_1(x_i) = +1$:

$$w_i^{\text{new}} = \frac{1}{10} \cdot e^{-0.424} = \frac{1}{10} \cdot 0.654 = \boxed{0.0654}$$

The weight DECREASED from 0.1 to 0.0654 — correctly classified points become less important.

After normalization: All weights are divided by their sum to ensure they add to 1. The net effect: the next weak learner "focuses" on the mistakes of the current one. This is how AdaBoost converts many weak learners into a strong classifier.

Problem 3: Random Forest Feature Importance vs Linear Model Coefficients

Question: You fit both a Random Forest and a Logistic Regression to the same dataset. A particular feature has HIGH importance in the Random Forest but a near-zero coefficient in Logistic Regression. How is this possible? What does this tell you about the feature's relationship with the target?

Recall: RF feature importance measures how much prediction accuracy drops when a feature's values are randomly shuffled. Logistic Regression coefficients measure the linear marginal effect of each feature on the log-odds.

Answer: The feature likely has a NON-LINEAR or INTERACTION effect.

Reasons this can happen:

  1. Non-linear relationship: The feature may be important only in certain value ranges (e.g., age matters a lot for health prediction but only above 50). RF captures this through splits; LR's single linear coefficient averages the effect to near zero.
  2. Interaction effects: The feature may be important only in combination with another feature (e.g., "education" is predictive of income only when combined with "field of work"). RF naturally captures interactions through nested splits; LR misses them without explicit interaction terms.
  3. Multicollinearity: If this feature is highly correlated with other features, LR distributes the coefficient across correlated features (or suppresses some). RF is unaffected because it randomly samples feature subsets — each tree uses different correlated features.

Practical takeaway: If RF says a feature is important but LR says it's not, investigate non-linear effects. Try adding polynomial terms or interactions to LR, or trust the RF importance for a more complete picture of feature relevance.

Problem 4: True/False — Ensemble Properties

Question: Determine whether each of the following statements about ensemble methods is TRUE or FALSE. Provide clear reasoning for each.

(a) "Bagging always reduces test error compared to a single model." → FALSE

Why: Bagging only reduces VARIANCE. If the base model has high BIAS (e.g., a linear model for non-linear data), bagging won't help — averaging many biased models gives another biased model. The fundamental bias remains unchanged, and you've wasted computation. Bagging only helps when base learners have low bias but high variance (like deep decision trees).

(b) "Random Forest can overfit by adding more trees." → FALSE

Why: Adding more trees to a Random Forest NEVER increases generalization error. Mathematically, each additional tree provides an independent (due to bootstrap + feature subsampling) estimate, and averaging more independent estimates always reduces or maintains variance. The OOB error monotonically improves and then plateaus. This is different from boosting! Note: individual trees CAN be overfit, but averaging them corrects for this.

(c) "AdaBoost with decision stumps can fit any dataset perfectly given enough rounds." → TRUE

Why: For linearly separable data in the "boosted feature space," AdaBoost minimizes exponential loss, which decreases exponentially with the number of rounds. With enough iterations on separable data, the training error can reach zero. Each stump adds a new dimension to the effective feature space, and with enough dimensions, any finite dataset becomes separable. (However, achieving zero training error doesn't mean good generalization — that's where early stopping helps.)

Problem 4: Decision Tree Overfitting and Random Forest [Practice Q13]

Question: A decision tree is grown to full depth on 100 training examples and achieves 100% training accuracy.

(a) Why might it perform poorly on test data? (b) Two strategies to mitigate (other than pruning). (c) How does Random Forest address overfitting?

(a) Why full-depth tree overfits:

A fully grown tree creates a leaf for (almost) every training example, effectively memorizing the data including noise and outliers. It has:

  • Zero bias (perfectly fits training data)
  • Extremely high variance (small changes in training data → completely different tree structure)

On test data, the tree responds to noise patterns that don't generalize. This is the classic overfitting phenomenon: low training error but high test error due to excessive model complexity.

(b) Two mitigation strategies (besides pruning):

  1. Set maximum depth / minimum samples per leaf: Prevent the tree from growing too deep. E.g., require each leaf to contain at least 5 samples. This reduces variance at the cost of slightly more bias.
  2. Use ensemble methods (Random Forest / Boosting): Instead of relying on a single overfit tree, aggregate many trees to reduce variance. Multiple independent overfit trees, when averaged, produce a much more stable prediction.

(c) How Random Forest addresses overfitting:

Random Forest combines TWO sources of randomization:

  • Bagging (Bootstrap Aggregating): Each tree is trained on a different bootstrap sample (random subset with replacement). This creates diverse trees with different biases, and averaging $B$ trees reduces variance by factor of $\approx 1/B$ (if trees were independent).
  • Feature randomization: At each split, only a random subset of $m \approx \sqrt{p}$ features is considered. This decorrelates the trees — without this, all trees would split on the same dominant features and be highly correlated (reducing the benefit of averaging).

Result: Individual trees may still overfit, but the ensemble doesn't because averaging uncorrelated overfit predictions cancels out noise. More trees → monotonically better (never hurts).

Problem 5: AdaBoost — Detailed Weight Computation [Practice Q14]

Question: Binary classification, labels $y_i \in \{-1, +1\}$. After round 1, weak learner $h_1(x)$ has weighted error $\epsilon_1 = 0.25$.

(a) Compute $\alpha_1$. (b) Write the weight update rule. (c) With 4 examples (initial weight $\frac{1}{4}$), examples 1&2 misclassified, 3&4 correct: compute new normalized weights.

Recall: AdaBoost assigns learner weights $\alpha_t$ (how much each learner contributes to final vote) and updates sample weights (focus on hard examples).

(a) Compute learner weight $\alpha_1$:

$$\alpha_1 = \frac{1}{2}\ln\left(\frac{1 - \epsilon_1}{\epsilon_1}\right) = \frac{1}{2}\ln\left(\frac{1 - 0.25}{0.25}\right) = \frac{1}{2}\ln(3) = \frac{1}{2}(1.0986) = \boxed{0.5493}$$

Since $\epsilon_1 = 0.25 < 0.5$, this learner is better than random guessing → positive weight in the ensemble.

(b) Weight update rule:

For each sample $i$:

$$w_i^{(t+1)} = w_i^{(t)} \cdot \exp(-\alpha_t \cdot y_i \cdot h_t(x_i))$$
  • Misclassified ($y_i \neq h_t(x_i)$, so $y_i \cdot h_t(x_i) = -1$): $w_i \to w_i \cdot e^{+\alpha_t}$ (weight increases)
  • Correctly classified ($y_i = h_t(x_i)$, so $y_i \cdot h_t(x_i) = +1$): $w_i \to w_i \cdot e^{-\alpha_t}$ (weight decreases)

(c) Compute new weights:

Initial: all $w_i = 1/4$. With $\alpha_1 = 0.5493$, $e^{\alpha_1} = \sqrt{3} \approx 1.7321$, $e^{-\alpha_1} = 1/\sqrt{3} \approx 0.5774$.

ExampleStatusUnnormalized weightNormalized
1Misclassified$\frac{1}{4} \times 1.7321 = 0.4330$$0.4330 / 1.1547 = \boxed{3/8 = 0.375}$
2Misclassified$\frac{1}{4} \times 1.7321 = 0.4330$$0.375$
3Correct$\frac{1}{4} \times 0.5774 = 0.1443$$0.1443 / 1.1547 = \boxed{1/8 = 0.125}$
4Correct$\frac{1}{4} \times 0.5774 = 0.1443$$0.125$

Sum of unnormalized: $2(0.4330) + 2(0.1443) = 1.1547$. Normalized sum: $2(0.375) + 2(0.125) = 1.0$ ✓

Interpretation: Misclassified examples now have 3× the weight of correct ones ($0.375$ vs $0.125$). The next weak learner will focus disproportionately on examples 1 and 2.
